THE RED PAGE.
A Wish.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some take no heed of any future day
But kiss Time’s hand while wearing yet his bonds,
Dreaming their young full-blooded life away
Among Life’s lotus-ponds.
And some there are who gird them shield and
sword,
War dawn and noon, fight the red sunset down
To fall when night falls, with the same reward
Death’s dark-hued cypress crown.
Ah ! when Death’s hand our own warm hand hath
ta’en
Down the dark aisles his sceptre rules supreme,
God grant the fighters leave to fight again
And let the dreamers dream !
W.H.O.
Arthur A. D. Bayldon writes.
I have just read Harrison’s article on English
Erose style in The Nineteenth Century. Some of
is remarks are excellent, some foolish. He is too
diffuse and modestly vain. He seems to think a
literary style cannot be acquired. How about
Southey ? He was such an incorrigible dunce as a
prose-writer at school that he determined to make
a special study of style ; and he worked diligently,
and acquired an aptitude for expression that won
Macaulay’s good opinion. Not that Macaulay’s
opinion about the higher touches of style is worth
much, but it has weight with the many. And I
dare say others besides Southey have gained some
distinction by similar methods. I will run over a
few stylists and knock them off. Harrison’s list
can be easily extended—he does not mention Haz-
litt, Lang, Saintsbury, Hawthorne, Irving, Boling-
broke, Hunt, Symonds, Newman—all of them
stylists. ,
* * * *
Among moderns, Lang stands pre-eminently
first as a master of Saxon. His style is not so
sensitive as Stevenson’s, another Saxon, but it is
more manly. Everyone praises Ruskin, and fools
try to imitate him. At its best, his style has a
wild flash, and is filled with the multitudinous
voices of the sea. But it is very often hysterical
and fibreless: Symonds’s style is scholarly, ornate,
and chiefly fails through its self-consciousness.
Newman’s is chaste and beautifully dear and soft-
sounding, like the far-off chime of village church
bells at sunset. ’Tis trrie it sounds thin after the
deep-chested harmony of Landor. Macaulay’s is
mechanical and latinised, and contains everything
for a literary hack. It is the kettle-drum of the
orchestra. Some great writers have a wretched
style. Scott and Dickens are unbearable. Glad-
stone’s is as bad as a lawyer’s.
* * * *
Style is either creative or critical. Despite her
success as a novelist Mrs. Humphrey Ward’s is
keenly critical. On the other hand, though,
critic Saintsbury’s is creative, The presiding in-
fluence of the creative is genius f Of the critical,
talent. Sometimes talent is superior to genius as
Arnold is to Longfellow. One touches the reason,
the other the emotions. One cuts like a razor-
blade to the heart of a subject ; the other blazes
along with pomp and power. Hazlitt argued
poets were bad prose writers. This is true in
many instances. Swinburne cannot write prose.
Wordsworth was a lumberer, Byron a “ slogger,”
Coleridge a stump-orator. On the other hand,
Keats wrote an artless prose that reminds you of
Shakespeare’s. And Shelley swept the whole
gamut of the instrument. And what sunshine
and mirth and the singing of happy syllables we
Set in Hunt! Sometimes a writer has a brain-
itch. As a poet Emerson drags you along lines
that jolt and words that jar. As a proser he
sharpened his periods to javelin point, but could
not switch on his paragraphs. What was the
hitch?
* • * *
Many books have been written on style. We are
cautioned against putting adverbs in the wrong
place. We are given rules to work by, errors to
avoid ; to much of which I reply—Mullock! An
adverb in the wrong place oft brings out a fine
shade of meaning, swells the music, and adds a
charm to the style. We are thoughtfully advised
never to use a long word if a short one will do ;
that Saxon phrase is more effective than any other.
To which I reply—Pish ! Just do nothing of the
sort. Use that word—be it of ten syllables or of
one—the most expressive of your thought, of your
impression, of your emotion. A number of short,
curt words stepping on each other’s heel is as tan-
talising as the raps of a door-knocker. Get to
know the different styles of the masters, and digest
them; and if you have anything to communicate,
write as you feel—that is the best advice. There
is not a great stylist who has not broken the laws
of the grammarian times ■without number, and
will do it again when they clog him. A good
style is the reflex of the writer’s mood. And
never be afraid of using the same word a dozen
times in the same sentence if by so doing you can
catch your reader’s attention. This is rank
heresy in the schools. It is rarely a college man
can write at all. His mind is crammed with
obsoletes. Judges and doctors and solicitors, and
all that fry, don’t know good English when they
see it. The best prosers gained nothing of their
style from college but its blemishes. And shun
such verbiage-mongers as Ingersoll, and men of
his kidney. They are ever seeking something
new, some striking phrase, some “fine” passage,
something tobring down the claps of the gallery.
Never quote if you can help it, nor parade your
knowledge of a foreign language which any fool
oa,n learn ; it’s a sure sign you are superficial and
Wish to presume on your bastard knowledge.
There is a peculiarity about style that has never
been noticed. It’s the reflex of the inner mam,.
If a man writes a fierce impetuous prose, and
brings down the whip on your haunches, he is
probably a bashful, patient, amiable sort of a crea-
ture in his daily life. Ruskin is a notable example.
If his style is grave, and recognises the classical
canons, you may be sure he is an irritable despot
at home. Landor illustrates it. And so Nature
works out her compensations.
* * * *
Perhaps the plainest, and yet the most difficult
to imitate, is Thackeray's style. There is no-spurt
or rocket-glare; it simply glides lucidly along.
Its deadly antithesis is Marie Corelli’s—the worst
style imaginable. It’s a clotted heap, subject to
spasmodic eruptions. Words are piled on words,
phrases on phrases, sentences on sentences, all sig-
nitymg nothing. Then comes an explosion of
imagery ; and then we are blown about like straws
in a whirlwind of abstractions. And yet she sells !
* » • *
There is only one Australian proser that strikes
me as a stylist—Nicholson, author of “Halek.”
Its prose recalls the prose of the school of Queen
Ann : clear and steady and full of a sweet word
music. It’s like a fine translation of an ancient
Hebrew. It’s monotonously smooth; not dintec
enough. But we cannot have everything. The
purling brooklet has an audience as well as the
organ-voiced Pacific. And as it is with the harps
and dulcimers of Nature so is it with the stylists—
each has his own particular note in the sterna!
harmony.
D. Mac Coll writes in Bond. Saturday Review
In Memoriam Edward Burne-Jones ”
We must do reverence and say farewell to
another of the great spirits of our time. The
painter, who for his generation ventured genius on
the great art, the imaging and making Ins own of
the mythologies of the race, has been snatched
away; and the exquisite brain and hands, busy
till yesterday, are a little heap of dust.
* » * *
If there are times when the Kingdom of Heaven
suffers violence and must be taken by storm, there
are constantly such moments for the arts ; for they
must be revivified by frequent invasions and
assaults from without. The waters of imagination
recede and leave one of them stranded, and must
be let in again from some neglected and unlikely
source. Painting with us, fifty years ago, was in
this perilous state, become a kind of painting-
mongering, a production of insignificant illustra-
tions and “family landscapes.” The spirit was
moving elsewhere, and the painters had not so
much as heard of it. The poetry of letters, with a
new passion of vision in Keats and Tennyson, had
welled up abundantly, but painting lay dry. At
such a moment innocent violence was called for to
break the degraded moulds to bring in the re-
covered spirit with an amateur’s daring: to take
up the instrument fingered and thrummed by
sleepy tradesmen, ’to startle it with a new song
telling of desire and the glory of the earth.
♦ * ♦
Rossetti was the poet who worked this miracle,
and his pupil was born into a world well prepared
for him. His shy spirit might, unhelped, never
have essayed to bridge the gulf, but the confident
prince and tyrant of the arts was there before
him ; the air was all eager and attentive under the
magic voice of Ruskin, and his generous hand was
ready to help. There was, therefore, no struggle
or time wasted in beating about, the painter had
to begin later in life than was convenient the
training for his craft, but the past of his mind was
clear; he began in a shielded, secret world, and
remained to the end of his days untroubled by
what might be doing outside. Thus, hardly
knowing perhaps in his ardour how much he
escaped and ignored, he attacked, as the most
natural thing in the world, the painting of mytho-
logy. Here his instinct surely led him to what
must ever remain the supreme province of paint-
ing. The example of letters sophisticates not a
little in this matter the view of many modem pain-
ters. The emotional advantages to a prose writer
of the modem setting or the historical setting are
countless ; for a painter of dramatic subjects the
disadvantages are enormous; the reason being the
poverty of his art in the means of narrative
exposition. If he invents a modern story, he must
spend all his ingenuity in explaining the incident.
How much better to take those situations known
to all, whose before and after are present to the
mind, enriched already by a hundred commen-
taries, and upon that text to write his own inter-
pretation. Delivery from the Dragon, the
Mother’s worship and the wondering hope of wise
old men about the birth of a new child, the Beggar
Maid enthroned, the Days standing on the glassy
firmament, with the new-created clans of birds and
fishes, of clouds and human creatures in their
hands—these, not novelettes, are the subjects for
the painter who essays the more complex ranges of
his art, sweeping as they do into one focus a hun-
dred stories.
» » * *
But if an inspiration of dramatic and lyric
poetry was thus carried back into painting, the
criticism of another art was also brought to bear—
that of architecture, the idea of a more comprehen-
sive design, of painting that should make its goal
not the bibelot, but the decoration of wall spaces.
Beside Burne-Jones was William Morris, in whom
a fine bullying enei’gy was so oddly mated with a
feminine taste in art, but who had the dream of an
architecture enriched, as in Gothic times, by
legend and poetry. The architect was missing in
the group, but in the painter’s work the architect’s,
designer’s impulse was vividly present in structure
and repetition of parts, in exercises of the art of
numbers, such as the “Golden Stair,” like a fern
with its fronds turning upon itself, in his passion
for the intrigue of line, for everything that coils
and tangles, in water and smoke and flame.
• * i * •
In how wistful a temper did those two powers of
his art, the poet’s and the musical, apply them-
selves to reimagining the legends ! He came, it is
said, of those shy peoples who linger on the hills
and margins of our country ; if theirs by blood, he
was doubly theirs by spirit, and the contagion of
poetry ; only not theirs in the sustained labor and
power he dedicated to an imagery that tells of
the passion for beauty and of discouragement
before life. To dream of the triumph of battle,
but to set forth defeated when the trumpet sounds;
to be filled with the sweetness of love, but to
lament over its forfeit before its age has come;
to remain spellbound outside the lists; to go to
sleep as others to business and adventure ; to have
chambers ready for sorrows as. for the most
honoured guests, meeting them on their way lest
they should mistake the road ; to flatter Death—
these were the impulses of his birth as a poet, and
the harder he strove to cast his art in the heroic
mould the more certainly they betrayed them-
selves. He was like a merman, who should strive
to nurture the changeling children of his thought
upon the earth, sending them to court, to camp,
to service; but wherever they dwell the springs
come up brackish, seaweed grows in their gardens,
and everything they say and hear is confused with
a sound of waves.
* • » •
But to this temper how beautiful the world
may be! If the fields are greener to the eyes
of the condemned, to those pilgrims and haunters
of earth, never at home in it, “transported
for life ” from some other star, self-sentenced
to defeat and death, flowers, the visit of spring,
gentle faces are like a reprieve. Oblivions the
length of a bird’s song, pities the breadth of
a tree’s shadow, stations among the desperate
hills where' mirrors are framed in forget-me-not,
bowers defended by impenetrable tangles of the
rose, shutting in histories full of sleep—these are
the hiding-places, and the Evening Star the de-
liverer, passing over “hilltop, treetop, headland,”
with release for the cities sprinkled beneath her
feet, and the cool drifting of her robes.
* * * *
, How all this imagery will appear to new genera-
tions it is not we who can say, though two periods
of unjust depreciation may be thought to have
paid its debt to mortality. For each band of
youth there is some wizard who opens the gates of
the dream-world, and ybuth itself, its desires, the
spirit’s fashion of the moment conspire to make the
vision glorious. After, when the mood changes and
another spell is cast, these conspiring forces fall
away, the art is judged, and the fashion is judged.
To many who were boys twenty years ago,
he whose wand has just b een broken was the
magician; he showed us what our eyes were long-
ing for. We may have become more critical since,
we may have worshipped, with reason, many an
art unknown to us then or not understood, but
that experience is not repeated twice. Who is the
opener of the gate for the boys in this year of
grace? Only themselves can tell. I wish them
such a moment as was ours when we saw “The
Days of Creation,” “The Mirror of Venus,” the
“Beguiling of Merlin,” all that secret world of
imagination suddenly disclosed; and I cannot
think that the day will ever come when “Medea,”
“ Hypsiphile, ” the lady in the “Chess Players,”
the Sleepers in the ‘ ‘ Garden Court, ” ‘ ‘ Psyche, ” or
she who,
“like a dreary spright,
Cald by strong charmes out of eternall night,
Had Deathes own ymage figurd in her face, ”
will fail to have their votaries. The fountains of
Padua and Florence will never run dry, and the
garden they watered in the north will be visited.
• ——
“Gilaomi”: Re Mr. Kevin’s sohool libraries.
If the one in my locality is a fair sample you have been
grossly misinformed. It is stupidly goody-goody—“ Lon-
don Sunday School Society” and “The Religious Tract
Society ” being the principal imprints on first leaves.
There are 23 books from these publishers ; 8 of travel and
adventure, 16 of fiction of a higher kind, 2 of history,
and 10 others. Several of the religious works are aggres-
sively Protestant, as this extract shows : “ From Luther’s
life we learn to be strong in faith ... to resist the
errors, superstition and idolatry of Rome,” &c. There
are many other passages of same import, and it was
pointed out by the teacher that these books were hardly
the thing for allegedly unsectarian schools (fancy R.O.
children compelled to attend schools where books of this
tone are kept for general use !) The departmental reply
was that the libraries were “ not under the supervision of
the Dept.” Andpeople wonder why the R.C’s build schools
of their own ! Also, the question of ownership of these
libraries seems unsettled. Whose are they ? One teacher,
on removal, took the best of the library with him. The
incoming teacher reported the books as missing to the
Dept., and got the reply sent regarding the sectarian
nature of the books.
She-O-ik: Bar steeplechase, I fancy, is a good-
old-days game also nowadays unheard of. It is de-
fined at p. 3 of ‘Something to his Advantage,’ edited
by Richmond Thatcher (Sydney, 1875)—“ The majo-
rity are bound for Nanangie, as soon as their cheques
are spent ; in short, they are being ' lambed down,’
and could not be in better hands, for as a financial
shearer ‘lrish Bill ’ had not his equal in the Gulf country.
A lull in the proceedings causes that worthy to ask if
they are getting tired, an enquiry which is accented, as
it was intended, as a sort of reflection upon their steadfast
adherence to a game they have improvised, and meets
with an indignant denial and an order to ‘stick ’em up
again.’ The game in which they are engaged is marvel-
lous in its simplicity, and the facility with which any one
with money can join in it. In consists merely in sticking
half-a-dozen bottles of rum at intervals along the floor,
and the company jumping in quick succession over them
till one is knocked down, when the delinquent is called
upon to pay for the lot, and generally kicks the others
till their contents bedew the floor of the shanty. A fool
ish game, you will -ay ; but what, will you have? What
game of life is sensible? Jn what element, of satisfaction
does ‘bar steeplechase ’ (the name given to the pastime
in question) fall short of other amusements at which the
world plays? . . . Yes ; in the famous ‘ Pursuit of Plea-
sure,’ Noel Patou did wrong to leave out bar steeplechase
and the bottles of rum.”
A Sigh.
It is nothing but a rose I gave her, —
Nothing but a rose
Any wind might rob of half its savour,
Any wind that blows.
When she took it from my trembling fingers
With a hand as chill, —
Ah, the flying touch upon them lingers,
Stays, and thrills them still!
Withered, faded, pressed between the pages,
Crumpled fold on fold,—
Once it lay upon her breast, and ages
Cannot make it old !
H. P. SPOFFORD.

In the Laboratory.
Failing Manhood.
When your manhood begins
to go you will twitch and show
signs of premature weakness.
Following are some of the symp-
toms :
Do your facil nerves twitch ?
Are you all run down ?
Spots before the eyes?
Do the muscles twitch ?
Have you pains in small of back?
Have you pains in head?
Have you shooting pains?
Do you feel fatigued?
Are you weary ?
Does your head swim ?
Do your knees knock ?
Does your hand tremble?
Nervous Twitchings.
A nervous man easily gets all
of a tremble, gets fits of weak-
ness, fits of twitchings. Here are
some of his symptoms :
Do your eyes twitch?
Do your hands tremble?
Do you feel shaky?
Can you walk without halting?
Are you melancholy?
Do you have ringing in the ears?
Is your blood poor ?
Are your eyes weak?
Are your eyes bleary?
Does your head swim?
N er vous_Debility.
This affliction destroys ambi-
tion, organic strength, energy,
and hope. Following are some
of the symptoms
Do you feel weak?
Have you cold feet?
Have you backache ?
Do you shun society ?
Are'you losing flesh?
Do you sleep poorly?
Are you low-spirited?
Are your eyes sunken ?
Do you have hot flushes?
Is your memory impaired ?
Have you no manly vigor ?
Have you no vital energy?
Do you have sick headaches?
Is there nausea after eating ?
Do your hands or feet sweat?
Has the brightness left your eyes
Do you feel unfit to marry?

A Sextet of Sonnets.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Akaroa Heads.
Oh, what a solitude is all around !
The hermit sea, the splintered cliff that falls
In altars on eternal pedestals,
That make the wilderness a holy ground ;
Yet surely do I hear an ancient sound,
Barbaric worship in these massy walls,
Souls bared to heaven where now the sea-mew
calls,
Wild rapture where is now a death profound.
Oh, may my spirit never fail to soar
Far from the foamy fabric of the brine,
And all the shallow coil that cumbers life;
Lest I be like this desolated shore,
For ever fretted, and for ever strife,
A soul whose altars are no more divine!
M.L.
HUBERT CHUROH.
To My Love.
“ Paint me,” you said, “ a poem ; give to me
A breathing thought that I may keep to kiss ! ”
While that low laugh that aye a mandate is
Nestled upon your lips. Call memory
To that fair moment when you heard my plea,
And in the tumult of my arms’ warm bliss,
You, like a broken flower crushed amiss,
Thrilled into frenzied life exultantly,
And all your body pulsed with love’s desire J
Can I in words that perfect hour rehearse,
Or write the vehemence of veins on fire ?
My lips would only kiss —and you require
From all my heart’s rich hoard one pallid verse—
The heap of ashes left on passion’s pyre !
N.S.W.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
Greeting.
Round what foul brood did thy dank folds en-
twine —
In what mirk feculent burrow underground,
Thou loathly reptile whose black coils up-wound
Of lurking treacherous cunning were the sign
For all who would avoid thee ? Wherefore pine
For human serfhood ? Surely thou wert crowned
'Mid vipers, ere thy hissing spirit found
And squirmed into that woman’s shell of thine.
Squat is thy body now, though hedged with steel —
Not lithe and graceful ; and thy shameless eyes’
Defiant looks all trustful ess destroy ;
No love but love of self thy soul may feel;
Spite drags the lips that droop with weight of
lies ;
Thy laughter is flat blasphemy of joy.
N.S.W.
J. LE GAY BRERETON.
Of My Lady.
Not here, dear heart, I praise your form or face,
Your smiles’ allurement or your eyes’ soft light;
Not here I sing with lover-like delight
Of your mind’s beauty or your body’s grace.
These precious things I love, yet in them trace
Only slight indications of your bright
Wise woman-soul, the treasure infinite
Which lights me through this ashen earth a space.
And this I know ; if my unworthy feet,
All bruised and mired by earth-ways weakly
trod,
Pass to the Presence; and if eternal Love
Shall bid me choose the gift to me most sweet;
Thus will I pray: “The greatest still, Lord
God-
Give me my wife, and I have Heaven enough! ”
N.S.W.
FRANK MORTON.
Venus before her Mirror.
Let me but look upon that form ; I ask
No more —no more but just to stand at gaze
Before that mirrored loveliness always;
But can I love it, knowing ’tis a mask
That hides the inner foulness ? Yet men bask
In that great tender smile, and ever raise
Arms that implore, and gloating eyes that praise;
To dare or die for me their lightest task !
What foolishness is this ? Do I not scorn
Those paltry virtues of a race decayed ?
Am I not still the Queen ? Shall this meek maid
Mary dethrone me ? Long ere she was born
I ruled the world, aye, and shall rule in lust
When these poor creeds have crumbled into dust I
Q.
E.A.
Disillusioned.
A group of sad-eyed zealots, we began—
Exalted faith our dominating note—
With soaring aspiration to promote
The Universal Brotherhood of Man.
But 10, ere far had sailed our Argos boat,
Laden with golden principles, the fan
Of dastard discord blew its blighting ban,
And each man tried to clutch his fellow’s throat!
Yea, grand the large, mouth-filling phrases sound,
But cankering cant lies often at their core.
Better no principles than such as burst
Like bubbles when they touch the solid ground.
Give me the true friend’s hand-shake more and
more,
For Universal I have ceased to thirst.
D. BOUVERIE.

JACK WANT TAKES HIS PLEASURES SADLY.
“Mr. Want at Adelaide.-Contrary to his original intention, Mr. J. H. Want Q.C., Will not proceed to Albany as a passenger by the P. and O. liner Himalaya, which arrived
at Largs Bay early on Thursday morning. 'I will stop in Adelaide as long as I can, 'he remarked to a reporter of the Adelaide ADVERTISER on board the vessel. 'I don't know how
soon I may have to get back,' he stated, as he scanned that morning's newspaper and read the telegram concerning the political situation in New South Walles."-DAILY PAPER.
King Cabbage-Tree will soon have to do his fishing at Circular Quay, as above.

‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-

communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 8, 1898.
Concerning Clause 122.
Big John Forrest having refused for the
some dozenth time to consider the demand
of Coolgardie and Kalgoorlie for fair repre-
sentation in the Sandgropers’ Parliament,
rumors of another separation movement come
from the land of camels and red dust. Cool-
gardie has one member for 2080 electors,
and East Coolgardie one for 1486 ; while the
electorates of Ashburton, Grey, the two
Kimberleys, Gascoyne, the two Murchisons,
Irwin, Greenough, Roebourne and Yalgoo
have 11 members among them for only 1338
electors all told ! The goldfields complain
bitterly about this huge anomaly, and several
times they have had bad dreams about
making an effort to separate and form a pro-
vince of their own. For various reasons,
however, very little energy was put into the
movement. The new province would be
practically all desert and dust. It wouldn’t
have within its bounds a sufficient
known fresh - water supply to keep one
big city going. It could hardly even
grow its own irrigated flower for its button-
hole, let alone its own wheat and potatoes.
And if its goldfields gave out, as they may
some day, no reasonable person would stay
in such a place, and the male population
would be liable to get down so low that the
whole of it wouldn’t make a Ministry and an
Opposition. Besides, if John Bull under-
stood the situation (which he probably
wouldn’t), it would be impossible to get up
the inevitable debt. A province which could
abscond bodily isn’t a sufficiently permanent
creditor.
* » * *
A new idea is therefore mooted on the
goldfields. It is to persuade the British
Government to shift the eastern boundary of
Westralia eight degrees westward, and thus
lift Coolgardie, Kalgoorlie, Norseman, Dun-
das, and Esperance Bay, also the Hampton
Plains land-grab, and most of the salt lakes.
and the great Victoria Desert, and Gibson’s
Desert, and much other sand, right out of
Westralia and into South Australia. If this
could be done, the goldfields would remove
out of the most Tory-governed and into the
most Democratic province of Australia. They
would get their handy Esperance Bay rail-
way, instead of having to pay some hundreds
of miles of extra railway carriage in order to
keep the boom going at Perth, where the
Forrest gang has much property. They
would also, probably, get a railway to
Adelaide just as soon as S.A. could borrow
the money. They would get fair Parlia-
mentary representation, and would find
themselves in a land where provisions are
cheaper and the tariff not absolutely insane.
Likewise, they would have a picturesque
vengeance on big John Forrest, whose 300
miles of iron pipes, bought for the Cool-
gardie water scheme, would be left on his
hands unless some friendly arrangement
could, be arrived at. Under a special clause
in the Westralian constitution the British
Government reserves to itself absolute power
to readjust the boundaries of W.A. The
Colonial Boundaries Act, of later date, seems
to reverse this provision, but whether the
Act overrides the Constitution or not is none
too clear.
* * * *
Coming just now, this agitation is a valu-
able essay on that especially insane clause,
No. 122 of the Convention Bill, and on the
amendment by which Premier George Reid,
of New South Wales, proposes to make it
still more insane than it already is. Said
clause No. 122 begins as follows :
The Parliament of the Commonwealth may, with the
consent of the Parliament of a State, increase, diminish,
or otherwise alter the limits of the State.
This clause is to cancel and override the
Colonial Boundaries Act, and its purport is
to make it a boiler-metal proviso that no
district can obtain separation from the State
to which it belongs, no matter for how good
a cause, except with the consent of the Par-
liament of that State. The escape-valve in
the boiler is clause 127, which provides
that the Constitution can be amended by the
assent of both Federal Houses of Parliament,
of a majority of the people, and of a majority
of the States. Therefore if one State so shame-
fully misused the power given to it as
to bring the Commonwealth to the verge of
civil war—or perhaps over the verge —the
others could combine to abrogate the clause,
and thus give the Federal Parliament power to
interfere. But Premier Reid proposes to
pile a ton of ballast on the escape-valve by
an amendment providing that clause 122
shall be absolutely incapable of amendment
(apparently it isn’t to be amended even if all
the provinces consent), and that the proviso
making the consent of a State Parliament
necessary before Separation can be granted
is to be absolutely impregnable.
* * * *
The present clause would be sufficiently
cranky, and Reid’s proposed amend-
ment would be even more foqlish, if they
provided that the assent of a majority of the
people would be required before Separation
could be conceded, for the only case where
Separation can be really required is where a
minority in outlying districts is oppressed or
neglected in the interests of a majority in
and around the capital. But when the
matter is to be decided by a majority of
the Parliament things will be incalculably
worse. Assuming Premier Reid’s amend-
ment to be carried, the situation in Westralia
—the province which represents one-third
of the whole continent—would be exactly
this : The goldfields districts, however
much they are misgoverned by an agricul-
tural government of old Sandgropers at
Perth, can’t get separation, except with the
consent of both Houses of the Sandgropers’
Parliament. Even if they come, as they
easily may, to constitute two - thirds of
the population of Westralia, they won’t have
a majority in that Parliament or anything
like one ; because the Parliament gives one
representative to 2030 mining electors at
Coolgardie and eleven to 1338 electors in the
thinly-peopled agricultural districts. It is
not the vote of the people, but that of the
Parliament which is to decide the matter,
and the old Sandgroper, by artful legisla-
tion, has secured the Parliament.
* » * *
The only legal remedy the majority could
have would be to get the representation re-
adjusted so as to give them the Parliamen-
tary power to which they are fairly entitled.
And they can’t get that without the consent
of the hayseed minority in the Legislature.
And the small minority which returns eleven
members to Parliament can simply tell the
great majority which returns one member
that it will see it dead first." It can supply
that answer by 11 votes to 1, and if it knew
that the majority wanted fair representation
to enable it to pass a Separation Act, it
almost certainly would do so. The Federal
Parliament will have carefully divested
itself of all right to interfere in the cause of
justice ; and if the majority tries the only
remedy left—insurrection—the Federal
troops will be almost bound to interfere on
behalf of law and order—the law made by
the minority which deprives the majority of
its vote.
* * * *
A somewhat similar situation is preparing
in Queensland. There a Tory minority
is on top, and it keeps on top by ap-
pointing Tory magistrates to revise the
rolls, and strike off the name of every
democratic voter who can be identified
as such. In view of these things The Bul-
letin recommends that Clause 122, instead
of being made more stringent, as Premier
Reid demands, and handed down to posterity
as a fetter which can only be removed by
revolution, be abolished altogether. Or, if
it isn’t abolished, that, at the very least, it
be made to read that Separation shall be
granted wherever a majority of the adult
population of any province (not merely of
the State Legislature or of the voters
on a faked electoral roll) desires it, and
that the vote shall be taken by Federal
officers, as a guarantee that the hostile
party in office won’t cook the returns.
But it would be vastly wiser to leave the
Federal Parliament with absolute power to
make any readjustments that may be neces-
sary. So long as all the country is Australia
and all its people are Australians, the loca-
tion of any provincial boundary is of as
little consequence as the location of a
cockie’s slab fence—always provided the
people immediately adjacent to it on each
side are satisfied ; they being the people to
whom boundaries are most important.
Nothing else matters. The provincial theory
is that it is necessary, from patriotic mo-
tives, and despite quarrels, and sacrifices
and heart-burnings, and waste of money,
and many other things besides, to keep a
blue streak on one particular place on the
map, when there is the same nation, and the
same Australia, and the same language and
hopes and aspirations, and the same Federal
Government, on each side of the streak, and
the streak is nothing but a streak at the best.
The reason of this is that “ we ” have to up-
hold the honor and interests of “ our ’’ pro-
vince, whichever it may be. The Bulletin
hereby moves, as an amendment, that all the
separate provinces, in their separate pro-
vincial capacity, and all their separate
interests and honor, should be damned
together, as far as it can be done, and that
the honor and interests of Australia be the
one subject for consideration.
Disarmament.
It was highly interesting, though not at all
astonishing, to hear, as the world did a few
weeks ago, that the Autocrat of Russia desired
to make arrangements for a general disarma-
ment. He proposed, in effect, that Europe
should (wholly or in great part) throw up the
sponge ; should disband its big armies, and
shut up its huge cannon factories, and let its
great fortresses moulder awhile, and stop
spending many hundred millions a year on
preparations for heaving things at its own com-
ponent parts ; and, all matters considered, it
was not surprising that such a proposal should
come from such a quarter. It was also only to
be expected that peace and snuffle societies of
all sorts should drink much weak tea and
assimilate much crumpet over thesuggestion,and
talk of it as a great step towards the abolition
of war, and wave a dubious cotton handker-
chief in celebration. The weak-tea-and-crum-
pet society, when it fixes its spectacled,
watery eye on a new idea, generally makes
mistakes like that. And now the news arrives
(if the cable isn’t badly mistaken) that the
Czar has changed his mind and doesn’t mean it
at all. Considering the direction in which his
interests lie, that is certainly surprising. If
there is any potentate in Europe who should
desire a universal disarmament the Czar is dis-
tinctly that potentate. And if he goes back on
his proposal just when there is a chance of
Europe taking a favorable view of it, the
natural assumption is that he is throwing away
the greatest opportunity he ever had.
* * * *
Of course, disarmament w'ould not mean the
abandonment of war. That is where the peace
societies of all sorts make their great mistake.
If Europe did disarm, the probability is that
wars would be far more numerous and longer
and more sanguinary than they are now. Since
the bloated armaments reached their highest
state of development the great European States
haven’t fought at all. There are a good many
reasons why they shouldn’t. It takes all their
loose change to keep the military machine
up in peace time, and there is little or
nothing left for the extra cost of war. The
scientific character of modern weapons makes
a campaign almost too expensive to be thought
of ; when a big gun costs ,£l2O a shot, and a
very moderate campaign runs to £1,000,000 a
day in ordinary expenses, the situation grows
serious. No State really w'ants to have the
fortress that cost £5,000,000 or so laid in ruins,
or to go and bombard the other fellow’s
£5,000,000 fortress at £l2O a shot. It was a
comparatively cheap thing a century ago to
send out wooden ships at £20,000 or so apiece
to pound each other with cheap iron guns, but
now' two ironclads can charge each other with
the ram and send between them about
£1,400,000 to the bottom inside six minutes.
The £700,000 battle-ship is a beautiful thing
to look at, but fora comparatively poor country
it is a shuddersome idea to risk so much money
in one mad scrimmage. And now it takes
years to train a soldier to a perfect knowledge
of scientific warfare. The raw recruits that
could be hustled in at an emergency to fill the
gaps in the ranks would be little use, for the
war would be finished before they began to be
effective. Therefore every country has to con-
sider this situation when it goes to war:
It has a great fighLing machine which it has
taken years to perfect. It has to stake the
issue of the campaign on that machine as it is ;
there will probably be no time for repairs. The
first great land battle will probably so wreck
one machine or another that the result will be
virtually decided, and, after that, the losing
party has nothing to do but make a death-
struggle in order to secure the best terms ob-
tainable, and then to cede a few provinces and
pay a bill which will keep its nose to the grind-
stone for the next five centuries. It takes an
appalling amount of cash and nerve, or a very
bad form of luuacy, to face war nowadays.
But, supposing the European nations dis-
armed, and did it so thoroughly that each was
left with only a strong police force; that the fort-
resses were allowed to crumble, and the muni-
tions to run out, and the old military discipline
was forgotten, and there weren’t enough rifles
in all Europe to arm 300,000 men. To begin
with, the nations would grow rich for a while
by saving many hundreds of millions per annum.
They would grow richer by the industry of
the 3J millions of men now standing about in
uniform. They would grow' so rich presently,
and war would become so cheap, that any nation
could afford a rousing campaign almost any
time. If they disarmed absolutely and com-
pletely, then, instead of France sending
1,000,000 trained men to argue over Alsace-
Lorraine with the same number of trained Ger-
mans, both crowds armed with immensely ex-
pensive weapons—it could send the same
number of untrained men to argue with clubs
against the corresponding untrained German.
And, as clubs are cheap, and it would have a lot
more money to buy the clubs with through the
saving of training expenses, it could afford to
argue out the Alsace-Lorraine question a great
deal oftener than it does now. Also, it could
keep on arguing much longer—as long, in fact,
as there were any men left; for a new soldier
of the disarmament pattern could be made in a
day, provided the supply of timber for clubs
didn’t give out. Among purely European states,
the one which would gain most would undoubt-
edly be Russia. It has more than twice as
many men—and good men at that—as any of
its neighbors, and the only reason why it can’t
squash all Central Europe is lack of corn and
lack of science. If war became cheap and its
scientific aspect got back to first principles,
then it would be mainly a question of numbers
and grit, and the Russian would be on top.
Therefore, the Czar—if he is the kind of Czar
who knows when to come in out of the wet—
should be all for a general disbandment of the
bloated arrays and a return to first principles.
* * * *
Probably, the one real obstacle to the accept-
ance of the Czar’s alleged idea would be Great
Britain. The British empire is bounded every-
where by droves of heathen with whom it
would be impossible to argue intelligibly on the
subject. If they did disarm, many of them
would remain about as dangerous as they are
now, for their weapons are of a kind which can
be replaced at short notice. And, besides, they
are mostly disarmed already, so far as the
European idea would go, and even in that state
they make trouble enough. Britain keeps them
under by science and discipline, and to
disarm would mean to abandon science and
discipline in a great degree. Then the heathen
would promptly make himself a new spear, and
cut a fresh arrow, and wade in after the old
fashion, but under circumstances where his
weight of numbers would get a better show
than heretofore. Naturally the. result would-
be trouble for the nation which has so many
frontiers to defend. And not only would war
become so cheap as to be within the reach of
even the poorest State, and become frequent
through its cheapness, but it would also become
more sanguinary than it is now. The man
with a gun at a half-mile range often hits
nobody ; the man with a home-made pike at
close quarters must be a shockiugly bad shot if
he doesn’t prod somebody. Also, he is close
and handy to give his enemy an extra prod
when he is down, and thus make sure of him.
In the old days, when the primitive warrior
plugged his foe by. hand, battles were vastly
more destructive to life than they are now, and
it was quite common for the defeated party to
leave half its force on the field. It is not
so much actual loss of men which gener-
ally settles the matter nowadays, as the
disorganisation of the fighting machine, and
the capture of the expensive apparatus, and
the giving out of the ammunition, and the slid-
ing of the whole order of things into chaos. Para-
guay, in its death-struggle with Brazil and
Argentina in 1864-70, fought its war out until
it collapsed through absolute -want of men—it
left practically all the able-bodied manhood of
the country on the battle-field. That was a
war of mob armies and raw levies—to all
intents and purposes it and the U.S. Civil War
were the last campaigns fought by white men
under anything like the proposed disarmament
conditions.
* * * *
Of course, there are overwhelming reasons in
favor of disarmament. It would save an incred-
ible amount of money, and enrich Europe by the
industry of 3J million able-bodied men who are
now wasted. Also, it would abolish, or
nearly so, an implement which does even
more to suppress the democrat at home than the
enemy abroad. But there is no reason why
a misguided Y.M.C.A. should wave the crum-
pet of deluded congratulation over the matter.
It wouldn’t abolish war. It would make it
cheaper, and, in consequence, probably more
frequent. Also, it would make it last longer.
If the two halves of the American dis-United
States had started their campaign in ’6l with a
perfectly-disciplined army of 500,000 men apiece,
one of the two elaborate fighting-machines
would have wrecked the other in a huge battle
or two, and all would have been over ; the
loser might have hustled 2,000,000 of raw levies
to the front to fill the gaps, but the other fel-
low’s fighting-machine would have blown them
away almost before they got in line. The Para-
guayan w'ar lasted seven years and nearly des-
troyed a nation, simply because each stubborn
new brown man who came to the call of the in-
sane Dictator Lopez was just as good as the
old dead brown man whose place he filled, and
the other party was in the same situation.
There was no fine military organisation on
either side that could be wrecked at one great
blow—only a cheap gun and a long knife
and unlimited grit. But disarmament would
at least give the world a lot more fun
for its money, and enable it to afford a war
much oftener. And it would shift the
balance of power a good deal by reducing the
weight of the comparatively small, scientifi-
cally-educated nations and increasing that of
the primitive races that have little science,
which would be a highly-interesting experiment
to watch from a safe distance. And it would
localise wars, for a mob army is difficult to
move long distances. And it would save a vast
amount of cash as well.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
Potato Young and Pumpkin Smith.
In the matter of the Hastings and Macleay
(N.S.W.) election, Minister Young has got up
and denied all the matters that he wasn’t
accused of, and shed much whine over the base
injustice of the charges that weren’t made. Also,
he said that the things that weren’t said about
him were utterly false. About the things that
were said he was quite silent, and, as to the
charges that were made, he didn’t even speak
about them ; he carefully shirked the lot. What
Minister Young wailed over and over again in
different keys was that he never, never, never
promised any works to the Hastings and Macleay
electorate as an inducement to vote for dull,
Pumpkin Smith, and if anybody could point to
the works that Young promised, Young was
prepared to eat his hat in expiation. Nobody
ever accused Young of promising to grant the
electorate any works- as the direct price of
Smith’s election. What he assured the
people of most persistently was that Smith—
his Pumpkin Smith—would get them works if
elected, and thatBARTON wouldn’t. And Premier
Reid, in a letter openly published in Sydney
Telegraph, also assured the Hastings electors
that they ought to vote for Smith as a good
man to get them works or to “ attend to local
requirements.” Now Young and Reid together
are the men who give public works—they give
them to the people out of the people’s own
money. If the people want works they apply
to Reid and Young either directly or through
their member. There is never the slightest
doubt about the works being asked for, but they
are only supposed to be granted if they are
fairly necessary and look like a proper expendi-
ture of public money. If they look like a
proper expenditure Reid and Young are sup-
posed to grant them just the same, whether
Barton or Pumpkin Smith is the member, and
no matter in what form they are applied for.
So it all comes to this : Smith couldn’t get the
people of the Hastings and Macleay any more
works than Barton unless Reid and Young
were so utterly base and corrupt as to make
the works the price of Smith’s election.
And when Reid and Young—but mainly
Young persistently recommended Smith
as a man who would get the district
more public works than Barton, it either
meant that Young, as the man who dispenses
such things, would give Smith more public
works than he would give Barton, or else it is
a fair thing to ask Young what it did mean.
Young’s statement that he didn’t promise to
get the district any public works is as irrele-
vant as if he said that he wasn’t Columbus ; all
he said (and he said it and also hinted it and
implied it in all sorts of ways) was, not that
Young’s department would give the district
concessions if it elected Smith, but that Smith
(who supported the Ministry) was the sort of
man who would get concessions (from Young’s
department), while Barton (who didn’t sup-
port the Ministry) wasn’t. And if Young
knew in advance that Smith (who supported
the Government) was the sort of man who
would get public cash from Young’s depart-
ment, Then that also implied that Youisg knew
that his department was the sort of depart-
ment that would grant public cash to Smith.
But Young didn’t promise any works himself,
and his assurance about that is quite needless.
So is Neild’s proposal to appoint a committee
(on which Young’s party was to have a
strong majority) to consider whether Young
did the thing he wasn’t accused of, or not. And
the final decision that the Young-Reid Govern-
ment should appoint a Royal Commission to
consider whether Young was or was not guilty
of the charge that wasn’t made is equally beside
the question. There is no matter of fact or
law to be inquired into. Unless Young pro-
poses to deny the accuracy of all the reports
which represented him as perpetually recom-
mending Smith for his ability in getting “ local
requirements ” attended to, it is merely a
question whether Young’s employer, Parlia-
ment, feels satisfied with its any
longer.

Victoria, the Marriage Paradise !
The consequences of Victorian Full Court’s
decision in the Simons divorce are far-reach-
ing. It will probably cover the following
cases : the case of the wife who refuses her
husband his “ marital rights ” on account of her
disinclination to bear children at all ; to bear
children to him because she dislikes him,
whether she loves another or not ; to bear
children because he or she suffers from a here-
ditarily-transmissible disease ; to bear children
because frequent childbearing affects her health
or tends to spoil her appearance ; to bear chil-
dren frequently because it conflicts with her
reading of the moral aspects of the Malthusian
law of population ; or to bear children fre-
quently because she thinks he is not able to sup-
port them; and the case of the husband who, for
similar reasons, deems it right not to procreate
children. It is difficult to see how the court
could have arrived at any other conclusion
while the marriage status is based as it is at
present in English law. The singular thing is
that the point has never been expressly de-
cided before in England, seeing that, though
desertion is not a ground for divorce there, it
has always been a ground for judicial separa-
tion. The difficulties will arise not so much
from the decision itself as in the administra-
tion of the law based on it in the divorce-courts.
To prevent abuse of it, more stringent rules
as to collusion will probably have to be
framed, unless, as might very properly per-
haps be done, the collusion doctrine is
repealed altogether. The collusion doctrine
is simply a standing invitation to wholesale
perjury of a not very reprehensible type.
In a land of unlimited divorce it’s just as well
that the thing should be made as comfortable
as possible to both parties. Hitherto one had
to submit to habitual cruelty, or to aggravated
assaults, or to habitual neglect of domestic
duties, or to send one’s husband or wife to gaol
for two or three years, or to submit to be
shared in harem-fashion, or to be the com-
panion of a habitual drunkard for the regula-
tion period, or to submit to the necessity of
cooking one s own meals, or of eating the hash-
house’s concoctions for upwards of three
years, in order to part company legally
with one’s partner. Justice demanded an
easier method, and Vic. Full Court has
parodied Gilbert and Sullivan to find
it. Desertion for three years and upwards
can now be managed without the incon-
venience of literal desertion at all. All you
have to do is to lock your bedroom-door while
the servants are at home, and tell your husband
or wife through the keyhole, but loud enough
for the servants to hear, that you aren’t tak-
ing any company ; keep this up at reasonable
intervals for three years, and, without any of
the jarrings of d.t.’s, hashhouse, taking in
washing, double establishments, gaol, or the
like, the thing is done, and the decree nisi in
its frame. Why, it’s easier than the long-
sighed-for ineompatibility-of-temper method,
and should prove a decided relaxation in the
ordinary monotony of married life. Of course
there must be collusion, but what Judge
bothers about collusion nowadays unless it is
obtrusively and foolishly thrust under his nose !

To the Vic. Democracy.
Apropos of the Victorian Council’s scornful re-
jection of the Woman Suffrage and One-man-
one-vote Bills, Melbourne Age takes a cheer-
less pot-shot at the skull of the Democracy
with the news that “ under the Vic. Constitu-
tion the Leg. Council is absolutely impreg-
nable”—therefore there is nothing for it but to
endure that angry mass of fat and live in hope.
It is this patient acquiescence which keeps the
House of 48 Earth-Stealers on top. Of course
there is no way provided in the Constitution by
which the Council can be shifted —but there
are ways outside of it. A moderate-sized in-
surrection would do it—but perhaps that would
be too wildly original. It could be done if the
progressive leaders of Victoria would set
themselves to work up a big Democratic League
for the purpose, and the biggest paper in Vic-
toria would put all its weight into the move-
ment. It would want ,£50,000 or more to work
on, but that could surely be raised. Then it
would have to find about 30 qualified Liberal
candidates, or, if there weren’t enough, to make
some by fixing them up with dummy pro-
perty qualifications. The test of each
candidate should be his solemn oath to
vote straight for a Reform Bill making the
Council a paid body elected by the whole
province as one constituency, under manhood
suffrage, with no property qualification for
members ; and each candidate should leave his
resignation in the hands of the League as
security. The candidates once found the block
vote of the League could put them in easily—
the electors are democratic enough, though they
have got the tired feeling badly. Or the Coun-
cil Reform Bill, above described, might be
passed through the present Council if there was
a big enough bribe attached—say a clause to
the effect that Victoria, in regretfully parting
with its present Councillors, gave each £lOOO
a year in recognition of his great and disin-
terested services. It would be only £48,000 a
year even at first, and many of the Councillors
are unhealthy and wouldn’t stay in the pension
list long. Moreover, their removal would make
it possible to pass a new land-tax law which
would yield three times £48,000. Another
promising wile would be a Council Reform Bill
providing a salary of £3OO a year for Coun-
cillors, and making the present M.’s.L.C.
nominees till death, resignation, insolvency, or
disqualification through absence for a whole
session, or through aoceptance of a State
contract or salaried State billet. But all
vacancies to be filled by election on a
manhood-suffrage basis, with no property-quali-
fication for members. The present M.’sL.C.
are life members in effect, so making them so
in name wouldn’t render things any worse.
And they are mostly old —death and other
things would remove them pretty fast, and when
they were gone there would be no more like
them. Victoria has to shift its 48 Rich Land-
lords, either by an expensive and elaborate
political propaganda, or by buying them out.
They are mostly hard men with a full appreci-
ation of the beauty of Cash, if offered judi-
ciously. And it would pay to buy them out.
“The Liberty of the Subject.”
The illogicalness of a woman in hysterics is
great, but the illogicalness and stupidity
of a politician (especially in Westralia) is
greater than a range of mountains and more in-
comprehensible than the vast Australian adjec-
tive. The other day the Sandgropers’ Parlia-
ment had a Health Bill before it, and the
Attorney-General, incited thereto by Vosper,
of the Tangled Mane, who owns beneath his
one shock of hair about 90 per cent, of the
brains of the House, moved the following very
sensible clause :
No person shall sell any patent or proprietary medicine,
drug 1 , or compound, of which a sample has not been
analysed by the Government analyst, and without the
copy of a certificate by the Government analyst being an-
nexed to the bottle, box, or package in which the medi-
cine, drug, or compound is coniained, stating the com-
ponent parts of the same according to the sample, under
a penalty not exceeding £2O. •
That looks simple enough bo bore into the very
humblest intellect. It provides that anybody
can sell his patent medicine as freely as he likes,
only it must be accompanied by an official ex-
planation on the label, so that the buyer will
know exactly what is in it. If it is an honest
compound the official analysis on the label will
help the sale. If the seller declares it is a rare
and infallible specific for Addison’s disease or
phthisis, and charges Bs. 9d. a bottle for it, and
murders people for Bs. 9d. by inducing them
to pin their faith to his nostrum and neglect
other treatment till they are past cure—well,
the compulsory affixment to his bottle of an
official statement that the contents are weak
cold tea and beer and peppermint is very mild
treatment. But the stupidest Parliament in
the Seven Provinces viewed the clause with
dark suspicion. Mitchell, M. L. A., got up and
said that a man should be allowed to use any
remedy he liked. Alex. Forrest said, indig-
nantly, that if a man wanted a certain patent
medicine he should be allowed to have it.
Wallace said that it would “create mono-
poly.” Another man said that he “saw no
reason why anybody shouldn’t be allowed to
take any medicine he pleased.” Hardly one
dolt in the whole crowd could be made to
understand that merely putting on the label an
official statement of the nature of the contents
wouldn’t prevent any man taking as much or as
many patent medicines as he wanted to, and
that analysis and monopoly had no possible
relation. But what killed Voseer’s idea
was the explanation by the prize ass of
the crowd, Wood, M.L.A., that to analyse
patent medicines is “a direct interference
with the liberty of the subject.” Which
of course it is—it interferes with the liberty of
the vendor to swindle the sick and dying by sell-
ing them a pennyworth of camomile water for
half-a-guinea as an infallible remedy for some-
thing, and it interferes with the buyer’s liberty
to be defrauded thereby. Which is quite as
horrible as depriving the burglar of liberty to
burgle, and the public of its inalienable right to
have its goods stolen in the night. When the
Sandgropers’ Assembly heard that the analysis
of patent medicines would interfere with the
liberty of the subject it rejected the idea on the
instant.
Shirking 1 the Difficulty.
One mistake about as big as a good-sized Alp
sticks conspicuously out of Premier Richard
Seddon’s Old-Age Pension scheme. Old-Age
Pensions are urgently needed —that much is
certain. The pension idea should be a part of
every Democratic policy—that also is certain.
Also, among these provinces Maoriland is just
the one with grit enough to lead the way, and
Seddon is just the man to be on deck at the
time. Also Seddon’s scheme, despite its re-
strictions, is at least a fair beginning ; about as
fair a beginning, probably, as money-considera-
tions will allow. It provides a pension of £lB
a-year for people over 65 with a decent charac-
ter and 20 years’ residence to their credit, and
the estimated cost, to begin with, will be £90,000
or £lOO,OOO a year. But the scheme altogether
shirks the question of raising the money. In-
stead- of being accompanied with plans and
specifications for finding an extra £lOO,OOO a
year, it is simply provided that the cash is to
come out of the Consolidated Revenue. Just
now the revenue can show a balance of £lOO,OOO
a year over ordinary 'expenditure, but it is
all wanted for public works —in fact, it isn’t
nearly sufficient for public works, and the old
loan-floating distemper is starting again as a con-
sequence. If the money that now goes into the
Public Works Fund goes in Old-Age Pensions
instead, then more of the public works will be
paid out of loans instead. And if it was put the
other way—if the surplus went in public works
as at present, and the Old-Age Pensions were
paid out of loans—it would be exactly the same
thing though it might not sound so well. If
the Government which grants Old-Age Pen-
sions doesn’t also find the money to pay them
with, it is simply taking the glory and leaving
the hard work to posterity. The finding of the
money is the middle and both ends of the Old-
Age Pension problem, and, if that isn’t done,
there is very little done.
The Sydney police prosecution of the Coupon
co. seems to have been based on the false as-
sumption that goods purchased with the
coupons are “ prizes ” in the lottery sense of
the term. Why “prizes”? Certain retail
traders offer their customers a discount on
cash purchasers, the dis. being granted in
coupons. Customers get it on the understand-
ing that they will “take it out” in other
goods at the Coupon co.’s shop. The coupon
system is quite an old device which has been
tried in many places and found effective—for a
time. It comes and goes. There can be no
enduring charm in it. If every shop were
to adopt the system the world would soon
get overloaded with fancy goods such as the
Coupon shop supplies, and if the Coupon
shop were to furnish necessaries, as well
as opera-glasses, &c., then the shops that sup-
ply the same goods in an ordinary way would
have nothing to do with the company. At a
casual glance the coupon, or “gift,” scheme of
business looks capable of wide extension, but
in practice it is bound to break down eventu-
ally. All the drapers in Sydney, for instance,
might agree to support a coupon co. which
confined itself to the tea-trade— i.e., the bits of
paper given as discount on every sixpenny dra-
pery-purchase would be exchangeable for tea
only. The all-round increase in the drapers’ cash
trade would not be very considerable. Pre-
sently a principal drapery shop, doirg one of
the biggest cash businesses, would break away
from the arrangement and start its own “coupon
tea ’’-shopou thepremises, supplying better value
tea than the co. could afford to give. Then other
big drapers would set up a competition until
the co. failed for lack of support, whereupon
the competing drapers would agree to cease the
tea struggle and stick to their drapery trades
as in the beginning. Or, more probably, one of
the big houses would upset the coupon system
by underselling its rivals, and showing to cus-
tomers that they could get more than the value
of “ tea ” discount in the extra value of the
drapery goods on which no coupon was allowed.
The tea-trade, by the way, was the first to
take up the coupon system and will be the last
to drop it. Nevertheless the large tea-dealers
have expanded their businesses without
troubling their heads about coupons, whilst
hundreds of “ tea-gift ” shops have sprung up,
flourished awhile, and faded away.
The M.L. Police Report has at last seen light.
It is a huge document, but of little value.
Ted Taylor, the prohibitionist and social
purist from Christchurch, was the man upon
whose specific charges the Commission was
appointed. He charged the police with im-
morality, insobriety and with resorting to
political influence, and named his men. He
also blew the religious horn, on the ground that
Catholics had received an undue number of
appointments. Also one sergeant had a prohi-
bition order out against him ; and so on. After
collecting 1300 pages of fearful stuff the Com-
mission found that the Catholics weren’t
specially conspicuous in the force, and the
sergeant was twelve months out of the force
before he took to whisky. The accusations of
immorality were mostly withdrawn. The
Maoriland police department is undermanned,
underpaid, under-officered, and very badly gone
to pieces through general mismanagement.
But the wild teetotaller and social gimlet man
always wants to prove that somebody keeps a
harem and has eight illegitimate children and
suffers regularly from snakes. Tea Taylor
wasted about <£6ooo of public money in tracking
imaginary Flossies to the Maoriland copman’s
back door, and left the real grievance preLty
much alone. The grievance that has no Flossie
in it doesn’t appeal to the Cold Tea under-
standing.
-<*> —
One of the loudest yells in Adelaide against
State fire insurance is that the business isn’t
big enough to make it worth while. Yet the
private offices collect about £52,000 a year in
premiums in S.A. ; a few years ago they col-
lected .£70,000, but expenses were high through
too many offices trying to live on the business,
and rates were high to cover expenses, and
many people left off' insuring on account of the
high rates, and business diminished. Anyhow,
if Adelaide, for instance, adopts compulsory
State insurance the premium - income will
largely increase, because places not now insured
will be covered under the new system. And if
the State, with no commission to pay, and only
one city office and staff' to live on the business,
instead of about a dozen, couldn’t make the
business answer even on the present £52,000
premium income, what do the private offices live
on? There were 16 of them last year with head
offices in Australia and Tasmania, and 12 of the
16 had a premium income not nearly approach-
ing £52,000, while seven didn’t even reach
£20,000. And some of these had to keep up
expensive establishments in three or four pro-
vinces to raise even this miserable little income.

Vic. Assembly inserted a minimum-wage clause
in the Maffra Beet Sugar Co.’s Bill, but the
48 Rich Landlords threw it out. It was again
inserted by the Assembly, and again rejected
by the Council, and then, at Premier Turner’s
invitation, the Assembly caved in for the usual
reason—“ so as not to jeopardise the passing of
the Bill.” Vic. Council practically always wins
for the same reason ; the Assembly doesn’t
want to “jeopardise the Bill.” But if the
Assembly the People’s Chamber —is ever
going to be anything more than a slave dragged
at the chariot wheels of the House of Property
it will have to insist on its Democratic ideas
every time, and either make the Council cave
in or else make it take the responsibility of
throwing out every measure—until the com-
plete stoppage of business wakes the people up.
Vic. Assembly should have re-inserted its
minimum-wage clause and sent it back to the
Council 70 times over, if need be, till the Coun-
cil either passed it or took the direct responsi-
bility of rejection. The Assembly’s efforts to
keep the peace with the Council are a mistake.
What it wants is war—gory war—to arouse the
people to an understanding of its House of
Bricks and Mortar.

“ J.H.P.” to The Bulletin
Rougemont’s magazine-booming taradiddles ought to
make Australians who pretend to culture and an interest
in scientific and historical research a little uneasy. A half-
educated charlatan can tell the most absurd stories of
aboriginal customs and of Far-North and Centralian
fauna, and, in our ignorance, we are unable to prove him
a “cram.” We can’t supplant his fictions and put the truth
in their place ; we can’t tell the world what is at our back-
door. Foreign Governments and institutions have done
more than our own to find out, too. At this moment Dr.
Eylmann, a German scientist, is plodding away in the
heart of the continent. Even the University of little
Christiania had its Dr. Dahl in the Northern Territory
two or three years ago ; and Dr. Lumiiolz, of the same
place, had preceded him. There is a marvellous store of
information to be had, much of it of the sort,
too, that a big crowd relishes —books in the run-
ning niggers, sermons in their spears, and good
copy in everything. Wherefore some great Aus-
tralian daily, which fills its pages with shallow stuff
concerning cyclists’rides through Asiaand elsewhere, might
do worse than equip a couple of scientific busbmen
(surely there are two to be had), and commission them
for a three years’ fossick. And if the experiment
answered—well, there is more than one area which would
repay such investigation; But the time is flying. Already,
owing to association with whites (mostly those whites
to whom an aborigine is a blanky nigger, to be belted if
the aborigine doesn’t happen to be a plump young she),
the young men turn their backs upon tribal customs,
and are not admitted to the final initiation : and so they
remain ignorant of the sacred tribal learning. And then
the spirits take the old men; and they in their turn take
with them much that, even if it isn’t pearls and rubies
when measured by the standards of the shop and Ex-
change, is at least worth a lot to those who now and then
like to drop those standards down the nearest sewer-shaft.

Cooke, of the Victorian House of 48 Landlords :
Neither a man nor a woman has a right to vote ; it is a
question of what is best for the country. . . . Matters
should be left where they are till the women threaten a
revolution unless they get a vote, and then it should be
granted as a matter of expediency, not of political justice.
This is a pretty straight hint that neither man-
hood nor womanhood suffrage nor Council
reform will be obtained from the 48 Land-
lords until they are driven to it by terror
(or perhaps paid for granting it), and as
Cooke suggests revolution, perhaps it would
be a good thing to take him at his word and
fetch the revolution along. Cooke, for one, ad-
mits that he doesn’t care about justice but will
yield to save his carcase, so the endangerment
of Cooke’s carcase is evidently necessary and
advisable. But revolutions get out of hand
sometimes, and now and then a Fat Man is
taken with a brick in the stomach just while he
is in the act of yielding, because he was too
dull to recognise the exact moment when the
country’s patience gave out.
Here is a seaman’s grievance, as set forth to
The Bulletin by “ Malle ” :
Was speaking on a northern trading boat to one of the
crew, and remarked that fares had dropped since I last
travelled. “ It’s all right,” he said, “ for you people to
save a few shillings on your fares, but how about us ?
Directly opposition starts, down come our wages, and up
go our hours. We used to have an hour or two at home,
but now we arrive in the morning and often go out again
same night. There is hardly ever time to put her in the
dock while she’s got a decent waddle left in her. Why,
we’ve been upon our feet 50 hours on end sometimes.
And those northern cockies, what do they care? They
get their bit o’ cane in and sit down and watch it grow,
and wait for the company to come along and cut it for
’em. There’s no legitimate trade on the rivers, it’s all
tick, tick ; sometimes a man goes up from Sydney, goin'
to play the devil with a cash trade, but you can see King-
street writ on his back-door from the start. Damn the
competition and cheap fares, I say.”
