Bmou “ The Dovecot.”
Opera House Rickards Variety Co.
Rev. Geo. Walters’ unspeakable drama
lasted one short week at Sydney Lyceum, and
then it went to its own place, which, if it is the
place that such a drama most calls for, is unpub-
lishable and over 90,000 in the shade. “ A Flight
for Life,” which succeeded it, is a change greatly
for the better. The new outbreak is well-staged
and well-played, and the usually weary F. Scuda-
more has for once built a rather clever plot with
at least one totally fresh idea in it. Said idea is so
fresh that it alone should ensure the “Flight for
Life ” a respectable run. It is the only drama
The Bulletin remembers wherein the hero, being
unjustly suspected of murder, bolts on the instant.
The regulation idea is for him to pound on his
stomach like the pure and spotless gorilla, and say
that he relies on British justice, and that Provi-
dence will not allow the innocent to suffer, and
a jury of his countrymen will clear his character,
or some such cheap moral dictionary. The present
hero, finding the dead body left on his hands, re-
marks instead that he isn’t too sure about Provi-
dence, and that the average jury is a great fool,
and that he will come back when he has
evidence to clear himself, and not sooner. Then
he stoushes the policeman, gathers his best
girl (who is also suspected) under his arm,
and strikes out for Central Africa. He gets along
part of the way disguised as a policeman and pro-
fessing to be in pursuit of himself, and the rest by
lying, or shooting, or driving his boot into the
chest of whoever gets into the road, but he is
quite solid in his determination not to stay and
take any British justice. And his friend, the
comic curate, does likewise. The curate’s small
trouble is that the girl whom the villain seduced
handed him her unlawful baby and went away.
He was too conscientious to throw the infant over
a fence, so all the elderly pussy-cats of his con-
gregation set him down as its father, and he is
cast out of his parish. He ought also to spank
his chest and say that he will face his accusers
and clear his character, but he doesn’t. He dis-
gustedly takes up his infant, and leaves his
character behind, and goes to Africa also.
* • * •
Then the seduced girl in this quite un-
usual drama, instead of drifting round in tears
about her lost honor, or drowning herself, or
becoming a mendicant and having the bone thrown
to her on the mat, puts on her prettiest clothes,
and takes a billet as barmaid, and is quite happy
and respectable. She doesn’t even come back
on a snowy night to die on her father’s doorstep,
or call in at a graveyard and faint on her
mother’s tomb. In fact, everything, including
certain adventures in Africa, in which the hero
routs an army of niggers by means of a flying-
machine, is more or less unexpected. Barring a
painful savage and a feeble policeman, the co. does
its duty very well. Miss Helen Burdett is a streak
of loveliness in the character of the heroine, and
Misses Ada Woodhill and Alice May are two more
streaks. Circumstances force the latter to disguise
herself as a boy, and three sailors partly undress
her for a bath before she can explain her identity
and stop a very interesting insult. Harry Diver,
for the first time since The Bulletin knew him,
makes a reasonable success as the good man of the
story ; also Bert Bailey, Harcourt, and Fitz-
gerald give the tale a kindly lift in various places.
Bailey, who wears the soft hat of a blameless theo-
logical life till he is blighted by having the infant
handed to him, is rather an interesting character.
An ineffective invalid is so promptly strangled in
Act I. that he hasn’t time to become a burden.
News arrives once that he is having a fit, but
happily he does it behind the scenes.
** * *
A dramatic spec, whose days promise to
be few and much blown upon by the cold wind of
affliction, lifted the curtain last Saturday at Syd-
ney Criterion. John Cosgrove, Johnston Weir
and Miss Inez Bensusan are three bright spots
in a hungry desert of cast, and two or three
other people leave some greenness in the wilder-
ness, but a considerable part of the company is
plain desert without even a small bottle-tree on
the far horizon. Cosgrove puts much vigor into
the part of the hero, and Miss Bensusan, who
appears for the first time as a middle-aged lady of
horrible character, makes that lady far the best
feature in the production, and earns a large fresh
laurel all her own. The heroine, on the other
hand, is wooden and woesome ; also her voice
is flat and her hat is unspeakable. The play has
some possibilities about it under better circum-
stances. Miss Bensusan’s scapegrace son is in
trouble at the beginning through his secret
marriage to an adventuress; but when his wife
proves to be a short costermonger girl with a
bunch ©f hair on her forehead, and a frowsy dresss,
and a huge sixpenny hat flying loose over one eye,
all the possibility goes out of the business. Es-
specially so as her father is a burglar with a
short pipe, and her brother a hairy murderer
attired in a black eye and a Bill Bikes eap. These
people heap themselves on top of Cosgrove and
carry him by night over the back fence of their
“crib” in a low part of London, and leave him on
the railway line. There is beautiful sylvan scenery
on the railway line, the kind one would naturally
find just across Bill Sikes’s back fence. And
there in the dead of night the express comes along,
and as the man who finds Cosgrove can’t unfasten
him, and has no signal light, he waves a very small
pocket-handkerchief in the dead of night, and the
express at once comes in and stops dead. Evi-
dently it was only ten yards off when the rescuer
hung out his handkerchief, and was a very tame
express indeed.
• • • •
An alleged phonograph, however, calls for
the loudest sniff of scorn. Cosgrove having been
disinherited for marrying the wooden girl, goes off
to some land not particularised to make his for-
tune, and his friend Johnston Weir brings back a
message which the ’graph delivers in a gruff voice
that seems to have lost its false teeth. Later on
Miss Bensusan lays a plot for the ruin of Cos-
grove’s bride (who gets a long spell in gaol) and the
phonograph, which was listening, repeats her
remarks afterwards in the proper quarter. It
repeats them in the same gruff tone with the same
blast blowing through its missing teeth—therefore
the audience gets a dark suspicion that the
same man is speaking each time. The ’graph
isn’t a weapon to be taken seriously when it
cries out through its funnel in the good cause of
justice. The short girl improves greatly as the
drama proceeds, but just when she is beginning to
develop into a pleasant character she suddenly
relapses, for no discoverable reason, into the old
frock, and the coster accent, and the insulting
sixpenny hat, and spoils the illusion. There are
patches of good dialogue in the “Dangers of Lon-
don,” most of them contributed by the two
burglars, and some humor and not much plot;
and a certain steamer isn’t at all bad. With a
better co. and better management, and a phono-
graph that had a less obtrusive cold on its chest, it
might come to something. But the present phono-
graph wants to be booted right over the tank.
** * *
The Doll opera at Her Majesty’s is alive
and well, though its fourth week is nearly over.
Probably the fact that Sydney has been so long
without opera makes it take its Doll in a more
cordial spirit than might otherwise be expected, or
possibly the wooden charmer is making way en-
tirely on her own merits. This paper isn’t quite
certain, but the cash results are the same either
way.
*& m *
No change is due at Sydney Royal for
some considerable time yet. The little wooden
horse which carries the last remnant of Baker’s
fortunes runs for his life like a little wooden hero
every night, and comes in by several lengths. As
for the Jewish-looking youth who strangles Miss
Watson, after hearing her perfidy through the
keyhole, the mystery of his fate remains unex-
plained. Miss Watson strangles very success-
fully ; she is of a lithe and sinuous build, and she
writhes in a realistic, serpentine fashion when the
Jewish youth gets her throat in both hands, and
finally coils up on the floor in a kind of boneless
abandon which suggests that she is really dead.
The complete straightness of the dramatic dead
too often spoils the illusion.
** * *
The old, forgotten Alhambra, down in the
remote wastes of Brickfield Hill, Sydney, re-
opened on Saturday after an almost unbroken
silence of years. W. J. Wilson and Percy St.
John are lessees. The theory is that at the far
end of George-street there is a population that
wants to get to the city theatres, but knows it is
no use trying because it would drink its eighteen-
pence by the way ; and that this population can be
profitably reached by bringing the bones and the
tambourine-nigger and the interlocutor to its door.
The new venture commenced with large houses, at
all events, and looks at present like bringing
wealth to a deserving management. A very
respectable co., including John Coleman, of the
never-to-be-forgotten Scarecrow Dance (that per-
formance which suggests the canter of a hanged
curate in poor circumstances), is in possession, and
the scenery is put on with a liberal hand.
** * *
Dante, conjuror, magician, wonder-worker
and miscellaneous wizard, opens at Sydney Palace
Theatre on Saturday under the management of
L. J. Lohr.
** * *
Albert Bellman and Lottie Moore have
returned to the Tivoli, the latter being favorably
remembered as the brown-haired vision with the
green shoes who used to turn a somersault in a
pantomime. The charming Ada Reeve has also
joined Rickards’ show, but, though she can somer-
sault likewise, the great heart of Sydney is true to
the two green feet that flew over the brown head
in those pleasant bygone days. Biondi remains,
too, and is becoming more different people every
day, and the disagreements which arise between
the different people who are all Biondi grow excit-
ing. Some day that combustible Italian, when he
has seen himself through the window making love
to himself disguised as his own wife, will burst in
before the other two of him have had time to get
away, and w ill do both of him an injury.
* « as *
At Melb. Bijou, the good humor of
“Jane,” plus the charm of popular prices, didn’t
excite a rush for seats, and, on Saturday, they
revived “The Little Minister,” with variations
from the original cast. Melbourne seems to be
“qff ” the Bijou, otherwise the quaint Scotch play
might have prospered exceedingly this time, for it
emits the smaller stage, and is, in a measure, all
the better for being more quietly aeted here than
at the Princess’s. Lesly Thomson—he was im-
ported to play the name-part, but fell a victim to
sciatica as soon as he came to hand —is now chasing
Babbie for the first time. Cecil Ward took a
bold, melodramatic stand, and was an essentially
heroic shepherd of the auld kirk flock, whereas
Lesly Thomson is a mere boy in appearance—
an odd-looking little fellow, very unassertive, and
quite true to the character as it shapes in Barry’s
book. Cecil Ward was an effective stage-lover.
The new Meenister is a simple suggestion of the
verdant Rev. Gavin Dishart, and he puts a differ-
ent complexion on the play. Some parts of it look
weaker, whilst others look stronger, than before.
Take him altogether, Lesly Thomson has a
nationalising influence on the play; the fine
old whusky elders of the kirk are in line
with him, and, although he is very much
gone at the knees, The Bulletin will put it
down to the sciatica and expect his unlucky limbs
to improve. Other changes in the cast are satis-
factory. Walner Gregory, who made a painful
impression as the Captain, is now posing as the
old Earl, and W.G.’s commanding pose and big
elderly voice (which would pass for Wilson Bar-
rett’s in the dark) fill the billet nicely.

The last week of Pattie Browne’s short
season will be probably devoted to “The Dove-
cot, ” a recent London comedy adapted from the
French by Charles Brookfield.
* * *- »
The scenery and costumes in “ Under the
Red Robe ” give a high tone to that vague, un-
certain narrative, and audiences at Melbourne
Princess’s are quite as numerous as could be ex-
pected. The play, apart from the fittings, doesn’t
improve on acquaintance. It is a gaudy adapta-
tion of a light story. There was not much character-
drawing in the novel, and there is no reality
about the principals in the drama. Julius'
-Knight introduces himself as a person to be
shuddered at. He swaggers into a “two-up
school” and the other gamblers remark, “Ha!
the Black Death ! ” after which he runs his sword
into a dissatisfied juggins and is sentenced by
Cardinal Richelieu to become a private detective.
When the audience next see Julius he is adding
burglary to his private detective business, and
from time to time he gives vent to remorse, by way
of endeavouring to explain why the heroine fell in
With him at first sight. But the dull baritone
‘ O Gods ” of the Black Death gentleman don’t
explain his success with the fair sex. To people
who haven’t read the book the play is a puzzle.
They never get an idea as to the purpose of
the insurrection which is about to be promoted
by the wooden husband. This useless rebel ap-
pears to have plotted away all his property, bar a
parcel of diamonds, and when the diamonds are
found to be missing from the place whence Julius
Knight prigged them, the wooden young man’s
sister states that the Honor of the Family is lost !
Julius, who was hiding behind a panel all the
time, produces the Honor of the Family from his
pocket, and the maiden having previously cursed
him for the mean private detective that he is, falls
at his feet and asks forgiveness, instead of asking
him what he meant by stealing the bag of Family
Honor from a secret drawer in a secret cupboard.
The one quite satisfactory performance is
George Majeroni’s Cion, but the drama is slow to
proclaim Cion’s dumbness, therefore the general
public regard his gesticulations as low-comedy
business, and his shrieks of agony in the back-
ground don’t command much more sympathy than
the sufferings of the Xmas pantaloon at the point
of a red-hot poker. Miss Ferrar and Julius Knight
are to blame in this matter. They speak of Cion’s
dumbness, but they don’t drive the point home.
Their elocution begins to pall upon the ear; it is
so mannered and mechanical. The Bulletin
would pass further remarks upon the two mono-
tonous voices aforesaid if they were not less irrita-
ting than W. F. Hawtry and Gaston Mervale.
** * *
At Melb. Royal the new serio-comic
spectacle is pretty freely patronised, especially in
the gallery, where the small boys take out their
sixpen’orth in warbling the chorus to “ Sons of the
Sea. ” Manager Charles Maurice sings the verses
with much Franco-German expression, but his top-
note needs assistance and that’s where the gallery
comes in. Left to himself frisky Maurice
would sound rather out of keeping with
the song’s sentiment; for he can’t bark like “boys
of the Bull-dog Br-reed that made Old Hingland’s
fame.” Nevertheless his foreign enthusiasm is in-
fectious, and his circus manner of bounding off
the stage between the verses is an entertainment
in itself. The Bell Ballet and the volcanic erup-
tion of-fireworks have improved with practice,
also the original Lord Glenarvon took ill on Satur-
day and another lord filled his place and shone by
comparison. There are places where shines by
comparison are almost inevitable.

The Poluskis are re-appearing at the
Opera House for 12 nights only, and the liver of
the dyspeptic infidel who writes this paragraph
will get shaken up violently during their stay.
The two Poluskis are many degrees brighter and
more various than any other two men in the
variety line, as far as Australia knows. Their
mixed performance yields fifteen different
sorts of laughs, and two or three of the choicest
brands are beneficial alike to the mind, the stum-
mick, and the soul. How rarely does one’s im-
mortal spirit find anything to roar at in a music-
hall “turn” which concludes with acrobatics!
Next Saturday a couple of new ladies, Nellie
Wilson and Ethel Yorke, will arrive at the Opera
House; also Ajax, the puttiest and contortion-
ablest person on earth, is to appear shortly.

Marriage Laws.
Public scandals connected with the perform-
ance of the marriage ceremony are compelling
Victoria, as from time to time they compel the
other provinces, to potter with its Marriage
Act. One would have thought that marriage
was an institution old enough to have had its
legal formulas permanently settled long ere
now. Yet every year reveals abuses which
create new ones, and, owing to the defective
ecclesiastico-civil legislation that is in fashion
in English-speaking communities, the loosely-
conducted matrimonial agency, the disfrocked
hitcher-on, the marriage-brocage contract, the
marriage-trap, the drunk’s ceremony without
ceremony, the carelessly - compiled marriage-
register, and so forth, luxuriate all over Aus-
tralia.
* * » *
There is no doubt that, while the State
deems it its duty to insist that couples are pro-
perly hitched, it is also its duty to provide
simple and uniform methods for the operation,
and that can only be done when the control of
the marriage ceremony is entirely within its
own hands. At present in Australia it allows
the Church to do part of the State’s duty, and,
to a great extent, trusts to luck that the
Church does that part properly.
* • * *
An imperium in imperio is always dangerous
when the Church is the imperium, and it is
nothing short of marvellous that British com-
munities, usually so jealous of Church interfer-
ence, should have allowed its intrusion in
marriage so long, especially when one remem-
bers that most of the most church-ridden States
of Europe have succeeded in preventing Church
interference in this particular matter. The
State cannot allow the Church to celebrate
binding civil marriages without recognising
Church officers as being for this purpose State
officers. And certainly this recognition is not
a desirable one—for the State. The Victorian
experience, resulting in the present attempt at
improvement, goes to show that serious abuses
must grow when the State recognises as civil
officers irresponsible people over whose choice
it has no control and whom it cannot dismiss at
pleasure.
* * * *
The plain way out of the difficulty is to enact
that, as long as a ceremony is held essential to
marriage, the ceremony must be performed and
the contract entered into in the presence of a
civil official, and in a civil form of words.
• m * *
Such a law, recognising marriage in the
only way it should be recognised by the State,
as a civil institution, can offend nobody ; for
there is no reason whatever against the parties
interested having, in addition, whatever ecclesi-
astical or other ceremony their consciences
approve, performed before or after the civil and
legally-binding ceremony. This method works
well in Austria and in Italy, countries in which
the ecclesiastical element would be sufficiently
strong to cause the repeal of the law if grave
objection were found against it.
* * * »
The person before whom the contract is
entered into would be a civil official, appointed
and removable by the State, with hours of duty
regulated with regard both to decency and
convenience, and would not be allowed to turn
his office into a mart or a house of assignation.
He would besides be in duty bound to accu-
rately register the marriages, and would thus
do away with the uncertainty and confusion so
often now resulting from the haphazard and
trusting-to-memory entries of perhaps half-
discarded pastors.
• * * *
It is to be hoped that, even if the provincial
legislatures do not dare to brave the clergy in
this matter, it will be one of the very first
things dealt with by the Federal Parliament
under the powers given it in marriage matters.
Curious that the last legislative work of
Q. Premier Byrnes—head of a long Govt. —was an
attempt to pass a democratic enactment—Work-
man’s Lien Bill.
Felicitous juxtaposition of two items in
Sydney D.T.
Mount Vesuvius is again in a state of dangerous
activity.
The Emperor William of Germany says that the Euro-
pean peoples now live in serious times, and revolutionary
designs will have to be forcibly combated.
Grainger, S.A.’s political Ishmael, of whom
the Moonta miners ought to be getting rather
tired, made an attempt to maintain his some-sort
of reputation as a financial critic by a slashing
attack on the State Fire Insurance Bill. Grain-
ger’s argument was that foreign insurance co.’s
only succeeded (most of them don’t succeed) by
spreading their risks over a large area—they put
only one small egg in each basket. Which was
answered beforehand by M. Twain—“ Put your
eggs in one basket, and watch it!” When a man
gets scared about having too much hen-fruit in one
basket he puts some in another. And very often
that other basket straightway goes over.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
N.S.W. revenue for September, 1898 (£691,240),
is £10,173 better than that for September, 1897. For the
three months now concluded of the financial year, the
revenue (12,226,146) is £110,973 ahead of that for the cor-
responding quarter of 1897. So far, the expenditure
(£2,248,695) is £22,549 larger than the revenue. As for
the loan expenditure, it still swells amazingly. For the
quarter, £501,348 of borrowed money has been spent, fully
half of it on unproductive works, which should be paid
out of revenue, as compared with £367,522 for the cor-
responding quarter of 1897 1 If this rate of expenditure is
kept up, the year ending June, 1899, will be celebrated by
the biggest riot of borrowed money that N.S.W.
has heard of since 1892 —the year when Dibbs
tried to slacken off the borrowing frenzy, and to
persuade N.S.W. not to charge quite all the culverts and
the wooden bridges and wharves to posterity.
y * * *
Tasmania’s revenue for September was =£68,745,
an increase of £1457 on the figures for September, 1897.
Nine months of the financial year have passed, and the
State receipts of the Isle of Sleep-a-Lot are,£61,175 ahead
of the figures for the corresponding period of last year.
At the same time, it begins to look as if the rise had
nearly spent itself, for the time at least. Tasmania’s best
record was in ’9l, when the revenue reached £883,199.
Then it fell so suddenly that in 1894 it was only £696,795.
For the present year the long-bearded Fysh reckons to
beat even the 1891 record with a revenue of £913,275.
This is assuming an increase of £68,000 over ’97, and with
£61,175 already to the good, and three months to run, he
may get there.
» * * »
Victoria’s revenue for July, August, and Sep-
tember is £1,684,281, being an increase of £100,332 on the
receipts for the same three months of 1897. And as the
proposed new borrowing boom hasn’t started yet, the
increase is not the result of any fictitious state of pro-
sperity created by the pouring of loan moneys into the
country—the fictitious boom isn’t quite due yet. Vic-
toria, like Tasmania, doesn’t publish monthly statements
of expenditure, so that no comparison can be made under
this heading ; in this respect Turner might do well to
follow the wise example of N.S.W. and Queensland. Also,
now that the revenue begins to show a large improve-
ment, it would be a good time to pass a law making com-
pulsory an annual payment of £200,000 or £250,000 in
reduction of the deficit. Nobody doubts that Turner will
toil vigorously at the reduction of the deficit so long as he
keeps office, but if he goes out of office Duncan the
Boomer has the reversion of his billet, and old Boom Dun-
can requires to be strictly tied up to make him go even
reasonably straight.
* * * *

increase of £4085. This looks as if the luck of the most
drought-stricken province in Australia had begun to turn
at last. The awfulness of S.A.’s bad fortune in the last

* * * *
Maoriland Opposition professes to be dreadfully
grieved in the stomach over the alleged fact (which no
one seems to have heard of before) that the London Stock
Exchange refused last July to admit a proposed Maori-
land conversion loan of £2,700,000 among its list of quoted
securities. It doesn’t appear to be absolutely certain yet
that the painful event ever happened, but, supposing it
did, the Tory party considers that the credit of. the pro-
vince has been dragged in the dust—something after the
manner of a drunk being taken to the lock-up along a dry
road. This necessity for keeping on terms of favorable
grovel with the London Stock Exchange is one of the
inevitable woes of the borrowing system. If one of the
Seven Provinces would absolutely swear off borrowing,
and disrespectfully cast off the London Stock Exchange
for ever, it would be a pleasant change. Then, when its
loans fell due and it couldn’t pay the principal, it could
simply give every holder 3 per cent, interminable stock,
repayable at the State’s option, for his expired securities,
and save all the usual flotation expenses, by telling him to
take them or leave them. It might be a trifle high-handed,
but it would be reasonably honest, anyhow ; and, if the
London Stock Exchange felt so bad as to refuse a quota-
tion for the new consolidated 3 per cent, debt, and thereby
damaged the province’s credit, the province need not
mind, it having resolved to ask for no more credit. Also,
if the Stock Exchange’s action ran down the price of the
securities, it would be so much the better for the province
when it started to buy them in and extinguish them.
* * * •
Premier Turner’s proposal for the conversion
of the Victorian public debt is plain and choke-full of
common sense. He doesn’t propose any large conversion
scheme at all, having decided that the reduction in inter-
est wouldn’t be worth the extra principal that the bond-
holder would want; he merely asks a general authority to
replace the 4 and 5 per cent, loans as they become due, or
nearly due, with a uniform 3 per cent, stock. During the
next five years 9J millions of high-priced loans fall due,
and by renewing at 3 per cent. Turner expects to save
the province £120,000 per annum interest. At least part
of the saving is to go towards a sinking fund —if Parlia-
ment has sense enough to see the value of the pro-
posal. Probably it was the experience of Maoriland
which convinced Turner that large loan conversions
don’t pay. That province, when interest dropped
to 4 per cent., decided that it had about reached bottom,
so it induced the holders of £19,724,000 of miscellaneous
high-priced State securities, with various terms to run, to
exchange them for £21,325,000 of 4 per cent, stock ; and,
in addition to giving the holders £1,600,000 more debt as
an inducement to convert, it fixed the new loan till 1929.
It saved a good deal of interest by the deal, but now it
finds that if it had left the matter alone, and paid the old
6 or 6 per cent, till the old loans expired, it could have
converted them at 3£, or perhaps even 3, per cent., and
saved more interest in the end without adding £1,600,000
to the principal. Then interest got down to 3£ per cent.,
and Maoriland started another conversion operation. It
exchanged about £6,000,000 of old securities for 3£ per
cent, stock, and gave the holders nearly £500,000 extra
stock as an inducement, and also fixed the 3£ per cent,
rate till 1940. Now interest is down to about 3 per cent.,
and this second transaction also looks like a hideously bad
bargain. The State that goes making big loan-conversion
deals with the London financiers generally finds it has
been playing dice with the devil.
* * * *
The Australian Alliance Assurance Co. (Mel-
bourne) finished up a bad year on 31st August, 1898. The
fire, marine, and fidelity-guarantee departments ended
with a loss of £14,792, and, on top of this, a dividend of 6
per cent, on nominal capital, or 15 per cent, on the
amount originally paid up, is declared, absorbing £7500.
This is done by withdrawing £25,000 from reserve. Just
at this same disastrous period, it has occurred to the
management that it has never made any proper provision
for the depreciation of its assets since the boom time, and
another £lO,OOO is appropriated for this purpose, reducing
the reserve from £llO,OOO to £75,000. Six years ago, the
Australian Alliance had a paid-up capital of £50,000, and
£200,000 reserve, but it transferred £75,000 of the reserve
to capital account, and wrote off £15,000 on account of an
unlucky transaction with the deceased Federal Bank.
The recent proceedings of the Australian Alliance have
been as follows :
Prnfita Dividend on original
ir roots. ca pital of £50,000.

While paying these huge dividends out of a declining
business, the Australian Alliance, as already mentioned,
made no adequate provision for losses, and its balance-
sheet simply intimates that £315,000 of assets (three-
fifths of the whole concern) are “ book value,” and £lO,OOO
more are put in at “cost.” Another item of the assets
are “Australasian Insurance co. shares £27,577” (the
whole paid-up capital of the Australasian is £25,000.) The
directors make no definite statement as to the amount of
depreciation; and whether the present appropriation of
£lO,OOO will cover all of them not otherwise provided for,
or whether it will only partly cover them, doesn’t appear.
The Australian Alliance runs a small life-assur-
ance department which is steadily fading away. The
triennial investigation (made on a sound 3£ per cent,
basis) shows a surplus of assets over life liabilities of
£9235. In 1895 no bonus was declared, and despite this
fact the surplus is now £9OOO less than it was three years
ago, so apparently the life department has barely paid its
way. This time also no bonus is declared, and the whole
surplus is carried to suspense-account to provide for de-
preciation. This and the other provision made leave
£20,287 to cover possible losses on £527,000 worth of
assets, and if it isn’t sufficient, as there is no more sur-
plus left in the life department, presumably the share-
holders’ reserve fund must stand the consequences. At
all events it would be much better to write everything
down to present values at once, and let the whole situa-
tion be made clear; the method of leaving inflated values
standing among the assets and a reserve (which may be
sufficient, or more than sufficient, or quite insufficient)
on the other side, is hazy and unsatisfactory. The fact
that the life assurance funds last year yielded only
£3 15s. 4d. per cent, interest on their alleged value cer-
tainly looks as if a very considerable writing down is
much needed. Also, the Australian Alliance has paid
huge dividends quite long enough on the reputation of its
past prosperity. Even a very strong co. can’t afford to
pay away more than its earnings in dividends too often.
* * * m
It is announced that the Southern (Marine)
Insurance Co. of Melbourne has decided to sell out to an
English co.—the British and Foreign. This makes the
fifth Australian insurance co. that has sold out this year.
There were 14 (Maoriland and Tasmanian not counted) at
the beginning of 1898 ; there are now only nine, and Tiie
Bulletin’s idea about State insurance, or else Protection
for the local insurance industry, keeps growing stronger.
The Southern died from the usual cause ; it paid glorious
15 per cent, dividends in the good times, and never
piled up enough reserves to enable it to stand even a
moderate spell of bad ones. So, after an honored and
profitable existence of 34 years, five bad half-years (not
consecutive ones, and only one of them very bad) made it
crumple up. During the last six years it made over
£14,000 profit, and spent over £33,000 in dividends—and
died. If it had accumulated a respectable reserve in its
good days, and invested it in State securities instead of
boom mortgages, the Southern might have been a 10 per
cent, investment for a few centuries yet.
The most remarkable collapse in the insurance
business has been in S.A. Fifteen years ago S.A. had six
fire and marine insurance co’s, with £215,000 capital and
£lBB,OOO reserves, and one mutual life office (the S.A.
Mutual Life, founded in 1880, and just trying to get on
its poor feet with £9BO of accumulated funds). Now the
whole seven have disappeared. Also, three banks have
vanished out of four that S.A. possessed in 1883, so the
great wheat province has established a very good record
of financial smash.

The Victorian Permanent Building Society,
another of Melbourne’s huge land speculation concerns,
closed the year to 31st July, 1898, with a loss of £6909.
How the Victorian has been going of late
July, 1893—Loss.. £7,832 I July, 1896—Loss.. £7,439
„ 1894 „ .. 15,809 „ 1897 „ .. 6,946
„ 1895 ~ .. 8,107 I „ 1898 „ .. 6,909
In addition to this the Victorian has written off, in six
years, £10,715 losses on realisation of properties. The
above list comprises trading losses only, arising from ina-
bility to earn even the very humble 21 per cent, interest on
the reconstructed deposits. In fact, the Victorian’s trou-
ble is that it earns practically no interest for its deposi-
tors at all. It has nominally £578,000 worth of assets,
and they produce a sufficient net revenue to cover
working expenses and interest on £BO,OOO bank
overdraft and mortgage. On these figures the
most probable explanation is that the whole lot
are worth something under £200,000 at present
values, in which case the shareholders’ capital of £231,000
is wholly lost, and the institution is only good for some-
thing over 10s. in the £to its other creditors. The Vic-
torian, like a good many other Melbourne companies,
makes no attempt to show the present value of its assets,
or to reveal how it really stands ; but, so far as can be
estimated by the figures supplied, its situation, barring a
revival in property-values, is quite hopeless. And no one
knows whether there will be a revival in property-values.
It all depends on whether present prices are normal or de-
pressed. If a comparison of the municipal valuations of
Sydney and Melbourne with those of British cities of
about the same size means anything, present values are
about normal.

Apparently the Victorian land - boom is now
an orphan—at least old O. H. James, the hairy “ father"
thereof, is dead. The circumstances are peculiar.
James went broke for £851,842, and he was crushed
for a dividend of 6Jd. in the £. A further investigation
was proposed, in order to see if any gold had escaped
in the tailings, but, when matters were still in a
rather unfinished state, James’s solicitor turned up to ex-
plain that the old gentleman was fading away, and
“ being one of those men who liked to leave a clean sheet
behind him,” he respectfully applied to have his certificate
handed over without further worry. It was no good ask-
ing a dying man for particulars, and the legal gentleman
thought the document might as well be made out at once.
But the court, after a very large think, postponed the
matter for a fortnight with a view to further information.
Perhaps it had occurred to the court how very foolish it
would look if O. H. James unexpectedly made a complete
recovery soon after the certificate was granted. Or per-
haps it couldn’t see how the certificate could make C. H.
James’s sheet look any cleaner where he was going, so
long as the other 19s. s|d. remained unpaid, and wanted
expert theological advice on the subject. And so poor
old James died uncertificated after all.
« * * *
The Bankers’ Institute in Melbourne is intro-
ducing “ to the trade ” the prospectus of a scheme for
enlarging itself into a Benefit and Assurance Society on
safe lines. The Institute hitherto has been mainly a club,
with a pretty valuable library of banking literature which
possesses few charms for the average member. Under the
new scheme the use of the institute as a club would be but
a minor consideration. The scheme appeals to bank em-
ployes everywhere throughout Australia (they number
6000, at least), by offering them, on easy terms, a means
of providing against the time-honored “ rainy day.” It
doesn’t ask a bigger subscription than the average pocket
can afford, and it is a feasible, business-like scheme, well
deserving support and encouragement from bankers, old
and young. Full particulars are given in the latest issue
of the Bankers’ Magazine, but anyone who wants to be
enthusiastic in the good cause had better communicate at
once with O. H. Davis, the Institute’s secretary.
* • * *
The Brunswick (Melb.) Gas Co. loomed large in
the Wallace family law-case. Old Johnny Wallace, in the
first instance, advanced £40,000 or so on mortgage to the
Brunswick co., and had to take over the business. Wal-
lace has now arranged, or is arranging, the sale of it to a
new co., the new co. to be formed on the strength of a
contract with the Collingwood and Fitzroy Town Coun-
cils. By the terms of said contract the new Brunswick
co. undertakes to supply Fitzroy and Collingwood with
municipal gas at £4O a year less than the Metropolitan
Gas Co.’s tender. The change will entail the disturbance
of 80 miles of footpath. The quality of the Brunswick co.’s
gas is pretty sure to be no better than the Metropolitan
co.’s gas, and the new co. will, perhaps, go to pieces as
the old co. would have gone but for Wallace’s
advance from the Monahan trust funds. In short, the
Fitzroy and Collingwood gas contract is such a very
unwise-looking arrangement that one of the Fitzroy coun-
cillors threw up his billet rather than keep company any
longer with the men who were working for Johnny
Wallace’s scheme. Wherefore the self-retired councillor
is a local hero of the moment. More particularly since it
has become clear that Wallace had urgent reasons for
wishing to get quit of his precious gas co.

Sydney Health-Inspector states that in recently
visiting a certain establishment he found the brands of 24
well-known butter-makers on boxes being filled with mar-
garine ; and the manager of Sydney Fresh Food Co.
alleges that he has known margarine to be shipped under
a well-known brand, as butter, to Victoria. In view of
the fact that the Sydney margarine-factories are turning
out about 14 tons of the stuff per week, it is high time for
official investigation as to what becomes of it. Margarine
may be as wholesome as butter, or more so ; but to put it
on the market branded as butter is simply false pretences.
* * * *
Among the jury empannelled in a recent action
under the Employers Liability Act in Vic. County Court
were a mining investor, a manufacturer, a manager, two
importers, two merchants, and three “ gentlemen.” No
wonder that employers and corporations are anxious to
have Supreme Court actions remitted to this court. The
pretence is that such applications are to save cost, but the
constitution of the average jury-panel shows that it is
really to ensure a verdict. And they generally get it, too.
* • * *
The dodging draper, so rampant in Australia, is
in Germany treated like any other obtainer of money
under false pretences. Anybody professing to sell cheap
by reason of “ expiration of lease,” “ removal,” “ retiring
from business,” and so on, is liable to heavy fine in case of
misrepresentation. Also it is penal falsely to profess to sell
at or under “ cost-price.” A great deal of what, with
us, is called “ business,” is, in Continental countries,
ruthlessly relegated by law to the proper category of
“ robbery.”

“ J.T.”: The inwardness of N.S.W. railway
administration of freight matters is ungraspable by the
casual consignor. Recently, a Sydneyite wished to con-
sign some machinery from Penrith to Bourke. He found
the freight would be, in even figures, £45 ; but, by con-
signing from Darling Harbor, £4O, while freight from Pen-
rith to Darling Harbor was about £2. He consigned to
D.H., and, without untrucking, re-consigned to Bourke,
and saved £2 ss. Therefore the dept, dragged the stuff
34 miles to Sydney, and 34 miles back, and made the con-
signor a present of £2 ss. for doing it. If the railways
charge £42 15s. (including shunting charges) to carry an
article 537 miles, and £45 (without any shunting) to carry
the same article 469 miles, how much will they charge to
land the mathematician who attempts the solution of the
problem at Oallan Park asylum ?
* » • »
The smelter-hands at Block 14 works, Port
Adelaide, have supplied union organisers with the text
for a mighty, moving sermon. These hands didn’t have
any organisation, and when a notice was issued intimating
that the furnaces would be run with fewer men, they
stared at one another, said “ Blank} ,” and wouldn’t “ go
on shift.” So the whole works were laid idle, part of the
mining and mill staff at Broken Hill was knocked off, and
railway traffic arrangements were upset. Three days
afterwards, when a Labor-member and Trades-hall man
or two had rounded up the crowd and talked to them,
they asked for an interview with the directors and
manager, got it at once (the manager said he had been
waiting for them all the time), explanations were made,
and the workmen went back as satisfied as fellows are
likely to be who realise that they have made asses of
themselves and lost three days’ pay into the bargain.
Now a union is being formed.

The 10-head battery at the Beacon Gold Mine,
Bucca Creek, N.S.W., ran continuously for 18 months
without a stop, and has now put through 10,500 tons ore,
and only had one short stoppage for overhaul. This bat-
tery was manufactured and erected by the Austral Otis
Engineering co., of South Melbourne, who are well-
known makers of all kinds of mining and other machin-
ery. They have lately purchased the plant and
stock of the long - established firm of Langlands
Foundry eo., now in liquidation, and, as they
have all the patterns, can supply duplicate parts of any
machines made by Langlands.*
The strike among Block 14’s smelting hands at
Port Adelaide bred uneasiness at Broken Hill, where re-
cent activity on the part of the Mine Managers’ Associa-
tion in calling for wages returns from all departments was
thought to foreshadow a “cut.” But managers deny that
it is anything beyond “ introducing uniformity ’’—though
news as to whether it is a matter of levelling up or down
is awaited with some anxiety.

Moonta and Wallaroo copper-mines, now held
by one co., still employ 2131 men, though of these a
couple of hundred are smelting Broken Hill sulphides.
Moonta’s outturn for last half was 8898 tons (921 men) ;
Wallaroo, 13,963 tons (760 men). Moonta stuff nowadays
takes a lot of getting, and is generally poor. Together,
the ore yielded 3420 tons smelted copper. Go., which has
just spent £75,000 of loan-money on sulphide-smelting
works, hopes to be helped into regular div.-paying again
by that and the improved copper-market. The recovery
in the latter has been excellent—from £37 in 1889, after
the collapse of the French Copper Syndicate, to £52 at
present.

Mr. you’ve been dodging the devil for the
last five years to my knowledge, and yet you can-

Sugar as food :
Experiments were made during the last German
manoeuvres, on the value of sugar as food. Ten men
were subjected to the sugar diet, while another ten re-
ceived exactly the same rations as the rest of the army.
The men chosen for the special diet were weak corporeally.
According to the official report, the weight of the sugar-
men increased during the manoeuvres more than that of
the men on the ordinary ration. “It was proved that
during long marches the feelings of hunger could be kept
down for a longer time by the use of sugar, that the sugar
people suffered less from thirst than the others, and that
a few pieces of sugar sufficed to still thirst for a consider-
able time. It was also observed that symptoms of ex-
haustion or of sunstroke were quickly overcome by a
small ration of sugar.” The authorities now recommend
that sugar be considered a regular article of soldiers’ food,
and that above all, soldiers on the march should have a
supply of it.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
SOCIETY
The story of the first instance known t©>
The Bulletin of a silent tongue marring a man’s;
domestic peace. A correspondent of this paper-
knows a man in Australia who is deaf-and-dumb,,
never learned any system of sign-language, and!
falls back on mimic-gesture to convey his views of
things. As a rule this has sufficed. Enabled him,,
20 years ago, to woo and win a lady as his wife..
Begat a family. Life was roseate as a toper’s;
hose' until 10 years ago, when his wife, probably-
tired of talking to a man who couldn’t hear her,
went off with' one who could. And now the first
man wants a divorce. Everything is fixed to that,
end but the necessary affidavit. That’s where the
thing jibs. His solicitor has had him in hand for
a month, and has worried himself bald over the-
matter. But he has had to give him up, which
must be heartrending, because tire deaf-and-dumb,
man has coin, and a noble bill of costs is thus
sacrificed. They say that Biondi isn’t a circum-
stance to that attorney since he undertook to,
interpret the affidavit to his client, and that his.
facial muscles have so far mastered his willpower
that his grimaces threaten to burst up his own
marital relations. It is a hard case for both lawyer-
send-client. It is particularly hard for the latter,,
because a pretty girl has consented by aid of
Cupid’s lingo to marry him, when he has cleared
the way.

The predominant vanity characteristic of
most criminals frequently, if not generally, leads
to their detection. An Australian “ wanted
A SEARCH FOR THE PICTURESQUE.
ROMANTIC E NGLISHMAN: “Aw! could you—aw—
direct me-aw—to the — aw—Deserted Claim?”
SPAKING SAM : “ The Deserted Claim 'S'trewth!
never even ’card of the darned place! But
you take it from me, the Yellow Dingo’s the
best pub. in the whole township! ”
for what looks, like the very worst form of cold-
blooded murder is a case in point. Some bar-
loafers were vieing with one another in the dis-
play of odd tattoo-marks. The alleged assassin,
who was present, could not resist the temptation
to exhibit his own, though they had been widely
advertised as chiefly identifying him, and was
spotted at once ; now he is in the grip of the
law. One of the most remarkable instances of
this sort occurred in England a good few years
ago. A man named Dench on was wanted for
the murder of a woman. He had got nearly a
day’s start, and the detectives could get no photo-
graph of him. But he was known to bear an ex-
traordinary birth-mark, the minute, but perfect,
profile of a face, quite black, imprinted like a sil-
bouette (pre-natal influence again ?), just under the
clavicle, described as unique by the doctor who
gave the information. After a month’s fruitless
search (the man had shaved off a thick black
beard, completely altering his facial appearance,
it turned out afterwards) a detective one night
chanced to enter a tavern in Hull much frequented
by sailors. The seamen present were discussing
tattooing, and showing a variety of astonishing
designs. As he stood looking on the d. was
tapped on the arm by a solitary sailor who was
sitting by himself near the .door. “Look here,
mister,” said the man, “ I’ve got something
nat’rel, as’ll beat all they chaps can show ’ee.
What d’ye think o’ this for a mark? ” He spread
open his shirt-collar, and there was the nigger’s
head ! In ten minutes he was in the watchhouse.
He was to have shipped the next morning for
Callao, but his vanity hanged him.

Taking forgranted thatDeputy-Supt. Webb
obtains the chief appointment, it will be interest-
ing to note whether N.S. W. Public Service Board
will go ’Ome, as usual, for a new officer for Sydney
Fire Brigade. There are several smart “foremen”
in Sydney, whose experience must be fully as valu-
able as that of anyone who can be drawn from
England at the salary. These were overlooked
when Third-officer Spark was selected a year or so
back, and the discontent sown at that time in the
brigade will become a big weed if the local men
with claims to promotion are again shunted in
favor of an outsider —who cannot possibly know
his Sydney from a fireman’s point of view for 18
months or more.
No humorist could possibly be a Christa-
delphian, and, of all pious bores who ever struck
Australia, lecturer Robert Roberts, whose death
is reported from ’.Frisco, was the most wearisome.
Roberts usually faced an audience of less than a
dozen—two bearded and solemn patriarchs, three
withered old ladies with corkscrew curls, and
several disrespectful small boys. Most of the
small boys filed out in disgust when they found
the entertainment to consist solely of talk ; the
others would fidget and scuffle in ' the back seats
until ordered out in a hilarious bunch by the door-
keeper. But the three withered ladies and the
solemn patriarchs nightly sat for two hours and
listened to a rambling discourse about the Beast,
and the Bull, and the Chariot, and the Arma-
geddon, all of which, seemingly, had some bearing
upon an imminent squabble between the Bear and
the Turk. The present scribe was naturally pleased
to learn that the inevitable Bull would' have its
customary horns in Armageddon.

The Chinese of Sydney, “ Clistian ” and
pagan alike, recently joined in a subscription to
assist in the erection of a Confucian temple in a
village near Canton, over <£6oo being raised. A
Clistian Chow was spoken to by a missionary
about his inconsistency in supporting with his
substance a heathen temple, but John was equal
to the occasion. “Me love Lord ; when you
buildee church me give two pong. Me only give
joss one pong ! Whaffor you sing out, eh ? ”
* * ♦ *
Most of the news which drifts this way now
from faraway Rue Australia, in Lane’s promised
land of Paraguay, is something about woman.
The problem in Rue Australia is mostly about
woman—or the want of her. There isn’t enough
white female to go round, and marriage with the
local brown female is not considered the right
thing, though the brown female isn’t at all bad-
looking sometimes if she is caught while young.
Lane, who spends most of his time in England
now, sent out one or two small shipments of
female for marriage purposes, but there wasn’t
enough to fill the gap, and, besides, a shocked
committee of inspection sent much of the ship-
ment back on the ground that its morals had been
bad to begin with and had got worse. So a good
part of the time of the Rue Australian is spent in
yearnful gazing at the brown girl over her brown
father’s fence, and in anxious waiting for the
white girl-baby to grow up. The most prosperous
Rue Australian now is a person who left the set-
tlement early and started a drink-shop.

“No!” “Where do you work?” “Nosavee.”

Adelaide tobacconists and hairdressers are
in arms at the Govt, proposal to bring them
within the Early Closing Act, and piteously com-
plain that they will be ruined. All the same, the
Bill is favored unanimously by the employes, who
allege that they have now to work over 70 hours a
week, the long Saturday afternoons and evenings
being occupied in shaving the working man who
lias knocked off at one o’clock. “I don’t see my
children in daylight except one day in the week,”
writes one of the individuals who drag your hair
off for a consideration, to an Adelaide paper.
Dear Bulletin.—l was in
Melbourne Picture Gallery on the
day the painting of “Chloe” was
hung. The space opposite the
picture was occupied by half-
circles of chairs, and these again
by society dames and baldheads.
One decrepid old gent, shaking
with palsy and mouthing silently
and horribly, was helped up the
gallery to a seat by a fat, ferocious-
looking dowager in blue goggles,
and there he sat trembling and
winking his wicked, watery old
eyes at the innocent naked girl in
front. Close to me at the far side,
beneath “The Brigands,” were
two young parsons, one gloating
over Chloe through a pair of
opera-glasses! Said his friend,
“ Are you not exceeding youah
allowance ? ” The other "relin-
quished the glasses with a sigh.
They were each having five-minute
pops. And all this disgusting
behaviour over the figure of a
fourteen-year-old girl. And by
such sexual wrecks was “ Chloe ”
condemned as “ indecent! ” Ugh!

The daily-paper bereavement
notices are getting to have quite a
business-like aspect. Latterly it
has become the correct thing to
incidentally thank Dr. Jones and
Nurse Smith for their kind atten-
tion ; probably, in the near future,
the florist will also have a look-in,
and the undertaker will be special-
ised for his sympathetic demean-
our. Also, what has the grave-
digger done that he should be left
in the cold ?
Harry Furniss has an illustrated article on
Sydney Domain in August Windsor Magazine.
One illustration depicts a haunted house ; a second
eight desperadoes most theatrically stalking a
“ Britisher ” and his best girl ; a third spirited
horses careering across the grass, chased by
mounted stockriders in impossible hats; and a
fourth a tree full of large brown bears. After
this, Cook and Sons will probably institute sum-
mer excursions from ’Ome to the Domain.

“Yardstick”: Drapers’ shopmen would
rather serve 20 men than one woman, because,
while men don’t enter a shop unless they really
want to buy, a woman will go in for amusement
and the feeling of superiority due to having a man
waiting upon her. Men are mostly so anxious to
get out that they will take almost anything in
reason, whereas, even when women have got what
they want, they will keep on asking to see more.
The most disagreeable women are the wives of
workingmen who have “risen,” and the hardest
men to deal with are the hen-peeked ; while worse
than either men or women are certain varieties of
parson. They are hard to please, and
their manner is so insufferable that it is
often only fear of the law that saves them
from being flattened out with the carp t-
presser.
Ed. Bulletin. —Re criminal
physiognomy, I know a man in the
Civil Service who was also profes-
sional burglar. He had marked
literary tastes, a wonderfully well-
stored mind, and unexceptionable
manners. Why he was a burglar I
don’t know ; but he was a scientific
criminal, whose skill exempted him
from the risks run by the average
law-breaker. He had been at this
work for three years when the matter
came to my knowledge. Never met a
cooler man. He knew what Napoleon
knew—“ what to do next! ” I can-
not say why he is still a free man,
or how I obtained my information,
but I challenge all-comers to in-
stance an equally surprising
case.—7 x 7.

The Christian Endeavorers, seemingly,
have no Orange tinge in their creed. When they
went excursioning to Melbourne the other day
they were specially recommended, by the com-
mittee, to include St. Patrick’s Cathedral among
their places of call, notwithstanding that Holy
Church exhorts the Faithful to steer clear of
heretical tabernacles. Which suggests that the
Endeavorers make soinewhatfor religious socialism,
and are not so sectarian as they look.

The recent departure for England of a
certain Governor was not unwelcome to his
Premier. The two could never hit it. Trouble
arose originally through the Minister’s wife being
ignored in connection with a big public function,
and the sore occasioned thereby never healed.
His Ex. as a blunderer in matters of etiquette was

A Melbourne young lady with a large con-
nection in the valueless cheque business lately
made one little rise of by a heroic streak of in-
genuity. She called at a suburban post-office,
which was also a stationer’s shop, and asked for
letters, and the stout sympathetic lady behind the
counter handed her a telegram, which the visitor
had carefully sent ahead as bait. That post-
mistress has only a vague recollection of what
followed, for things went with such a'rush that
they took her breath away. The caller opened
the telegram with a smile on her pretty face ; then
FOR ALICE IN AUGUST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Alice sits weeping- in the garden-chair.
A pique—a pout—and all our dear dream-palace
Falls in a rosy ruin : ’tis by malice
Of some sly deity who harbors there !
And as I stand, “ Ah, please to go ! ” the fair
Sentinel lily hears, that bends her chalice
Over the garden-chair and weeping Alice :
Yet going, “ Ah, don’t leave me ! ” thrills the air.
Wondering, I watch a sad cloud hide the sun :
A cloud—a mood—hint for a lover’s part !
I’ll clasp the soft hand by the lily sleeping . .
It clings ! A sudden ray gleams in the dun,
And leaps down gulfs of space to light my heart
Where, in the garden-chair, Alice smiles weeping.
A.G.S.

dock the other da3% and when she got 12 months

court wasn’t taking any cheques.
* * * *
The case of Dr. Orpe«, acquitted, at Auck-
land, of a charge of murder, arising out of an
alleged abortion operation, will be an expensive
muddle for the Maoriland Government. It was a
tangled proceeding from first to last. The girl,
who died, left the usual depositions behind her,
but they were taken by an amateur J.P., called in
at the last moment by another doctor, and the
J.P. didn’t get them in legal form to begin with.
Then they were kept back somehow till Orpen had
left. Orpen, who is old, and frail, and nervous,
claims that he was advised to leave by another
doctor—or scared into leaving, or hustled away
before he had time to collect his ideas. It cost
-£2OOO to capture the old man and bring him back
from California. Then a doctor, who had gone
to England, had to be found and brought out
as a witness. After all this was done, the in-
formality in the depositions suddenly rose up, like
a camel in the early dawn, and confronted every-
body. Finally, it appeared that no one was quite
sure it was an abortion case at all, and everything
faded away in mist, or went to pieces. When the
trial was over, poor old Orpen went down to his
former quarters, to look at the dust which had
accumulated on the ruins of his practice. And
there he probably decided, as a good many other
doctors have done, that when a patient starts by
saying she is an unmarried lady in trouble, about
the best thing to do is to shoot her on the spot.

* * * *
In a Victorian court, the ‘'other day, a
butcher-man was charged with exposing decayed
meat for sale. The meat was placed on view, and
it promptly made a rush for the door and had to
be brought back by the police. Defendant pro-
duced his father as an expert, and the old man
not only gave it as his opinion that the meat was
perfectly wholesome, but offered to eat a sample
of it in public by way of guaranteeing his own
good faith. Probably he didn’t expect to be taken
at his word, but the prosecution sawed off a strip
of the horror, and the ancient ate the gruesome
provender there and then. When he was quite
finished the Bench convicted his butcher-son and
fined him £ 5. If it had convicted him in the
first place, the parent would probably have thrown
his awful hunk of food out of the window, but the
court didn’t want to lose the fun. And, consider-
ing the old man s evidence, the court was quite
right. Every man who gives a certificate of
character to bad meat deserves to eat it —even if
it is dog.

“Melb.”: All the meaning is hardly
realised of the St. Patrick’s requiem mass for the
Austrian empress. Is Roman Catholicism the
State religion ? Here we have the Acting-
Governor, representatives of Parliament, the
military and naval forces, the Melb. corporation,
and everybody attending in an official capacity.
Why not a service, rather, at the Anglican cathe-
dral? Well, not a Catholic would have attended,
but all the Protestants were here. Surely it can-
not be credited that the bulk of those who went,
including Dr. Bevan, believed they were solemnly
assisting at a mummery—a mass for the dead in
purgatory.

The sanitary officials report that nearly
all the fatal Melbourne cases of measles occurred
in poor-tenement localities. When “God saw
everything that He had made, and behold it was
very good ” the landlord did not exist to squeeze
toll out of his fellow-mortal for the privilege of
enduring an existence in a swamp-built tenement.
Some of the members of Vic. House of Land-
lords receive rentals of 3s. per week for the most
miserable dens conceivable.
» * * *
Gilbert Smith, once well-known in Sydney
as a pressman and as Oriental interpreter,
puts his solemn affidavit to a hair - raising
leprosy tale in Perth (W.A.) Sunday Times.
There is no law in Westralia authorising
the arrest and detention of lepers, and no one
attempts to make such a law ; therefore the
patient, if he likes, wanders about loose till he
dies. Gilbert Smith alleges that he personally
knows of twelve lepers who walk the streets of
Perth:—
Some of the men (all Asiatics) were engaged in various
pursuits —market gardening, laundry work, cooking, and
even (last summer) selling ice-cream. ... I often
meet these lepers. One unfortunate wretch in par-
ticular, hiding his hands in his pockets so that
his wretchedness and misery may not be observed,
the skin drawn tight to his leonine features, show-
ing that only a little longer and the end must come.
To that extent has this miserable creature gone, that he
has cut the toes'off Jhis boots so that he might give relief
to the fast vanishing portions of his feet. His country-
men, out of pity, gave him shelter in sheds and other
outhouses, and his disease keeps him away from the
streets at night, so no policeman can arrest him for vag-
rancy. Sometimes, with the few pence he collects, he
visits hotels and indulges in long beers to drown the
misery which he suffers. Is it possible to imagine how
far these visits to hotels, the occasional feeds at coffee
stalls by this poor leper, have helped to implant in some
poor European the germs of this most awful of all
diseases? Truly, do the Orientals say that|God, when
He sent leprosy to man, was only kind enough to
make it a painless death.

“ Y.A.” : Suicide comes
very near to manslaughter
sometimes. Friend of mine
was “ tubbing ” in his bed-
room, when he felt a tug
at his sponge, also he heard
a muffled report. Thisseemed
uncanny, so he got up to in-
vestigate. Saw the water
getting low in his tub, and
found it running out through
a hole in the bottom, and,
when he shifted the tub,
he was able to look through
a hole in the floor! The
bullet that had lodged in
the sponge had come from a
Martini by way of the brain-
pan of the senior partner,
whose room was immedi-
ately below, and whose
affairs were afterwards found
in a very “ kronk ” state.
Luck. The other day a young man at Albert
Park (Vic.) took up a revolver from a friend’s
mantleshelf with which to hammer a tack into the
wall. The revolver was loaded, and went off ;
the ball cut a thin gold ring from the man’s little
finger, tore through his trousers, struck on some
silver he had in his pocket, ricochetted and pierced
the toe of his boot. Result: damage to trousers
and boot, and one broken ring. The man did not
suffer to the extent of a pin-prick. Moral: always
carry some money in your pockets.

Dear B. —Re impure-drug question. I
happen to know that a dispenser at a very piously-
conducted Australian hospital was “ squared ” by
the leading salesman of a wholesale druggist’s firm
to take inferior brands. The firm would not have
tolerated such a thing for a moment if it had
known, but the employe was going for his bonus
on the profits, whilst the dispenser was supple-
menting his miserable pay.—H.G.
* * * *
Melbourne will hardly know itself when it
gets the new Flinders-street railway-station, at a
cost of <£200,000 or thereabouts. Under the
present arrangement of a main entrance at Prince’s
Bridge (where the booking-office is an open shed)
one may get soaked on a wet day, between the
time of taking the ticket and getting into the
train. For passengers, by most lines, there is no
way of escaping the wet. They may drive up to
the station in a cab, but they are bound to brave
the tempest before finding shelter in a railway
carriage. The terminus at Flinders-street and its
shabby brother at Spencer-street have been the
most marvellous features of Smell bourne ever
since that gaudy city started to build beyond its
means. Fink’s lofty edifice, at the corner of
Flinders and Elizabeth-streets, was a strange satire
on the railway-station directly opposite to it.
* * * *
One noticeable feature re the women’s
vote in S.A. is that the attendance of the fair sex
at the Parliamentary debates is no greater now
than it was before they got the franchise. Legis-
lators, however, pay those who do come far more
attention than they used to do, and they are
always escorting a bevy of women over the House
or buying them afternoon tea. The latter item
must be very expensive to some of the city mem-
bers, who, as a general rule, have very little money
to spare.
*-• • •
Inconsistency is the salt of life.
A woman likes a man just as long as she holds
him in perfect subjugation. When he rebels she
loves him.
Many an old maid wishes she were more re-
spected and less respectable.
We can swallow our scruples, but we taste them
again after they have gone.
The selfishness of the garlic-eater is perceptible
in every word he utters.
Better to be born lucky than rich. True happi-
ness is only for the poor man who wins first prize
in Tatt’s sweep.
Many people take the first step in a downward
career when they start to run up bills.
A still tongue never won fair lady, but a faint
heart makes a wise head in most cases'.
Good living makes bad livers.
He who gets intoxicated with flattery has a
swelled head in the mornihg.
Sins are just what one makes of them.
A fellow who sees himself as he is, always thinks
he is looking at somebody else.
Jealousy makes a man hot and a woman cold.
Money is the root of all —plural voting.
To the married couple who think of taking a
lodger—Three’s company, two’s none.

“ Timi ”: In a recent Bulletin par.,
written less than half in earnest, it was stated
that a portion of the city of Auckland stands on
the crust of a great volcanic bubble, and that some
day it may “fall through.” Though the bubble-
story is no doubt a myth, there are circumstances
about the site of Auckland sufficient to cause un-
easiness to the imaginative mind. There are over
80 distinct points of volcanic eruption—extinct, of
course—within a radius of 10 miles of the town,
and the Maori names of some of them—-Rangi-
toto, for instance (meaning “ bloody sky ”) —would
seem to indicate that volcanic activity occurred
within comparatively recent times. Mt. I’ara-
wera, about 140 miles south, and the scene
of the disastrous eruption of ’B6, had no
traditions of previous outbreaks, and was
believed by the Maoris to be so thoroughly
and trustworthily extinct that they interred their
dead chiefs on its summit. Rangitoto, the island
guarding the entrance to the harbor, is therefore
just as likely to resume a career of destructive
activity as to remain quiescent; and, when it does,
the steamer from Sydney four days out will strike

FAIR PARASITE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Her husband? Oh, he died the other day.
She married for his money, and she set
Herself the task to break his heart, they say,
But she is beautiful—and men forget 1
Deep in the shade of a Papuan dell
I chanced upon a flaming- jet of red,
A mighty pillar, hollowed, like the shell
Of some dead tree ; but glowing till it shed
A rosy light into the forest gloom,
A sunny radiance beyond compare.
It was a creeper, flauntingly in bloom,
Surpassingly, unutterably fair.
Unaided it bore up towards the sky ;
The giant stem that felt its first caress
Had disappeared—and when I wondered why,
The breezes seemed to whisper : Murderess !
VIXI!
I have lived and I have loved;
I have waked and I have slept;
I have sung and I have danced ;
I have smiled and I have wept ;•
I have won and wasted treasure ;
I have had my fill of pleasure ;
And all these things were weariness,
And some of them were dreariness ;
And all these things—but two things—
Were emptiness and pain :
And Love —it was the best of them —
And Sleep—worth all the rest of them.
L.S.

An Excellent March.
A rather unique, though very expensive, method
of advertising has been hit upon by the Bile Bean
Manufacturing Co., who have lately issued an ex-
cellent piece of music entitled “The Bile Bean
March.” This production is not, as many might
reasonably suppose, a cheaply-gotten-up, unsightly
piece of music, but is published in a really first-
rate manner. It is printed upon best paper from
clear plates, and is altogether as neat a production
as any piece of four-shilling music on the market.
As to the march itself, suffice to say that it can
hold its own with any we have heard, and is far
superior to a great many. There is a swing and
go to it that is quite entrancing and altogether
peculiar to itself. The Bile Bean Manufacturing
Co. are giving this music away by post, and they
will be only too glad to send a copy to anyone
who will send their name and address to their
head office. The Bile Bean Manufacturing Co.,
39 Pitt-street, Sydney, will find them, and the
only condition is that you mention this paper
when writing, give your full name and address,
and enclose a penny stamp to pay postage.*
CATAPHORESIS
St. James’ Chambers, Kino St-., Sydney
(opp. the Queen’s Statue).
To the Readers of “The Bulletin.
It is with pleasure I inform you that I have
secured a NEWLY-INVENTED PROCESS,
by which Teeth can be prepared for filling without
causing the slightest pain whatever, no matter to
what extent they may be decayed, having already
proved beyond doubt the efficacy of this treatment
on some of our well-known citizens, whose names
can be ascertained if necessary.
That this is an immense boon goes without say-
ing, as by its adoption there is happily no room to
doubt that the Temporary Deadening 1 of Teeth
is possible; and you need not, therefore, be deterred
from having your teeth attended to on the plea of
the pain, which has hitherto been inseparable from
having a tooth filled.
In conclusion, I may add that I do not propose
to charge any extra fee for this treatment.
I have the honor to remain, yours truly,
CHARLES I. HARRIS.
Note.— The various methods adopted for Saving the
Teeth are duly explained in my Little Book, a copv of
which will be sent free of cost upon application.
