A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October 4, 1898.
My Deae Myee—
Manly Wild Flower Show wasn’t the expansive
festivity of former years. Time was when the
show was looked forward to for weeks beforehand,
and drapers and milliners used its name for a bait,
as they use Randwick or the Cup. This year one
little hall held everything, and ten minutes put
the whole show away. Cardinal Moran, in a red
frock, rather dulled the colors of the wild flowers
at the opening ceremony last week ; Mrs. Prender-
gast’s pale-green toilette melted more har-
moniously into the background, and Mrs. Toohey,
in periwinkle-blue, was another conspicuous
helper. Canaries and wild flowers were the chief
products. The patience that converts prickly,
unyielding heaths and wooden waratahs into
sheets of flat decoration cannot be too highly com-
mended, but a wild flower show isn’t a kindness to
Australian flora. Our flowers are fairly happy
with a setting of native scrub and a background
of old grey gums, but they refuse to shine in arti-
ficial schemes.
Another churchy wind-raiser was the Gaft
Chantant lately held at St. Mary’s. Everything,
even the bishops themselves, point to the near dis
solution of the church’s dream, but Cafe Chantant
is still a long way from most clerics’ imagination.
Imagine Primitive Methodists branding their bun-
struggles with a name that calls up memories of
gay Paree ! By the way, the Sydney Diocesan
Association gives its annual musical festival at St.
Andrew’s Cathedral, Oct. 20, when all the Angli-
can choirs around the city will lift up their voices
in unison, led on by Montague Younger and Mr.
R. B. Parry.
Mrs. Waterhouse, of Waverley, where shells are
not unknown to fame, has been lending her house
to yet another charity, the Children’s Mission
Home. Shells on the beach are one thing, and
massed together in family parties under glass-
covers are quite another story, and a visit to this
collection is as much a charity to the visitor as t«
the Mission children. The finest group of cowries
in N.S.W. live together in one case. Then there
is the strange Voluta Musica, a little West Indian
shell scored all over with bars of music. Rare
butterflies and island curios are also on view, and
nice boys take you round and explain until you
swell with knowledge.
George Moore, in his new book, “ Evelyn
Innes, ” casts off a few disparaging remarks about
Gounod’s “Faust,” the Faust of the people, and
insinuates that it lives only by its Margaret.
Berlioz’s “Faust,” written as far back as 1866, is
coming up in popular favor, our own Philharmonic
Society giving two admirable renderings of it last
week. Certainly Faust, in the hands of Mr.
Weir, was a young man who might have lived and
died —as any other young man might die —on
lemonade. And Miss Edith King, as Margaret,
didn’t get beyond her brilliant vocalisation. And
the gentleman who sang Mephisto. was encum-
bered with a large, rubicund countenance that
mocked at the epithet “ pale-visaged, ” and a fine,
big voice that ran off the lines at intervals.
But the background of chorus and orchestra was
carefully managed, the instruments playing up
as if they knew they were working for Berlioz, the
master who gave them their first big chance.
“The Ballet of the Sylphs,” with Signor Truda
at the harp, waked all kinds of exquisite airy
phantasms, and the chorus that preceded was a
delicately-balanced matter wherein dreams and fire
and romance mingled fascinatingly. “The Hun-
garian March ” was encored, of course, and another
fine orchestral effect was the “ Will-o’-Wisps ”
minuet, with the orchestra calling up all manner
of ghouls, and gnomes, and night-birds to shriek
in musical vagaries.
More modern in its music was the “Willow
Pattern Plate,” a little operetta by A. C. Goring-
Thomas and W. B. Beattie, of this city. The
Society of Artists’ rooms were turned into a
theatre for one night only last week, and a bulky
crowd struggled for seats, or a bit of wall,
or anything to support them through an amateur
production presented by amateurs. As far as the
operetta went, the amateur element was scarcely
visible. Mr. Beattie’s words snapped happily
round the cleverly-arranged yarn of “ The Willow
Pattern Plate,” and Mr. Goring-Thomas’ slight,
but telling, music meshed the Japanese atmos-
phere into its melodies. Mrs. Fleming, a Willow
Waly who died for love and changed into a white
fowl, was a yum-yummy Jap. study of the right
calibre, but the three six-foot maidens with swan
necks somehow disturbed the symmetry of tbe
Japanese scheme. George Lambert’s brush made
some fine mountain scenery for the background,
and thunder and lightning were laid on with quite
a professional ease.
Mrs. Bland Holt’s ‘‘ at home ” brought another
big crowd to the rooms next day. After all the
“sit-up” parties of the season an afternoon in
Bohemia was as good as a flask of ozone. Miss
Ross, Miss Watson and Miss Fitzmaurice Gill
helped to make things cheerful, all the long hairs
in the city drank tea in unity, and the outer world
revelled in the light of the profession’s smallest
smiles. It was a treat to go to a party where the
hostesses didn’t think they ought to look bored.
Songs and music went on all the afternoon.
Frances Ross, in a highly-becoming toque, gave a
little Scotch recitation, Madame Leon Caron
carolled some of her husband’s music, and numbers
of professional musicians made up the rest of the
programme. Miss Elizabeth Watson, in cloud-
grey and daffodils, looked like a replica of hand-
some Henrietta, and revealed a much more charm-
ing phiz than her stage make-up allows her.
Breezy Mrs. Bland scattered smiles and pro-
Erammes in a floral silk frock, and kissed every-
ody’s babies between whiles. Miss Fitzmaurice
Gill was tailor-clad and a beautiful theatrical
with a carven profile wore wicked magenta with a
calmness anyone might envy.
Hetty Holroyd’s concert on Eight-hours’ Day
was not favored with an overcrowded house.
Eight-hour-holiday makers generally like to finish
up their trip round “our beautiful” with some-
thing “rorty,” and a concert falls a long way
short of the desired animation. Miss Hol-
royd, gowned in white satin and green
flowers, shook glorious shakes into the
echoing hall, and followed Ernani with
the “ Harp that Once.’ But surely a youthful
singer who has not quite arrived at the prima-
donna period might appear a little earlier on the
programme. It’s all very well for European stars
to work up the agony and keep the audience wait-
ing till the concert is half over, but it isn’t an
advisable line for budding pros, to follow. Edgar
Straus, with thin white face and Gottschalk’s
“Romance,” snared the biggest applause of the
evening, and Mr. Burns Walker sang “ Once ”
with just the feeling that the Once genus de-
mands.
That pleasant, precocious little girl, Lady Mar-
garet Villiers, who stunned Sydney society by
hopping gaily round the Vice-regal balls with her
hair in a pig-tail, is going to marry Hon. Walter
Rice, eldest son of Lord Dynevor. The same
Lady Margaret was a nasty pill for the people
who prided themselves oil keeping all the dear old
English conventions, and sent their own rosebuds
off to bed at eight every night. The baffling part
of the matter was that nobody was quite sure
whether Lady J. was merely countenancing a new
custom or whether she reckoned Sydney society
wouldn’t know any better.
Ever since Brenda Gibson went to England,
rumors of matrimonial engagements have been
wafted to Australian shores. Now all reports have
been condensed into the direct statement of her
marriage with a high-class English actor. The
separation of Brenda from the co, set at rest, it is
said, a lot of internal jealousy. Miss Romer
(mother of Bob Brough and of Brenda) used to be
very annoyed at the “fat ” parts being allotted to
her daughter-in-law, Emma Temple, and much
heart-burning on both sides resulted.
Down at the Quay, when the ferry boats come
in, lively doings go on at the doors of our ’buses.
Nowadays many ’buses have their own boy, and
every boy works all he knows to get people into
his vehicle, and to keep them out of any
other. _ Some of them merely crook their first
fingers into your face, and beckon furiously with
yelping invitations, but the really earnest ’bus-boy
will follow you right into Pitt-street, yelling
“’ere’s the ’bus you wants ; puts you down at the
door, and starts ’fore all others !.” I saw an old
lady endure much mental perturbation from three
of these rival parasites the other day. One danced
in front of her and tried to get her out to Waverley;
one caught her by the arm and dragged her towards
Glebe Point; one tugged at her parasol and en-
deavored to lead her by the handle into the wiles
of Waterloo. And she didn’t want a ’bus at all;
was merely trying to get a letter into a pillar-box.
But the ’buses that don’t keep boys are just as
larky. Some of the drivers play little games of their
own ; can’t count, can’t understand what you say
when you pass up sixpence, and ask for fourpence
instead of twopence change. At night they art-
fully prolong these little misunderstandings until
the theatres are reached, and people have to get
out and give up the struggle for their change. In
a crowded ’bus a little confusion is only natural,
but when there is only one passenger inside, and
that one has three fares stopped out of his coin,
and can’t make the driver understand, the con-
fusion assumes the aspect of the good old Heathen
Chinee biz.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ That charity concert in St. Kilda Town Hall
(Monday, Sept. 26th) drew a big audience, thanks
to the comedietta essayed by society amateurs.
The name of this drawing - room drama was
‘ Who’s to win him ? ’ —said ‘him ’ being an eligible
bachelor driven to bay by a quartette of charming
husband-hunters, personated respectively by Mrs.
Vallange, sisters Daisie and Leslie Madden, and
their bosom friend, Miss Eily O’Hara. Mrs. Val-
lange, as the horsey sister with hair coiled low
under a hard-hitter, skirt pinned up in front over
smart riding-boots, and whip in hand to flog her
habit in pauses of conversation, did not look her
best in these severe togs. Like her sisters, the
blooming buxom bride scores heavily in decollet-
age, showing plenty of plump white neck and
arm, or at a Govt. House garden-party, sitting
about in spring-like muslin and flowery hat.
_ “ The Madden girls, as the daughters of a prac-
tising barrister —for such was pa in their growing-
up days—were not brought up from the cradle to
ride, shoot, and hunt like the Countess of Hope-
toun (say) or, to come nearer home, the womankind
of the Cainperdown and Werribee clans. This ac-
counts perhaps for a certain lack of dash and
aplomb, without which a belle does better to leave
sporting cuts and colors alone. All the same, the
Acting - Governor’s girls are gifted charmers
enough. In the course of her stage man-hunt the
married one carolled ‘We’ll all go a-hunting to-
day ’ tunefully as a thrush. Little sister Leslie,
as Minuetta, a light-heeled schemer, tried to bring
down her shy bird by means of a skirt-dance. The
full-flounced skirt of her pink-muslin afternoon-
frock trailed on the ground in accordance with
prevalent methods, but the dancer didn’t hide her
satin shoes under its bushel, any more than her
gauzy under -skirts or filmy nether-frills. Judging
by that song of hers on the gullibility of men,
Eeggy Pryde probably would have pinned her
faith to Minuetta in the matrimonial sweep-
stakes. You remember her recipe for befooling
the soft ’uns: ‘ A nice little face, a neat little
figure, and a dainty bit of lace.’
“Bridegroom Yallange worked hard at a carica-
ture of an elderly lady-killer. Baronet Clarke’s
young step-brother was an inoffensive Squire with
a quiverful of marriageable daughters ; and pretty
Miss Ruby Madden, with pink re vers to her blue-
satin pelisse and blush-roses in her hair, arrived
under the careful escort of Colonel Campbell to
supply the comedietta of music.
“Also, the programme included two gruesome
recitations by girl elocutionists in virginal white.
The first of these parlor tragediennes led off with
‘ Clarence’s Dream. ’ The second followed suit
with a dismal tale of a patriot who, for the sake of
his country, saw his mother, sister, and sweetheart
butchered in cold blood, though a word from him
would have save them. His fiancee died hard too,
reproaching and cursing her young man, who saw
the thing out, and then was led off to the mad-
house.
“Tuesday night (Sept. 27th) conservatorium
students gave a concert in Melb. Town Hall in aid
of Children’s Hospital. Marshall Hall seemed to
jib slightly at the thought of his first plunge, since
the row, into the presence of a possibly disapprov-
ing assembly. Anyway, he was slow to show up
and worked off his preliminary excitement in warm
despatches to any door-keeper who dared to admit
late-comers during the item. One item lasted
20 minutes, and on a squally night that vestibule
is like the cave of winds. When at last the direc-
tor followed his worn, wan nose (he’s much thinner)
on to the platform, he came in for quite an ova-
tion. Even Mr. and Mrs. Acting-Governor pam-
pered him a bit. You see the concert was in
response to Lady Madden’s appeal, so in proof
presumably of gratitude and confidence she
brought along the youngest of her grown-up
daughters in an overall of snowy satin and fur.
Mamma herself was in black with a turquoise
necklet, a throatlet of pearls, and a blue-mounted
osprey in her hair. Janet Lady Clarke was on
view, too, in crisp crepe and speckless cuffs and
collar.
‘ ‘ Most of the girl performers wore white, and
all looked more or less attractive. Belles of the
occasion were winsome Olga Woinarski, bound for
Berlin to carry on her violin training, and Zelma
Wittowski, fiddling gracefully with pink roses in
her high-piled hair. They all smile so sweetly at
their director, these students, and he takes it with
the calmness of a prophet who knows his disciples
would rather have a kick from him than a kiss
from anyone else. Among the fiddlers that night-
a gentleman with curls hanging down his collar
was pointed out as ‘Max Dietrich,’ the subject of
one of those famous poems. People who don’t get
their hair cut regularly always seem to appeal
more strongly to trie artistic nature.
“The ‘"Under the Red Robe’ dressing is just
delightful. In the first scene, gallant after gallant
swaggers into the gaming-house until quite a con-
vincing crowd are on view. And such peacocks as
they are ! White satin small-clothes, laced and
corded with gold, plumed velvet hats, flowing
love-locks. And when their coats come off in pre-
paration for duels, their nainsook shirts are puffed,
shirred, and ruffled like one’s very best white mus-
lin blouses. It’s a mercy men don’t dress like
that nowadays. Matrimonial warfare would
increase a hundredfold if they did. Picture three
people trying to heat their curling-tongs at the
same candle ! And instead of merely darning and
sewing on buttons for hubby, a girl would always
be tacking in fresh tuckers and wristlets, and fix-
ing his back-hair for him, and re-curling his
feathers. She’d have no time to look after her
own things, and would have to settle down into a
drab little hen-bird on pairing-day.
“For that matter, the conjugal curling-tongs
difficulty is, perhaps, commoner nowadays than is
generally supposed. If the Acting-Governor’s
back-hair crimps of its own accord, I’m a booby!
“To return to the stage. Ada Ferrar’s first
appearance is in a sweeping gown of oyster-white
satin with chestnut coiffure of chignon and side
curls. She looks her best when she flings on an
imposing green cloak, lined with cinnamon-brown
and tied with green velvet cords and tassels which
brush her insteps. One red rose in her white satin
corsage, a plumed hat, and she is for the moment
a veritable Queen of Love and Beauty. Mrs.
Maesmore Morris wears a pink redingote over
white satin under-robe brocaded with pink, and a
small white matron’s cap. She looks very lovely,
willowy, golden-haired, and, of course, her features
are faultless. But as yet she’s not too graceful on
the boards. There’s something lolloping and flop-
ping in her gait as if she were due for a good old
loaf and lie-down. Gaston Mervale all in scarlet,
as Richelieu, wears a funny little white lace top-
skirt not under but over the red robe.
“Mrs. Besant had made all arrangements to
come to Australia to lecture on Theosophy early
next year, but wrote by a recent mail that, through
unforeseen circumstances, she would be obliged to
return to London from India, where she is now.
Also —this with reference to some rumors current
in a contrary direction—she appears to be per-
fectly satisfied with Theosophy in every way. The
long illness of her daughter, Mrs. Besant-Scott,
who is located at Melbourne, was probably a factor
in inducing her to come so far, but her intention
was to lecture on Theosophical subjects solely.
While in London, R. S. Smythe called on her, by
her request, to arrange for a second Australian
tour, but, owing to her refusal to lecture on any
but Theosophical subjects, he declined to under-
take the risk, feeling sure that Australia was not
sufficiently interested in Theosophy to make the
tour a financial success.
“Why,” said a lady University student, dis-
cussing a Professor, ‘‘ he actually told me one day
about his relations with his own wife. ” Chorus of
ladies: “Did he? did he? What did he say?
Tell us ! ” “ Oh, he said her knowledge of fugues
was shocking, and her harmony weak, and he said
there could be no true love where there was no
counterpoint. ” “Oh ! ”
Agar Wynne, M.L.C., in Yic. Legislative Coun
oil, when discussing the Women’s Franchise Bill,
said that when he read “The Sorrows of Satan”
he found it was written in such a masculine strain
that he was surprised to learn that the writer was
a woman. He did not know any man who could
write a more masculine work. The intellectual
quality of a legislative body where Marie Corelli
is accepted as a classic is rather hard to charac-
terise. The shrill and blithering Marie as a “ mas-
culine ” writer, too! A few cranky people have
talked of forming an Omar Khayyam Society.
Better make it an Omarie Corellyyam Society, with
Hon. Agar Wynne, M.L.C., as first president.
“Frances Tyrrell Gill (who has mightily im-
proved in her platform manner) had the bad luck
to fall over a box at South Yarra railway-station,
a few nights ago, and the bruised bardess is only
slowly recovering from the shock. She hopes to
be able to give her third lecture, subject, ‘ Women
Writers of the Century,’ on Oct. 6.
, “ The Trinity College warden (Dr. Deeper) pats
himself on the back, in his annual report, re the
students’ Greek play-acting at Melb. Town Hall.
‘ The year has been made specially memorable by
a performance of the “ Alcestis of Euripides.” It
has helped to carry the fame of the college to dis-
tant parts. Some of the leading English journals
have referred to the event in complimentary terms,
one especially noticing as an interesting fact that
this was probably the first occasion when a Uni-
versity college had been able to supply both the
men and women performers in a representation of
the kind from among its own students.’ Dr.
Leeper’s subservience to Bishop Goe and kindred
‘ goody-goodies ’ of the Trin. Col. directorate is
shown in the further interesting fact that he says
nothing about Marshall Hall’s musical adaptation
of ‘Alcestis.’ It were treasonable to mention the
‘ impious ’ author of ‘ Hymns Ancient and Modern ’
who converted the ancient tragedy into a grand
opera.
“A beautiful story of the Gearl of Yarmouth’s
life in Australia has been published by a London
newspaper. His lordship, it seems, came to this
rather dweadful country to make his fortune, but
at first he found it difficult to get to work, owing
to the hospitalities that were pressed upon him.
However, at last, he forced his noble way to the
sultry North, and went in for the cultivation of
mangoes, likewise other fruits of industry. He
used to gather the mangoes with his own fair
hands and pack them himself. Sometimes he was
‘ entirely alone, ’ and then, no doubt, his thoughts
used to wander to dear old Hingland, and the
House of Lords, and all sorts of lofty subjects. He
lived a busy, yet meditative life among the man-
goes until a third attack of fever gave him pause,
and finally persuaded him to transfer his valuable
services back to his native land. It is quite an
affecting story, especially as regards his Australian
struggles to get away from the people who pressed
their attentions upon him. And yet ‘ Yarmouth ’
didn’t wear a very worried appearance at the time
he was flinging his slender legs around the Bijou
stage. As a skirt-dancer, he seemed quite happy,
and there was never a note of sadness in his voice
except when he sang ‘Knocked ’em in the Old
Kent Road.’ He was thought to be enjoying him-
self. Nothing visibly troubled the noble Earl, not
even the Bulletin’s theatrical notice of his first
performance. He skipped into the manager’s
room, saying gleefully, ‘O, I say, oldfellar, doesn’t
this heartless johnnie give it to me.’
“There is much talk among Vic. politicians
about the snub administered by Duncan Gillies to
Acting-Governor Madden at the railway servants’
banquet. The A.-G., wishing to be genial, allowed
his fluent tongue to run away with him. And as
reference had been made to the claims of the rail-
way men against the Government, he expressed
the hope that they would get all they asked for,
and said they deserved even more. Whereupon
Gillies rather nastily said, when he spoke, that it
was easy for some people to ‘ ‘ play to the gallery ”
when they had no responsibility to bear. Turner
said very much the same thing in a nicer fashion,
but folks agree that Chief Justice Madden de-
served the snub. He has not acquired the guber-
natorial way (Hopetoun had it to perfection) of
saying nothing at all gracefully.
"Vours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Queensland’s first Premier —Herbert
still lives. Five others of her ex-Premiers are to
the fore, working or resting; five—Byrnes the
last—are dead.
The famous Duffy Land Bill came into
operation September, 1862. Dennistoun Wood,
now of Tas., is the only one of Duffy s colleagues
alive, but Sir Henry Barkly, the Governor of the
day, is still extant.
Sydney University has had seven Chan-
cellors since its foundation in 1851. _ Excluding
the present office-holder, Dr. M‘Laurin, only one
—Dr. Nicholson —survives. The medical baronet
was the second Chancellor.
The speculative Captain Wallington, well
known in Govt. House circles all round Australia,
is seriously tipped for private secretary to the
Gov. General of Federated Australia. Federation
must indeed loom near when the spot on which to
seat Captain Wallington can be descried.
Charles Pinhey, of the Treasury, Sydney,
and later on N.S.W. Registrar-General, but for
some time in England, has been appointed
secretary to the N.S.W. Agent-General in Lon-
don. Pinhey, an able man, is son of the old and
much respected Sydney coroner of that name.
The memorable M‘Sharry (N.S.W.) rail-
way-contract arbitration case closed May 13. The
arbitrator’s award may now see light any day—five
months after close of case. But there were 12,000
typed p.p. to be read. The case began 26th July,
1896, two years and nearly three months ago.
In a hut on the outskirts of the Murchison
(W.A.), the other day, they found the dead body
of “ Count ” O’Regan—an identity of many Aus-
tralian mining camps in their earliest days. The
“Count,” who had never lost the polish acquired
somewhere or other, died as mysteriously as he
had always lived.
Dicken, secretary to Agent-General Tozer
in London, and himself a candidate for the Agency,
was a N. Q’sland P.M. in the early days, and for
some time a goldfields warden. In the latter
capacity he was a terror to the Chinese. His
salary as London secretary is <£7oo, with a Tozer
promise of early increase.
Mr. Rennie (aged 78) may be too old to
continue as N.S.W. Auditor-General; but, if so,
he is certainly too old to take a Public Service
Commissionership. For how could the Board,
presided over by an octogenarian, justify its dis-
missal of the sexagenarians in the service, many
of whom are really in the prime of life ?
Rabbi Davis, in a sermon in Sydney Syna-
gogue, illustrated “the fleeting character of this
earthly existence ” by the demise of ex-legislator
S. A. Joseph, whose funeral had taken place that
day. Yet Mr. Joseph was 74 years of age, was
member for West Sydney 34 years ago, and was
recognised throughout the community as a shrewd
business man for nearly 50 years.
Hori Grey’s principal legatee will probably
prove to be his nieoe, Mrs. Seymour George, who
was long his constant companion. Her husband
was for years a member of M.L. Parliament, but
his sole claim to fame was the ability with which
he acted as chairman of the Parliamentary Re-
freshments Committee. A year or two back he
made a big scoop on Auckland Stock Exchange.
“ Yang Anyut ” : About that Q. Govt.
Geologist (“ B.” 17/9/’9B) whom I knew well in
his pre-Australian days, it is related that so often
did he leave the key of his portmanteau behind
that on one occasion his travelling companion
fastened the key round the geologist’s neck with
a string. At the end of the journey, when dinner
was half over and the man of mines had not
appeared, his friend went to investigate and was
relieved to behold the key still round Skertchly’s
neok. “ Oh, the key’s all right,” said the pebbly
one, “ but why in the name of Geikie didti t you
hang the blessed portmanteau round my neck ?
Then I should have stood a chance of having it
with me now.”
Major De Winton, who has just died in
England, was the author of a recent book,
“Soldiering Fifty Years Ago.” He came to
Hobart Town as Ensign in charge of a convict
guard in 1843. With his regiment (99th) he
served also at Parramatta, Windsor, Newcastle,
Port Macquarie, Brisbane, and Norfolk Island.
In “Fifty Years Ago” he mentions having met a
young attorney’s clerk who had journeyed from
Sydney to the coal city to defend a case, repre-
senting “ Bob ” Nicholls. The commandant was
the presiding justice, and after court invited the
young clerk to mess, a joyous evening being spent.
“ The young clerk was James Martin, afterwards
N.S.W. Chief Justice.”
Editor Bulletin. —You tell of “ Baldy ”
Davis, a member of your (N.S.W.) Parliament,
who has 14 sons, who all want to join the Austra-
lian Horse (cavalry regt.). We of Yankeeland
can always cap your stories. For instance, at the
interment of the Mormon elder Taylor, buried at
Salt Lake, Utah, a few years ago, there assembled
forty (40) stalwart sons of deceased, not one of
them under sft. 9Jin. (nine were over 6ft. lin.), of
the average weight of 156J1b. I was there and
saw them weighed, and can show you a photo, of
the group, taken at the grave-side, with my own
good self in the background.— Over Thar. [Send
along photo. Also, Baldy Davis is a decent, one-
wife’d man and not a Mormon.—Ed. B.]
Captain Lockyer, who died suddenly at
Port Adelaide just after the close of a dangerous
and difficult voyage with the disabled steamer
Adelaide, whose construction he supervised in
England 16 years ago, was one of the best-known
and most popular skippers on the Australian
coast between Sydney and Fremantle. He had
been in the service of Adelaide S.S. Co. ever since
its foundation 25 years ago, and during that time
he carried nearly half-a-million passengers, not one
of whom sustained the slightest injury of any kind
while under his protection. The worst accident
that ever happened to any of his ships was the
loss of a propeller or the smashing of a rudder.
Both these mishaps befel the Adelaide during the
last voyage he made on her.
There was a time when Hori Grey’s praise
and blame made or marred politicians. The popu-
larity of Joseph Augustus Tole, a young Maori-
land graduate of Sydney University who after-
wards became Minister for Justice, dated from the
occasion when Grey alluded to him as “my
talented young friend.” But when Tole took
office against Grey’s wishes in the Stout-
Vogel Ministry the old man wrung his hands with
a magnificent simulation of despair and said,
heart-brokenly, “ Poor Tole ! ” “ Poor Tole ”
stuck, and the star of Joseph Augustus waned.
Saturday Review has never heard of
Henry Kendall as a poet. In a scorching review
of Mrs. Fisher’s alleged poems it quotes her
egregious line : “ Lindsay Gordon, sweet Kendall
and Stella,” and comments thus : “If the sweet
Kendall of these extraordinary lines be the chaste
matron of the English stage we cannot think she
will be edified by any such company. And, in
any case, if Australia continues to produce such
verses as these we are determined to oppose Im-
perial Federation with our last breath.” Truly
Mrs. Fisher is awful—but Australia doesn’t make
her its official poet as England does with the un-
speakable Alfred Austin.
A Melb. weekly states that John A. Wal-
lace, M.L.C., settled =£40,000 on his present wife,
the niece of Premier Reid. People who ought to
know have said that the lady refused a settlement
(the explanation being that the money wasn’t
“ settled ” but just handed over) ; anyhow, if any
aspect of the <£40,000 story is true it may explain
trustee Wallace’s readiness (when cross-examined
re the Monahan estate) in asserting that he had
willed all his property to his children. Otherwise
he might have paused at the thought of how his
children’s stepmother would regard the arrange-
ment.
Be Johnny Wallace, M.L.C., and his
jumper. When “old John Bull Barton,” the
Little Collins-street “ kitchen-doctor,” kept the
hotel at corner of William-street and Chancery-
lane, his midday meal was a feature of the es-
tablishment, and very particular the “ Doctor ”
was as to his customers. Mr. Wallace came in
with a crowd from Goldsbrough’s, and when Bar-
ton objected to the “ jumper,” it was explained
that Mr. Wallace was somebody. The kitchen-
doctor allowed it “to pass for once ” on condition
that the wearer should occupy a seat beyond the
range of vision of most of the other diners.
Wallace had his dinner, but never paid Barton’s a
second visit.
More news about the wondrous Louis de
Rougemont, or the man who is identified as that
great romancer, keeps dropping in. The latest
assertion is that he was once a rouseabout in a
Sydney 6d. restaurant —this was before he took to
photography ; and that somewhere about ’B2 he
started for the far North to go pearl-fishing but
speedily came back disgusted. “ The last timejhe
was seen about Sydney he was in Enmore carrying
a baby.” Carrying a baby in Enmore reads like
themostplacidand commonplace existence possible
—especially as Rougemont ought at that time to
have been bossing his cannibal tribe in the north-
ern deserts and eating his victuals off plates of
virgin gold.

that the Woman’s Suffrage Bill was only asked
for by extremists and by women of improper char-

That once-bright intellect, A. G. Taylor—
“ The Giraffe” of N.S.W. Press and N.S.W.
Assembly— is now, sad to relate, suffering from
general paralysis, and is confined in Callan Park
Asylum. Taylor’s intellectual decadence ac-
counted for the hideous inaccuracy of his furious
•anti-Federal articles in the Australian Star.
Rut it didn’t account for the Star’s conduct for
publishing them.
Another Parkes yarn, told by a pressman.
For some time it was my duty to “ do ” the Sydney
Govt, offices. Parkes was in power, and I had to
see the old man every day. And, to do him
common justice, he never put on “side.” When-
ever I sent him a message, no matter how he was
engaged, he would come into that little
library where the clock with the joybells is,
and see me, and tell me what I wanted to
know. 1 loved him for his loyalty to press-
men. ‘ I’m one of your own
noble army of martyrs,” said he
at Alfred Bennett’s jubilee din-
ner to the press in ’B7. Well, one
day, when he was not in a
hurry, Parkes told me this
yarn, with great glee : A Syd-
ney “city” man picked up a
poor little waif somewhere, and
wanted to set him up. He
showed him to Parkes, who be-
came interested in the young-
ster and said he could put him
into a nice home in the after-
noon. The youngster was put
into the room of O’Loughlin,
the messenger, and when the
clerks and flunkeys heard of the
Premier’s protege they came in
scores to see and talk to him.
Later on, old Parkes said to the
boy, “ Well, my little man, how
would you like to live always
in this big place?” “ I wouldn’t
like it at all,” answered the lad.
“ But why?” “ Because,”
says he, “ there’s too many fa-
thers here.”
An officer in N. S. W.
roads - department, who had
been toiling out-back, sent in
an application to the late roads-
lissioner Bennett, asking for a
month’s leave in order that he might be
married. In pathetic terms he set forth his
claim to the holiday asked for,, and wound up by
stating that he had had nothing to eat but bandi-
coot, ’possum, and snake for the preceding three
weeks. Bennett’s reply was : “ Mr. Blank may
have the leave he applies for, but I really think
that he might have provided for more nutritious
diet on the eve of so auspicious an occasion.”

Poor “ Tommy ” Walker, ex-word-painter
of N.S.W. Assembly, escaped from his crushing
defeat at Broken Hill to Westralia, and does

Perth possesses a curious quartette —C. E. Jones,
in his day one of the most fluent and forceful of
Vic. democratic M.’sP. ; Rowland Rees, ditto of
S.A. ; Zillman, of Queensland, though he was
not so deeply in politics ; and the sadly-afflicted
Walkei, And all, bar Reeves, now strive to eke
out a living as public lecturers.
Hori Grey provoked more than one laugh
at the expense of Vincent Pyke, another veteran
of M.L. politics. The man from Tuapeka was
once grumbling in the Parliamentary refreshment-
room about his advancing years, and quenching a
powerful thirst meanwhile. “ You know, I have
gone through a lot in my time, Sir George,”
quoth Pyke. Hori gave a meaningful glance at
the other veteran’s empty glass, and quavered :
“ It isn’t what you have gone through, Pyke, so
much as what has gone through you.”
An Australian ecclesiastical storyette. He
was an untiring member of a preaching order, and
so long-winded in the pulpit that the superior in-
sisted on a discontinuance at the ringing of a
small bell. One evening, in full tide on “ Effects
of Mortal Sin,” he had just enunciated : “ And
all who die in the state of mortal sin shall be
damned for ever and ever ” —when the bell rang.
Out went his hand over the congregation with the
usual concluding benediction —“ A blessing which
I wish you all in the name of the Father, Son and
Holy Ghost. Amen.”
There was a lot of coincidence or fate or
something in the case of John Dickson, who lately
suicided in Melbourne. Seventeen years ago he
killed his “ housekeeper ” with an iron bar. Tried
for murder, he dodged the gallows by the skin of
his teeth—escaping with 15 years. After release
he lived quietly until three weeks ago, when the
ghost of the defrauded Ketch apparently worked
on his feelings so much that he attempted to
remedy the omission. He was cut down and
resuscitated, but seems to have concluded that he
had not even then acted quite squarely in the
matter, so the other Saturday he again took the
matter in his own hands, and this time suspended
himself in workmanlike fashion. The only moral
visible is that if a man is born to be hanged he
shouldn’t waste time dodging the hangman.
Little Gagliardi, Melb. Public Librarian,
who died a few weeks ago, got to Australia in a
curious way. He never intended to settle here,
but started to the Southern hemisphere in search
of a brother. The latter, at 15 years of age, ran
away from home and fought for Garibaldi. Then
he started to travel, and whenever he wanted
money he wrote to his father. The letter was
always from a different place, and the ‘ ‘ old man ”
had a map of the world marked with red dots,
showing the movements of the ad venturous young-
ster. At last he got to Maoriland, and Fer-
nando, his brother, was despatched to arrest him.
The doctor met him on the West Coast of Maori-
land in ’76. Gne of Ferd.’s fellow-passengers was
Baracchi, the present Vic. Govt. Astronomer. The
lost brother is now a flourishing importer in
Sydney.
The history of Abbey Altson’s “ The
Golden Age ” (which he painted for Vic. Art
Gallery in fulfilment of the Travelling Scholar-
ship obligation) is given in Peabson’s Magazine.
Young Altson, like Longstaff before him, was
terribly anxious to do justice to the Scholarship.
No subject seemed big enough for him. Finally,
he decided upon a creation of Arcadia, a beauti-
fully-yellow and impossible place where the girls
are happy all day long and wear no clothes.
Altson, being in France at this time, thought that
the lie de Noirmontier was the best place lie
could strike for Arcadian atmosphere and senti-
ment. So he took some girl models with him and
filled himself with the spirit of the island, and put
such a lot of time and expense into the picture
that when it was finished he was a “dead
broker.”
The long-neglected grave of Bishop Sawyer
in Grafton cemetery is about to receive a memorial
brass, the tribute now being on its way from
friends in England. Bishop Sawyer, who was the
first Anglican Bishop of Grafton and Armidale,
after preaching one Sunday night in 1868 set off
in a boat for his home up the Clarence River. He
had with him in the boat his, son, a servant, and
three members of his flock. The night was gusty,
and about two miles up the river the boat capsized
and all went under. Three managed to scramble
ashore, but the Bishop, his son and the servant
were drowned. The bodies' were discovered a
couple of days after. Dr. Sawyer was only 37
years of age at the time, and had been but three
years a bishop. Physically and mentally he was a
strong, able man, and the memorial now on its way
will revive many stories of his pluck in the early
bush.
Rev. Dr. Brewster, the first barrister who
went to Melbourne (in 1839 he became Chairman
of Quarter Sessions) and died last March at the
Cape of Good Hope, where he had long been a
clergyman, left, as has already been here re-
corded, an estate of about .£150,000. His pro-
perty in Melbourne includes the Victoria Arcade,
upon which there are four pubs, and two theatres,
[n ’76 the doctor let the site on building lease for
30 years to the late Joe Aarons, and Joe built the
Academy of Music (now Bijou Theatre) and the
Arcade. At first the Arcade was a street of
shops, but Wilson’s billiard-room and the Gaiety
Theatre wiped them out. It was rather enter-
prising for a parson to have four pubs, and a pair
of playhouses on his property. His Melbourne
agent, James Graham, used to boast to his prin-
cipal : “ I own three pubs., but you lick me with
four pubs, and two playhouses. If I could only
let half an acre for a church .” The lease of the
Arcade ground falls-in seven years hence, and then
young Mrs. Brewster will own the lot without in-
cumbrance. She is the daughter of a Birming-
ham civil engineer.
The unaffected esteem in which Hori Grey
was held by the Maoris dated from the beginning
of his first pro-consulship in 1845. He never re-
fused to receive a Maori suppliant even of the
lowest degree, and the energy with which he set
himself to master their tongue and to make him-
self conversant with their traditions and supersti-
tions was, he once told the present writer, due to
the anxiety he felt to understand their grievances
thoroughly. Filtered through an unsympathetic
interpreter, many a story of wrong, he felt, might
not be adequately presented to him. He was on
terms of the warmest friendship with all the great
native chiefs whose names live in the history of
British colonisation, and it was a pathetic sight to
see these tattooed warriors wait upon Grey on his
birthday at the Northern Club, Auckland, and to
watch them with streaming eyes and broken
words pay their respects to one who had been for
50 years the friend and guardian of' their race.
The last request made by Rewi Manga Mania-
poto, the rebel hero of the memorable defence of
Orakau, was—“ Next my grave let there be room
left for Nga Hori Kerei.”

Aboriginalities.
“ Yangan ” : Snake-yarn season has com-
menced. In June, ’92, a python entered a pig-pen
in Borneo, swallowed full-grown inmate, and was
unable to get out. Brought into town, we bought
him for 6dol. (125.). Measured 23ft. 4in. At this
time a bluejacket from H.M.S. Egeria was lost in
the jungle ; and, as some fool started a yarn that
“Bill” was the cause of the swelling in the
python’s middle, day and night “ Bill’s ” mates
would come up and ask to view “ the serpent wot
skoffed poor Bill.” “ Blyme,” said one, “if Bill
ain’t got ’is long legs drored up to ’is chin,
same as ’e done when ’e ’ad the ’ammick
next to me. ’ Then, as the snake made an effort
to move his dinner another inch, “ Garn, Bill 1
why don’t you keep quiet and give the snake a
chanct ?” Bill didn’t seem too popular, but when
he turned up safe much beer was shed. Thirteen
months later we shipped the snake for England at
an offer of £2 a foot, but the brute, having got
accustomed to a diet of police-impounded dogs,
declined fowls en voyage, and pegged out
before arrival. Only one larger python (25ft. 6in.)
have I actually measured. Once, out yachting,
had been without fire for three days, landed on
Pulo Hantu, made driftwood fire, laid out our togs
to dry, and went for a swim. Came back in about
an hour, and fouitd the ground about the fire alive
with snakes—blue-grey beasts, with flattened tails.
Killed over a score with mangrove branches before
the rest took to the sea.
* * * *
Strathmerton, the border township near
where the recent mate-murder was committed, is
probably the drunkest and most blasphemous
spot in all Australia. It consists of about 30 flat,
galvanised-iron buildings sprawling around a
railway-station, and has a population of about
150, most of whom took a few days off to
get properly drunk the moment the body was
brought in. Then, for the following three days,
the township surged in the bars, and swore and
talked fight in their vicinity from 6 a.m. until
midnight, only resting between drinks, when the
post mortem was made, to try and have a look at
the doctor “ openin’ up the poor bloke.” Though
the opinion was freely expressed that the
murderer “ oughter be ’ung up over a fire! ”
Strathmertonians were obviously delighted that

his act had caused them to awake famous, and the
possibility of floating a loan in London and build-
ing a £20,000 town hall was plainly in everybody’s
eyes.
S* * * *
Re bottle-trees. A Bulletin man desires
to give some further information for the use of
schopl - papers, Sunday - school librarians and
others. Writer knew aQ. squatter—he owned
a station called Rougemont—who grew rows of
the trees about his paddocks, and irrigated
67,000 acres from them. He had a very big one
near his homestead, of which he was extremely
proud. The hands about the place were trained
firemen, and maintained an excellent brigade.
The old man, when there was a colonial-experi-
encer hanging-out, would usually ring the firebell,
whereupon all hands would turn out, whip along
with the hose, spike it into the big bottle-tree
with a clever auger arrangement, and—gee, whiz !
—there was the nozzle spouting a stream sufficient
to wash the homestead off the face of nature.
Measured the best stream she threw, and it was
five yards seven inches under the quarter of a
mile. There was only one accident that I remem-
ber, and the result was the flooding of the whole
countryside. Something went wrong with the
fittings, and they couldn’t turn the tree off at the
main for nearly a week.

Re that item re t’other item concerning
counting-capacity of Port Phillip blacks. When

answer : “ ’Underds !—tousandts 1— millions !
plurrynear dozens.”
* * • *
“Steele Rudd ” : When two “ old-man ”
bears meet upon the same tree there’s generally a
row about sundown; the “go-in” is one of the
most exciting sights the bush affords. Have
dropped upon several well worth witnessing. First
the bears sat up dingo-fashion on their respective
limbs and snorted savagely across at each other.
Then, standing erect on all-fours, they trotted
down head-first (the only time bear does descend a
tree head-first), and, meeting at the fork, flew at
each other and fought like dogs, filling the air
with a queer medley of snorts and squeals,
until, locked together, they reeled out of the tree
and came wallop on the ground. Then, letting
each other go, they scrambled hurriedly up the
tree again and snorted violently, but renewed not
the fight.

Dear Bulletin. —Anybody tell me what ia
the greatest distance from the sea-coast sound of
surf has been heard? Yesterday, stormy; to-
night, calm and frosty ; I can distinctly hear roll
of sea at least 30 miles distant. Stony nature of
country no doubt helps sound-transmission, be-
cause, on similar surface, have heard the sea
between 40 and 50 miles away by applying ear to
boarded floor of old station, built on solid lime-
stone rock. The previous lessee was “planted”
in the backyard, and we used to suffer touches of
the “ Jims ” until we found out what the rumbling
really meant.—Long-un.

Dear Bulletin. —Recently saw some
Chinkies, who had been cutting cabbages, knock
off for lunch, and on going over to see what the
fare was, was thunderstruck to find it plumcake
and lemonade.—T.P.
“ Alia ” : This is the origin of
at least one of the stories of the
“hairy man” prevalent in New
England, N.S.W. In the rough
skirts of the coastal range a party
of prospectors came across a mob
of about 50 “ clearskins,” and
decided to go to Armidale, arrange
for the sale of the cattle; then
return, take the mob to a neigh-
boring selection, brand them, and
divide the proceeds of sale. How-
ever, a man who had been watch-
ing them on the quiet, guessing
what they were up to, made him
a suit of goat-skins, stitched a cow-
tail in the proper position, fixed a
pair of horns on his head, and,
planting himself on a ledge above
a water-hole at which the pros-
pectors camped, waited till the full
moonlight would give them all the
benefit of his extraordinary appear-
ance, and then set to work throw-
ing rocks at them, and howling in
a most blood - curdling manner.
Now, as the scene was a gloomy
gorge, in a wild, desolate region,
small wonder that next issue of
the local journals devoted much
space to a thrilling account of the
“ hairy man ” —most convincing to
everyone but “ Dick,” who quietly
rounded up the mob, got a couple
of black boys to help him with
them to the station, and put his
brand on them. The prospectors,
however, on the strength of their
remarkable experience, struck it
rich in drinks for weeks afterwards.

The sign of the dead dog ia
now pretty well recognised in the
West. When a party has pros-
pected a locality for nix, disgusted
with its bad luck, it hangs its dog
m a conspicuous place and decamps. The Bul-
letin is much afraid that the Westralian dog-
supply will soon run out.

“Snell”: I also have a bunyip story.
Higinbotham had twice seen the animal in the
Goulburn, near his homestead, just beyond Na-
gambie (Vic.), and described it minutely in the
bar parlor. It had a round head, he said, and
large, staring eyes, and there was much long,
reddish hair floating around its face, as he saw it
close to the surface of the water in the bright
moonlight of the summer night. Higinbotham
admitted being scared, and giving the brute lots
of elbow-room. Then there was much talk about
bunyips, and every man had seen a different kind
°f bunyip ; but Scott was the authority, and gave
Higinbotham minute directions for setting a trap,
with ropes that would be sure to land the monster
if he visited those waters again. Higinbotham
and Scott, it was understood, were to go halves in
the bunyip—when he was trapped. We heard no
more about Higinbotham’s bunyip at the pub.,
but, three days later, the district was full of a wild

but, as I said before, we heard no more of Higin-
botham’s bunyip.

“0.T.M.”: lie rat-enemies, you can’t
beat the carpet-snake. He is also a time-econo-

be swallowed to day. In maize field recently saw

‘‘ Steele Rudd ” : The jackass is a feathered
fraud. When very young I was taught, like
many more, to believe in him, and whenever a
snake came to the house after lodgings I expected
to see jack swoop down upon him & la St.
Patrick. But he never would. Jack is afraid- of
snakes, and it’s only a young one that he’ll even
try to kill. But a snake isn’t afraid of a jackass.
It’s a common thing to see a foot or so of snake
hanging out of jack’s nest, while jack and a dozen
others, with ruffled feathers, squawk and cackle
at him in a most absurd manner from the top
branches of the tree. Place jack and a snake
together in a cage, or in a 24ft. ring, and my
money goes on snake.

“Jim. Pan.”: Ever heard of the “ Peli-
can Spree” occasionally indulged in by the up-
roarious cane-cutter in the Big Scrub ? Recipe :
Disused barn; three gallons of Richmond River
rum ; 3doz. red herrings; 7doz. onions ; 11 loaves
stale bread; a dozen tins anchovy paste; 131 b.
good old galloping cheese. All to be consumed in
five days. If, at the end of that time, the
breath be of a deafening color and strong enough
to hang your hat on, you may conclude that the
festival has not been a failure.

“Tommy, J.P.” : Susie Capps, an abo-
riginal lady residing near Tabalam in the Big
Scrub, recently applied to the Aborigines
Protection Board for an inscribed brass plate to
hang upon her fairy chest in token of her dignity
as “Kaween belonga Alstonville,” on the Rich-
mond River. The constable ordered to make the
necessary genealogical researches, however, re-
ported that the aspirant to royal honors is only
plain Susannah, wife of Harra Capps, of Two-
wharves, and has absolutely no claims to sove-
reign rank. After all, though, Susan’s request
is only on a par with her white sister’s cravings
for a tinpot title. Even a reddot republican gene-
rally has to take some handle or other “ just to
please the old woman, you know ! ” Her would-
be Majesty Susannah used to scrub house and
school in Twowbarves for a teacher-friend o’mine,
and it was great fun to see her sailing around in
Mrs. Dominie’s cast-offs. The Dominie also once
gave the husband, Harra Capps, a discarded
dress-suit; but Harra caught a severe cold through
the open-fronted garment’s exposure of his re-
spected bingie. He said: “Baal gammon, that
pfeller coat nearly all farresh air. Mine think it
want door alonga front! ’’ And he traded the
coat to Jimmie Pannikin, who used to receive dis-
tinguished commercial travellers in it at Ballina.

“E.S.”: Have not seen it mentioned
that the flood-bird, which heralds big rains out
west, jumps the nest of the crow, throws out the
latter’s eggs, and deposits its own in their place.
The crow hatches the latter, and feeds the young
flood-birds until they are pretty well fledged, by
which time Mrs. Crow begins to have suspicions,
and finally fires out the aliens to battle for them-
selves. Next flood they’ll turn up again, and do
what mother did.
Antiquari-
ans in Eng-

fond regard
for those

been Roman
camps, Bri-

or Saxon vil-

■ the signs of

On the Red
Bluff, St. Hil-
da, at the an-
gle nearest
Melbourne,
there is a deep
deposit of
hard - caked
black wood-
ash and burnt
shell and
stone. It is
many feet
thick, just at
the edge of
the red sand-
stone cliff.
This was for-
merly for
generations,
probably a
blackfellow’s oven, where the savage cooked his
meals. Few people,, enquiry shows, have ever
noticed it. Years hence, long-haired spectacled
savants may pay respectful pilgrimages to that
black patch, and spin learned theories about it.
It is the only deposit of the kind on the shore
of Hobson’s Bay.

More about the classic N.S.W. town of
Booroowa. During a gory struggle one moonlight
night between two different clans of Mikes, the
defending party was driven back, and took refuge
in the hut of Patsy Burke —a poor old man whom
no one wanted to hurt. The leader of the attackers
went forward to reconnoitre, and, just as he was
sneaking along to the window, a head appeared at
it. It shone in the moonlight, and all hands could
see it was old Patsy’s; but the temptation was
too much for the skirmishing warrior—he lifted
his “ sthick ” and brought it down with a bash on
the bald head. It disappeared with a yelp, and
the smiter returned to his men. “ Och, Moike,”
cried one of them: “ poor ole Patsy ! How cud
yer hit ’im loike that ? ” “ What! How cud I
nit’im!” yelled the warrior. “Shure, how the
divil oud I miss him I ”

“ W.L.” writes this quaint letter :
I should like to know why do you call the unfortunate
Chinese people “ Chows ’’ in the fraud of a paper called
Tub Bulletin, which is not worth buying. It is a great
disgrace to the miserable publication called The Bulle-
tin and the editors. I will have nothing to do with you
now, and I shall disgrace the paper if you shall print', or
rather publish, bad things about the Chinese in the
paper. Although lam an Australian, learning my trade,
I have been almost all through China (which the Chinese
which are known as a very kind race of people), has
taken me from town to town and giving many presents
to me, I cannot stand them getting their bad feeling over
the Australian people. My father is an Englishman, and
my mother an Irish-woman. I call the British people
Upside-down Devils.

Heterodoxy on the snake-poison question :
Dear Bulletin.— Re present beginning of snake-season.
Australian State-school children are taught that the teeth
of the snake are hollow, and that, when a snake bites, the
poison is expelled through these teeth into the wound.
This, though the generally-accepted theory, is false. The
teeth of the snake are not hollow and the poison is not
thus ejected into the wound. The snake uses its teeth to
make a surface wound only, and the poison is placed on
the wound by a hollow protuberance, like a parrot’s
tongue (situate at the back of the teeth), which can be
opened at will by the snake, and contains (in a venomous
snake) a greenish viscid substance. This is poison, and,
when a snake bites, the protuberance opens and, by a for-
ward and upward movement, dabs the poison on the
wound. If a person is bitten on a bare limb, no fatal
results will follow if the wound is immediately washed or
wiped with a wet rag. It is quite possible for a snake to
bite without poisoning. These observations are the result
of long experience of, and experiment with, snakes.—F.
Bradshaw.

“ E.D ” : Re the working girl. Knew
one on a small Tasmanian rush—a brown-faced,
bare-footed, scantily-clad damsel of about 16 who
worked her eight hours swinging an axe. Her
father was splitting slabs for the claims, and the
girl would fell the gums, and cut the lengths,
without appearing to be at all put about. Despite
the heavy work she was a most playful and in-
genuous “ critter,” and romped around amongst
the diggers at crib-time like an exuberant young
kid. Sometimes she would bring a baby-sister to
work, and attended to the youngster in addition
to her ordinary duties as axewoman. Her
father was a stolid, fat-headed man who seemed
to think the girl was only doing her duty by him.
“ What’s she doin’ here ? ” asked a digger, signify-
ing the baby. “ Why the devil ain’t she work-
in’?” “ Oh,” said the father soberly, “she can’t
walk yet.”
“ The Mocker ” : Be big-city notion that
a man has only to stroll into the bush with a swag
on his back to find work, or, at all events, bound-
less hospitality. The rations given
nowadays in station country will
hardly keep a mouse alive. If a
traveller has money the stations rob
him (4d., 5d., or 6d. a lb. for weevily
flour); if he has none, he has to rob
them. Tucker-getting in station
country is reduced to a science, yet
if it were not for surreptitious doles
from cooks, and an occasional break-
ing into a boundary-rider’s hut, death
from starvation would be much more
common in the bush. The hardest
articles to come by, honestly or
otherwise, are baking-powder and
tobacco, and, with all deference to
“ .Three-years-for-a-tin-of-baking-powder-
Gibson,” both of them are necessaries out
back.
“Nell Cook”: I had two little spotted
wild pigs from the Queensland scrub given me
when they were very small. Soon they would
follow me everywhere like dogs ; they were
as playful as kittens and developed an in-
telligence very remarkable considering their
youth—and people’s ideas concerning their species.
When hungry they would nibble at my feet with
aggravated little squeals, and if I did not attend
to them they would seize the hem of my skirt in
their teeth and try to drag me to their trough.
They would bristle up when strangers came about,
and take sly snaps at their heels when they turned
away. They had an affectionate but embarrassing
habit of taking refuge under my skirts from all
dangers, real or imaginary. I had unpleasant
forebodings as to the inconvenience of this and
their other sportive little ways when they should
be fully grown ; so when it was proposed they
should be killed I consented to the deed, but I
felt as though it were a murder, and would not
have eaten a bit of them on any account.

“ Steele Rudd ” ; Now, is the Australian
bushman—born or otherwise —naturally a good
horseman ? I say he mostly isn’t, and he’s a
friend of mine. When a fellow’s name is Bill
or Jim and he wears a big beard, and can shear or
shoot kangaroos, and walk bandy, and smell like
brigalow, it’s no criterion whatever that he’s a
horseman. We all know that the bush can claim
some good riders—the best of riders—but for
every one of these how many chumps has it—
how many fellows who couldn’t be persuaded by a
ton of dynamite to run the best horse in the world
at a two-rail fence were it to save his life, or mount
a cuddy he thought would “root” or ccck its
tail ? It’s full of ’em. Of course all —or nearly all
—can ride quiet horses, but is that horsemanship ?

“C.H.R.” : Knowan old West Coast (Tas.)
hand who lives mostly on rats and ’possums,
though raw bacon, chiefly slaughter-house, is his
particular chow-chow. He is also partial to white-
grubs spread on damper, and smacks his lips
over ship’s biscuits plastered with Holloway’s
ointment. Saw him one day fish out of a creek a
drowned tiger cat, which, to judge by its aroma,
must have been defunct about ten days. In a few
minutes he had it in the billy—which lie afterwards
used for tea. Told me he liked ’em well dead—
the flesh was so sweet and tender. He was a
splendid guide in the bush—you only had to keep
about a quarter of a mile to the rear—dangerous
to get much closer—and follow the scent. His
was the easiest camp to find I ever struck. At
two miles you caught a faint whiff, and by in-
creasing the strength you couldn’t go wrong.
When you smelt something like a dead elephant
you would be within hail, and when the air com-
menced to turn green and everything felt inside-
out, well —you would be thar or tharabouts.

“Raspberry”: Knew an old bushman,
where bull-dog ants were innumerable, who used
daily to slice about 200 in half with his sheath-
knife. Have seen thousands of bodiless heads in
his camp walking around looking for their busi-
ness-ends. The ancient reckoned they lived three
weeks after amputation, and he used to make kind
of pets of them in their harmless condition. It
was curious and interesting to see “ Anti-Sting ”
and “ Old Humpy ” cantering up for a shred of
meat or a grain of sugar ; and often, just as one
would be opening his pinchers for a tit-bit, he
would suddenly fall back, roll up his legs and die.
Have never seen the two halves fight, but have
often noticed legs carry off body. For what pur-
pose I know not; to bury it, perhaps.

“ Matthina ” : Re Zeehan mud. You
merely sink ankle-deep there now, whereas in the
happy boom-time you went hips under. In those
days you spotted a Zeehanite by his bald eye-
brows ; and I knew an old N.S. W. merino king
who had to be stripped naked and shorn close to
get the mud off him. The man who barbered him
told me that when the fleece fell off it rattled on
the floor like a string of pebbles, and the old man
took it away with him as specimens of Zeehan
galena.

“Thomac”: Re derivation of “pushing
the knot.” When I wore the Order of the
Wallaby (ere the advent of the “flash swaggie ”
with his half-dozen straps) the apropos of the
phrase was this: Swag consisted of blanket, spare
singlet, pair of moles, towel, couple of colored
handkerchiefs for binders, and woollen muffler for
sling (wool being springy and easy on the
shoulder). Swag was fastened near ends with the
binders, through which was passed the sling, so
arranged that the knot came just below the breast
and gave a rest for the hand, which thus acquired
a habit of pushing the sling outwards _ from the
body as the man neared the end of his tramp.
Makes me sit-up to see the useless make-weights
fellows have in their swags nowadays. Two or
three pair pants, several shirts, spare hats (fancy
a swaggie with an extra boxer), and carrying
besides fry-pans, axes, tin-dishes and buckets, or

It was the first week at Chuckumout shed.
Only a couple had their hands in, and Dick had
third-highest tally. Swelling with pride and
magnanimity, he said to M‘Kenzie: “Cut in,
Mac, you can lick lots of these coves ’ere. You’ll
be fourth, anyway.” Next day “ Mac ” wasahead,
and said he to Dick : “ Cut in, Dick ; you can lick
lots ci these coves ’ere. You’ll be fifth, anyway.”
The yarn went round, and every man-Jack was
doing his level best. One after another, the others
passed Dick’s tally, till at last one shouted across
the board: “ Cut in, Dick; you’ll be last any-
way ! ”

“ Wer.” : Shearers nowadays are not the
happy-go-lucky fellows they used to be ; they’re
studying economy. Coming to a shed, they take
the shoes off their horses and put them away till
the shed’s cut out, when they fasten them on
again. Saves wear. One time, a shearer wouldn’t
start work in the morning if the wool was the
least damp; now, when the water’s dripping out
of it, he’s got to be told to leave off. Also, in the
old days, a “chip” was an insult, followed by a
vacant stand ; now the shearer takes as many as
the boss will stand him, and quits only when told
to. One hardened rep., so leaving, asked me if I
was “ financial ” ; if not, could he sell me tickets
for ’9B and ’99 ? Union, boys, union ! It’s stronger
to-day than ever it was—among the squatters.

Dear B.—Heard nothing from any of your
bush-biologists about the tree-climbing fish, of the
Bloomfield and Gulf rivers, N.Q., which, when
alarmed, may be seen jumping from the mangrove
branches in shoals ; or about the toxotis, or archer-
fish, of Cape York Peninsula creeks —the bush-
man’s “ 10-gun brig ” (on account of five black
spots on each side, like port-holes). This little
chap keeps aloof from other fish, and dodges
around waterhole-edges, dislodging its prey from
overhanging branches with a single swiftly-pro-
jected drop of water —sometimes to a height of
lOft.-C.B.S.
A queer thing happened 30 years ago to
Mrs. Austin, the rich and philanthropic Victori-
enne. The Duke of Edinburgh, then touring
Australia, was taken out West to see the country
and mot rabbits, which were scarce then. (It
was Mrs. Austin’s late husband who first let them
loose in the South.) Well, Edinburgh came to
dinner at Barwon Park tired and sleepy, and at
dessert suddenly fell asleep on the hostess’
shoulder.
ROUGEMONT, or GREIN, the Australian-
Fairy-Tale Man.
After all, Rougemont, the French person
creating such a stir in credulous England, is a
very commonplace variety of tozer. But he is
certainly a romancist of some force of character.
When his forty-ton ship was in the storm:
Still the current drew me helplessly onward, and in a
few minutes the ship was in a dangerous whirlpool round
which she was carried several times before I managed to
extricate her. Next we were drawn close to some rocks,
and I had to stand resolutely by with an oar in order to
keep the vessel's head from striking.
Another good effort is where he calls at an un-
civilised island and gives the blacks tomahawks,
knives, &c., in exchange for cases of condensed
milk and tinned fruits. The uncivilised nig. who
lives on condensed milk and tinned fruit is cer-
tainly a new discovery.
Mr. R. J. Black, who has recently received
the distinction of election to the directorate boards
of both the Bank of N.S.W. and the A.M.P.
Society, is one of the most popular and best res-
pected men in Sydney finance. His family’s
Australian history begins in 1798, when his grand-
father, a navigator, having assisted to capture a
Spanish prize, was placed in command of her, and
brought her to Sydney. Here he settled and
carried on mercantile business until he sailed on a
trip to India, and was never again heard of. His
son became manager of the Bank of N.S.W. in
1824, and managed it for 30 years, through all the
stormy periods down to ’54. The navigator’s grand-
son, Reginald J. Black, was born in the Bank of
N.S.W. in the year 1845, what time the bank
stood on Walter Bradley and co’s. present site.
The barracks frowned opposite, and the bank, like
many other George-st. houses, had a plum-garden
in front and a fruit and vegetable garden at the
back, through which flowed the Tank Stream.
Young Black went to Sydney Grammar School
with Edmund Barton, Trickett, M.L.C., E. W.
Knox, A. J. Cape, the late Justice Mein, of Q. ;
and he often saw the bullock-teams winding
their way through the city, and saw gold-dust
sold by auction in three and four ounce bottles.
Young Black knew Woolloomooloo as mostly
bush, and Mostnan’s and Neutral Bay as shooting
grounds for gill-birds. He inherited the sporting
instincts of his grandfather who, in 1799, chased
black swans 15 miles on the Parramatta River
without getting a shot at them. In the gill-bird
time of Mo-man's there was but one small
steamer running to North Shore, and she was so
small that she carried a number of 561 b. weights,
so that when a couple of fat men got together on
one side of her, the weights could be placed on
the opposite side so as to preserve the vessel’s
evenness of keel. Mr. Black entered the B.N.S. W.
in ’63 at the age of 18; rose rapidly to inspectorship,
and stayed in the bank service until ’B2, when he
entered into private business with the well-known
firm of Messrs. Jones and Black, of Bond-street,
Sydney. When the 1893 bank crisis came Mr.
Black was the first man outside the Ministry
whom Dibbs consulted. Mr. Black was member
for Mudgee from ’B7 to ’9l, and he has always
taken an active interest in educational, charitable,
political and financial movements.
Of Australia’s recent visitor, the Duke of
Manchester, thus a writer in London Pelican :
The Duke is not a rich man ; indeed, for a Duke he is a
pauper, as his income at present does not exceed £3OO a
year. There are three Dowager-Duchesses, the widow of
the sixth Duke, who married Sir Stephenson Blackwood ;
the widow of the seventh, who is Louise Duchess of
Devonshire ; and his mother, all of whom have dowries
paid out of the estate. Perhaps now that he is range
his grandmother will forego her dowry in his favor,
which as Duchess of Devonshire she can well afford to do.
His fiancee has no association with the Tranby Croft
scandal beyond being a niece of Mrs. Charles Wilson, the
chatelaine of that place; her father is Mr. Arthur Wil-
son, M.P., who, besides being a shipowner, is a part pro-
prietor of the Weekly Sun. Miss Joan Wilson is a tall
girl, but the beauty of the family is her sister Enid.
Lawyers have always laughed at the
familiar notice, “Trespassers will be prosecuted,”
and have called it “ a wooden lie.” Certainly the
prevailing theory among lawyers was that unless
the trespass was aggravated by a dog or gun or
wanton destruction, no “ prosecution ” could take
place, and, unless a claim of right was in ques-
tion, little or no satisfaction could be got in the
civil court. But if the decision in Grayford v.
Ghouler this year be correct, the trespasser is no
longer in his old privileged position. In this
case the defendant, to make a short cut, walked
across a grass-field, with a notice-board, and after
warnings. The grass was pretty highland he is
said to have done 6d. worth of damage in his pas-
sage. The two Judges decided that he was rightly
convicted of damaging the grass, under a section
similar to the ordinary Australian section on
“damage to real property.”
Mr. Aubrey Davis, dentist, of 492 George-
street, Sydney, opposite entrance to new markets,
has —he states—an absolutely painless method for
extracting teeth. This method has quite eclipsed
nitrous-oxide gas, for it not only allows patients
to be conscious the whole time of operation, but
also leaves them without such after-effects as head-
aches, etc. Hundreds upon hundreds of grateful
patients have testimonialised Mr. Davis’ method ;
and, by the nervous and the sensitive, it is regarded
as a blessing little short of a miracle. Mr. Davis,
with his 16 years’experience in his present suite of
rooms, is an accomplished setter of artificial teeth,
and has developed gold crowns over decayed roots,
crown and bridge-work, to a very tine art indeed.
He does not charge for consultations.*
What We Should Do for the Mining
Industry.
“ Quartz ” to The Bulletin :
Australia owes something to mining. The chief State
effort at its encouragement has been subsidies to boodiing
companies, &c. We have agricultural shows and model
farms for the farmers—why not some national effort to
systematize mining? In Victoria there have been two
abortive attempts at an exhibition of mining machinery.
The first failed because it was held in a foundry, at which
other foundries would not exhibit. The second was held
in Melb. Exhibition, and would have been a success if it
had even got properly finished. To the very last, the
machinery-men were rushing about with nails in their
mouths, preparing to get. ready. Yet these little attempts
were something in the right direction. The stamper-
battery and amalgamation uere for many years the Aus-
tralian miner’s Bible and Book of Common Prayer. As a
sort of Solace to Sinners, he has of late got a vague notion
of oyaniding. But the nolion is very vague indeed,
and the attempts at realisation are mostly Failure.
What would you have ? says the Department of Mines.
We have subsidised the Schools of Mines. Colleges of
Technical Education, at which are taught orewel-work,
violin-scraping, and possibly Lewis Carroll s three R’s
at the eel’sschool -reeling, writhing and fainting in coils.
One province set up a cyanide works, but, its metallurgical
knowledge was so limited that, when it sold itsgold. it
complained about a falling-off in the returns. A sum in
simple division had to be forwarded by the gold-buyer, to
show that there was more zinc left in one case than in
the other. Let the State interfere for good by employing
experts, whether obtainable in Australia or abroad. Stop
the waste of School of Mines’ subsidies to young ladies’ frills.
Stop the jobbery of prospecting votes. There are- more
corrupt practices under this item in the estimates than
in a N.S.W. election campaign. Give us annual exhibi-
tions of mining machinery, duly advertised to attract the
makers in Europe and America. Perhaps we might get
some literature on the subject written by trained men
with practical knowledge. (For a hotchpotch of fact and
fancy, refer to Rose’s “ Metallurgy of Gold,” second ed.,
just out. Rock crushers that were out of date years ago,
describee, at tedious length ; important machines dis-
missed in a few lines; undigested statements about
China, Mexico, Australia. Yet, withal, enough good in
it to make it almost a classic). Give us a decent hand-
book of mining, if it be possible—but by all means bring
the inventor and manufacturer nearer to the miner.
Dear Bulletin. —Some Tasmanians might
object to your name for their little island across
the Strait —I don’t. Better to Sleep-a-Lot than
wander aimlessly around with eyes half glued-
up with sand and brickdust, as they do in
Sydney. We can afford to Sleep. Here, in Tas.,
all our surroundings are awake and calling. Is
not the everlasting music of our rippling streams,
winding aw ay beneath the cool, myrtle forests,
preferable to the noiseless, soulless billabongs of
N.S.W., which creep miserably away across sun-
cracked, death-spotted plains ? Is not the crystal
clearness of our lakes, where the fish leap to meet
the sunlight, and the ducks dance upon the waves,
more to be desired than the melancholy of the
dead-boxtree-dotted holes they call lakes on the
Sydney side ? A man told me (and I believe him
because he was a rabbit-inspector) that he had
seen cod crawling along the bottom of a N.S.W.
river with an army of crows feasting off their
spines. We have rivers here—not dead canals ;
and lakes—not dustbins. Sleep-a-Lot ? Yes, we
do. But in one of our waking moments we in-
vited Tattersall over to stay ; and we didn’t sacri-
fice .£30,000 per an. on the altar of our heavenly
REGINALD BLACK, a director of the Bank
of N.S.W. and of the A.M.P . Society.
Photo., Freeman and Co. Ltd.
hopes. In another lucid interval we appropriated
the tote, instead of sporting our coin on the off-chance
of dragging miserable divs. out of Moses and Aaron.
We were considerably awake, too, on 3rd June
last; and it was just their sleepless, hidebound,
gummy-eyed condition that caused the N.S.
Welshers to fail their country at the right moment.
The Bulletin expresses conviction that oiir
bishop is too wide-awake for Tasmania. That is
my opinion, too. The general run of Tas. bishops
appear to divide their attention between saintly
Hobart and the mutton-birders, and 43 men of
that stamp couldn’t better our morals. We shun
them with awe in the city, and thank God they
never visit us in the bush. And if the present
boss-parson humped his bluey clean out of the
island no one would care much, and few be wiser.
The Bulletin, which also draws attention to our
national virtue re an old tub in Hobart which is
said to have lived through the Nile business, is
quite in error when it says we are much disturbed
over the argument. Nothing disturbs us, except
the beastly whistles of the Sydney boats when
they get bushed, and we don’t care a rap whether
the ancient wreck aforementioned turns out to be
old Noah’s Ark or King Solomon’s foot - bath.
Yes, we sleep and grow fat. Why, we can show a
Governor who can weigh up Sydney’s vice-regent
and its beloved Premier on top of him; and we
can turn out on sight enough raspberry-jam to
silt up its beautiful harbor. Then look at our
Braddon ; and old never-die Douglas—but enough
said. “The Land of Sleep-a-Lot!” Good!
We’re proud of it.— Tasmaniao.

The Co-Operative Wool & Produce Co., Ltd.

Prisoners Lilley and Funnell showed in-
genuity of the Jean Valjean order in breaking out
of Coolamon, N.S.W., “logs.” To burn out the
iron door-slide and fixings they used shredded-up
cocoanut matting. The whole centre of the door
was burnt out, and had they set the building on
fire at any other part they might have been roasted
in a hell of their own igniting. A little girl going
past the police-station early in the morning gave
the alarm, or the building would have been burnt
down.
Do you ever have a headache ? Would
you not have a cure—simple, harmless, effective,
of trifling cost, and occupying no more space than
a thimble ? Then, write or call on Newman and
Snelson (chemists to Lord Hampden), King-
street, Sydney. They have discovered a specific
which instantly relieves a headache of any origin,
and which never produces any of the injurious
after-effects so common with other headache cures.
The new cure is put up in packets of ten tablets —
ten doses —for Is. Id. by post.*
