Thomas Joseph Byrnes.
My school life was in some sense contemporary
with that of the late Premier Byrnes; but he
ended his Grammar School course while I was
still in the throes of Euclid and Virgil. I well
remember how his success in winning one of the
first University scholarships given by Q. Govern-
ment was stressed for us youngsters’ benefit, with
the appropriate teacherly injunction to go and do
likewise. Later, I met Byrnes occasionally in the
course of journalism. I saw him for the last time
at the end of August, when he was in Sydney to
discuss New Guinea syndicate matters.
* * * »
At school Byrnes was regarded rather as a
plodding than as a brilliant boy. Ido not mean
that he was dull—far from it; but some of his
classmates seemed brighter. Arid though a
clever man, a brainy man, he never became
what I would call a brilliant man. This is not
disparagement; for Byrnes was entitled to rub
brains with any of his compeers in Australia; he
was on Barton’s and Griffith’s intellectual level.
Yet I do not think he had Barton or Griffith’s
intellectual force. It is a matter of adjectival
definitions. Judged by old-world standards, are
even Barton and Griffith brilliant? I think of
Disraeli, and say No.
But Byrnes sufficed for his
sphere. He was obviously
and consciously above his
colleagues in the present Q.
Parliament—l do not mean
in the little arts and crafts of
politics, or even in business
capacity; but as a brain-
force, an original stimulus,
an all-round intelligence.
And I have taken trouble
to make his place plain be-
cause the press notices have
been crowded with refer-
ences to Byrnes’s “ brilliant
career,” his “ brilliant
achievements.” As is gener-
ally the case, the memorial
notices were full of errors.
Even in his life Michael
Davitt wrote of a 15-stone
Premier, nearly 6ft. tall.
Byrnes never weighed 15
stone ; and he was a man
of medium height—certainly
little over sft. Sin. He rather
left upon you the impression
of a short and broad man.
* * ♦ *
Almost my first remark
when I saw him last was
that he looked old and tired.
He was only 37, yet his hair
and beard were turning grey ;
his beard was markedly grey.
To my comment he said that
“ what you call the trium-
phal tour through Queens-
land was very fatiguing.
But,” he added, “I feel
strong ; I have a good consti-
tution.” He had influenza,
and refused to smoke. And,
as Henry Hess says, when a
smoker cannot smoke it is
time to see a doctor. From
influenza Byrnes passed
through measles and pneu-
monia to death. The doctors
laid the blame on his weak
heart. I go farther back, and
lay it on degenerated nerves.
Byrnes never spared his
nerves. He had worked
and later he had enjoyed him-
self, trusting to his “good constitution.” But I
noted his Fat as a sign of weakness. Of course,
there is Fat and Fat. The fat of a hearty young
woman springs from sheer overplus of vitality ;
and every atom of it is hard and healthy. Her
fat is force. But fat in a young man usually
means failure of the vital processes ; it is accumu-
lated debris which the body is unable to get rid
of. Perhaps no man has any business to be fat
before he is quite 40. And Byrnes was not quite
38.
♦ ♦ * *
His death once more raises the question of Aus-
tralian stamina. Griffith has worked far harder
than Byrnes ever did; he has had infinitely more
worry and anxiety ; yet Griffith is alive at 53,
though he is ageing or aged. I suppose Reid has
lived harder than Byrnes—has attended more
banquets, drunk more whisky, had farther fling—
yet Reid is lively at 53. Young Curnow, son of
the editor of S.M. Herald, collapsed suddenly
like Byrnes; yet old man Curnow is well above
ground, even if his health is not as good as could
be wished. I could quote a dozen promising
young Australians who have suddenly dropped
out of tiie team when the strain came. Are we
not as good as our fathers ? If not, why are we
not? Is it climate, environment, education ?
* • * *
Byrnes deserved all he got, yet he always
struck me as a lucky man—in the conventional
sense of luck. Griffith picked him out in
1890 and put him on the ladder ; he had only to
hold on and to climb. Certainly, a weak man
would have dropped off; but Byrnes was a strong
man, though not, as I have said, one of the strong-
est. He did justice to Griffith’s choice; and with
a little more grit would have done more justice.
But Byrnes was spasmodic ; he was. fond of
change; and his heart was never wholly in politics
—he had too much brains for that (or too little,
according to standpoint). “ What is there in it
at all?” he said to me. “I shall fight the next
election : after that, I don’t know.” Yet Byrnes
was ambitious ; Tozer thought he was very am-
bitious. I fancy “that tired feeling” came over
him frequently ; his body was not supporting his
brain. I liked him for his leaning to literature ;
he did nothing in literature, but he thought he
could do something ; he wished to do something;
he took far more pleasure in books than in politics.
I have an essay on Brunton Stephens’ poems which
Byrnes read to the Brisbane Literary Association.
It is one of my measures of his niind; when a man
sits down to dig something out of himself and put
it on paper, you get his value as you would not
from a hundred speeches in which rhetoric is used
to gild commonplace. It is a good essay—rather
heavy, not over forceful, written in the style of
the man who talks. Byrnes talked well; he was
The late T. J. BYR NES, Premier of Queensland.
Photo., J. Hubert Newman.
no orator, but he was sensible and effective.
« * * »
Byrnes did not like to think that he owed any-
thing to his R.C. religion ; but he did. It cost
him. something, too ; but not as much as he gained
by it. _ His religion helped to decide Griffith’s
choice in 1890, when a Solicitor-General was
wanted. Macrossan and Thynne had dropped
out of the Cabinet, and a R.C. representative was
wanted to balance things. Later on, religion
gave him many active friends; and, except at
North Brisbane election, his religious opponents
took little trouble against him.
* * * *
I did not see much potentiality of political use-
fulness in Byrnes. To be frank, I saw none. He
had begun wrong ; had associated himself with
the corrupt Stagnation Party in Queensland—the
party which persistently opposes reform, progress;
partly because it does not understand what these
things are and imply, and has no sympathy with
them, and partly because its interests are bound
up . with the maintenance of the status quo,
which means office rewards, bank over-
drafts, Government funds diverted to personal
uses. Byrnes would never have had the strength
to sweep the Augean stables of these parasites on
the people. I doubt if he had the inclination. He
had a contempt for the mob, a just contempt; he
knew that it bites the hand which feeds it, stones
its prophets, ruins its well-wishers through fickle-
ness or ignorance. A Democratic politician must
not expect gratitude, must be ready to see his
greatest measures overthrown by those he de-
votes his life to help, and—must go on helping.
There is a fragment of Tourgenieff’s on the fool’s
judgment and the laughter of the crowd. “ A
man has done all that he could ; has worked stren-
uously, lovingly, honestly. . . . And honest hearts
turn from him in disgust ; honest faces burn with
indignation at his name. What is that man to do?
Go on working! not try to justify himself, and not
even look forward to a fairer judgment .” Alas !
that is beyond a man’s strength, unless he be of
the rarest nature.
■*•***
It was beyond Griffith’s strength. Griffith, as
a politician, honestly tried to act uprightly for
Queensland’s benefit. But he had bad tools, had
to work with treacherous allies. To carry one
point he had to give way on a dozen, and gradu-
ally the pitch defiled his own hands. He found
himself misrepresented and reviled by those he
wished to help; and, heartsick, he gave up
everything— and saved himself. Byrnes was less
conscientious than Griffith ; he was more like
cynical MTlwraith, who believed that the art of
politics is to gull the people—and succeeded. For
the present Byrnes was going to adopt “the
Nelson touch ” —he was going to do nothing, drift
and dribble, and try and consolidate his influence
and power. Then, I believe he hoped that
he could help reform, for he was liberal and open-
minded, did not covet money for its own sake, was
desirous of doing good in his day and generation.
• • • *
But his position was insecure; he had to con-
cede much ; he carried the compromise with evil
so far that there was practically no hope of good
left. He was really disgusted with the Q.N.
Bank influence in politics, but, when he came to
grapple with it, he found the influence too strong
for him ; and he had not pluck enough to cut him-
self adrift from his party and declare Democratic
war to the knife. Or, rather, he did not see how
it would pay him, and shirked the encounter ; he
valued himself first and Queensland next, though
he wished to benefit Queensland, if it could
be done with advantage, or even without
harm, to himself. So he would not widen
the franchise; he would not stop - colored
immigration; he countenanced, if he did
not encourage, the system of constituency-
bribery to keep his party in office. Like Griffith,
but without Griffith’s preliminary struggle he
was saving his own skin. His course was irre-
vocably taken ; there was nothing to expect from
him, save by accident; and his allies would have
fought hard to prevent accidents. So I saw no
Democratic hope in him.
* * * *
Nevertheless, I am sorry at his premature
death ; it seems unjust. A man’s life is his life,
to use or to spoil. One has a sense of wrong
when he is cut off in his prime. And
Byrnes was personally a good fellow. He
had a clever man’s contempt for the stupid people
with whom he was brought in contact, and often
he showed it and earned their resentment. He
was not tactful. And the habit of power, coming
early, had made him a little arrogant, despite his
good sense and good nature. He was intolerant
of opposition, especially of stupid or dishonest
opposition. I do not consider that his action in
taking the fees and emoluments he did from the
Government was that of a high-minded man. But
he had overwhelming precedents. In Queensland,
where the level of public life is as low as it can
well be, it is everyone’s business to loot the
Treasury. Byrnes might have secured a much
higher reward had he chosen. It does not matter
now.
A.G.S.
“ Peritonitis,” to The Bulletin :
“ Gouli-Gouli’s ” recent anecdote of the poor lady-
typist and her greedy medico touches on a burning sub-
ject in more ways than one. As a doctor in fair practice,
I have never yet been able to see the justice, from
either point of view, of the system of charging all
patients the same fee. In “ Gouii-Gouli’s ” instance, the
lady had only herself to blame, as, with such slight
ear-troubles, she should have visited some decent
general practitioner, and avoided the high-rent-pay-
ing consultant who runs the guinea to two-guinea show.
In N.S. Wales, either doctors should graduate their fees
according to the means of their patients, or patients
should avail themselves of medical facilities otherwise
provided for them. As for Victoria, every married man
under 50 joins the A.N.A., and all the rest of the male
population over 50 make their sons (if they have any)
join. The remuneration to the over-worked iodge-doctor
varies from 10s. to 14s. per member per annum, and in
my own city merchants, lawyers, and bankers have the
gall to become members, and calmly appropriate their
lodge-doctor’s services at the aforementioned munificent
figure. And yet, incomprehensible as it may seem, this
eBsentially-to-the-core sweating society has actually as
chief figureheads in the branches and on the metropolitan
committee, practising medical men who, apparently for
the delight of perpetual swelled-head, help to run this
miserable medical grinding-mill. Friendly societies
should be encouraged when honestly run as such for
people with limited incomes, but there ought to be a
legal squelching of pseudo friendly-societies which take
in all, rich and poor—and more especially of the A.N.A.,
which has made itself the curse of up-country pracl
tioners in Victoria.
Another royal prophecy has missed the
mark. Louis Philippe, in ’48, predicted that fifty
years later, there wouldn’t be a crowned head in
Europe.
“Malle”: It is mawkish sentimentality
bo howl about Kitchener’s action in having the
wounded Fuzzies, along the line of advance, dis-
patched. Perhaps the following extracts, from
my note-book of boyish experiences in ’B2, may
explain
A squad of dragoon guards had a brush with the enemy,
and were returning with some prisoners, when they saw
two apparently wounded men gesticulating to them. A
trooper rode over, and, finding they wanted water, dis-
mounted to fill his water-bottle in the canal. Whilst he
was doing so, the supposed wounded men jumped up,
shot him dead, and rushed for his horse. One got it, and
although pursued, escaped ; the other was cut to pieces.
D was one of the finest fellows in the corps.
Another: —
A wounded Egyptian officer asked one of our fellows for
water. Our man unslung his bottle and gave him a
drink, and, as he was reslinging it, the brute lifted his
revolver and shot him dead. He didn’t live long himself.
Sometimes the Gyps have lain down and pretended to be
wounded, and after we’ve passed them have jumped up and
fired on us from the rear. At Tel-el-Kebir our boys
blocked this game by bayonetting every man lying on the
field as they passed. There were a good many shammers.
A miner, who lately arrived in Melbourne
and happened to buy one of atheist Symes’ pam-
phlets, had his religious feelings so outraged
thereby that lie smashed Jos’s shop-window, and
mauled a lot more of the accursed literature.
An ungodly beak, however, charged him 255. for
the righteous deed.
Bull A-begging.
“ Ex- naval officer ” to The'Bulletin :
Chamberlain and Goschen are very anxious just now
for Australia to further subsidise Britain’s marine-murder
monsters. It is possible they take such a soulful interest
in Australia’s welfare that they desire to put our protec-
tion on a sure basis. It is possible, on the other hand,
that “ grab ” is the game, and that these eminent states-
men have been stimulated to covet our cash by the jingo-
whoops of absentee Australian fat men and the faint
echoes of the similar revelations we hear our side occa-
sionally. Which is only a circumstance either way.
The real question for us is: Do we get as much
protection for our money at present as we are
entitled to, and is Britain’s navy all it is cracked
up to be? Ali the world knows it is very fine
and large on paper. But ships are of no use
minus men, and just how British warships are
going to be manned by skilled crews, after a couple of
wholesale slaughterings in big actions, is a problem with
no present apparent solution. Mere intrepidity has no
chance in modern naval battles unless it is linked with
expert personnel ; a lesson that has just cost Spain very
dear. Of the many miles of ships paraded at the Jubilee
Review not half had their complement of men, were
efficiently boilered and engineered, or carried a complete
modern armament. And right here comes in one little
incident —which did not matter much because the game
of manoeuvres was not “ for keeps,” but which would have
mattered a lot in real hostilities. Thanks to a look out
vessel hoisting the signal “ west by north” in
place of “south-west,” the defending Channel squad-
ron steamed off to intercept the enemy in the
wrong direction. In another mimic battle, too,
the day was lost through the Edinburgh being only able
to dodder along at 10J4 knots I Gosciien’s special pet,
too, the Terrible, recently went out to break records on a
run to Gibraltar and back, and so sure was Goschen of i he
result that he actually went to Gibraltar to see the fun.
But instead of keeping up 20 knots of her computed 21,
the lame duck staggered back at a bare 15—didn’t even
get 20,000 h.p. out of the 25,000 she is supposed to develop.
This expensive machinery-box had previously tried to
keep up 15 knots for two hours ; but after 95 minutes her
starboard engine required a 3 hrs. and 25 min. rest. Then
she reeled off 140 knots in 7 hours but compensated for
this with a final crawl of 14 knots an hour. Tons
of money have been spent on this colossal failure
and her misfortunes can be paralleled with those
of many other similar Admiralty mistakes. The
Powerful, for instance, having to proceed as fast as
possible from England to Hongkong, actually arrived
there in about the time one of the old tea-clippers would
have taken to sail the distance —this through a trifling
miscalculation of her coal-capacity. Had she quietly re-
mained at anchor and only utilised her coal for working
her numerous minor engines and for condensing pur-
poses, she would have consumed, in the 40 odd days in
which she was supposed to do the voyage, the vhole of the
coal she is able to carry. And this without being pro-
pelled a single yard ! Again, as for armaments, obsolete
muzzle-loaders are quite common in the British na\y.
The Hercules, Sultan, Superb, Temeraire, and Monarch
have all been re-boilered and engined, but they
still retain their antique cannon, and in the case
of the equally out-of-date Bellerophon, the sagacious
naval authorities have given her up -to - date guns,
but have left in her her fossilised 11-knot engines.
In fact, while not a muzzle-loading gun is to be found in
the navies of France, Germany, Italy, Russia and Japan,
the B. navy is full of them High time, therefore, to ask
if our sea protection by Britain is quite such a certainty
as we fondly imagine. And still more imperative is it to
force upon Australian Governments —ere they even con-
sider Chamberlain and co.’s disinterested propositions—
the need of strict and searching enquiry'into the capa-
bilities of the squadron Australia now partly pays for.
Admiral Cervera’s experiences are not yearned for ‘his
part of the world.

Inspector Cullen, of the Maoriland police,
says there is less crime on the West Coast gold-
fields than in any other section of the country.
The churches are few and the schools many on the
Coast.
During the war fever at New York, every
dude wore a star-spangled banner in his coat or
hat-band even when going to church. But the
boss patriotic Johnny outdid all others by wearing
a Spanish flag in the vicinity of his lumbar region.

stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in, and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.

Though the Beltana Pastoral Co., in S.A.,
and the Momba Pastoral Co., in far-western
N.S.W., successfully breed their own camel herds
locally, imported Afghans are still employed to
work them. And one of the periodical agitations
against the camel and the camel-man is brewing.
Nine-tenths of the Perth tobacconists’
shops are run by foreign ladies, mostly Belgian,
but calling themselves French. In one street
there are seven tobacco-shops all in a row, which
furnishes the wicked man with food for reflection !
Eleven missionaries, four of them ladies,
left Sydney for China on Saturday week. Almost
every Australasian province was represented in
the contingent. The missionaries propose to ex-
press Baptist, Congregational, Presbyterian, and
Anglican phases of Christian faith—making four
different renderings of one fact.
A Westralian Parliamentary library is
being formed, and a large collection of Italian and
Spanish books has lately been bought for it in
Melbourne. Melb. Parliamentary Library is ex-
ceptionally rich in Italian and Spanish books
bought while James Smith was librarian, so W.A.
has followed suit. But one doesn’t fancy the
W.A. politicians—judging from the specimens ex-
hibited at the Federal Convention—reading Dante
and Cervantes. Deadwood Dicks would seem to
be more in their line.
“0.P.”: Noticing the general robustness
of butchers and slaughtermen, writer has struck a
theory, and hereby undertakes the physical re-
generation of this continent. The butcher fattens
on smell. Have been told that a smell is just as
solid and material as anything else—the smell of a
dead bullock is merely a number of infinitesimally
small junks of bullock floating about in the air
and waiting to lodge on somebody’s “smell”
membrane, and thence, presumably, get to the
stomach, for good or for evil according to the an-
tiquity of deceased. Now, as weak digestion,
with consequent debility, is the trouble with most
of us, and a man can get fat and vitalised upon
writer’s system without any digestion (provided
he smells long enough and judiciously), what’s the
matter with our breaking out forthwith as a nation
of Smellers; cultivating the practice in all schools,
hospitals, gaols, &c. ; giving big prizes at public
sniffing-competitions, and placing the large, fat,
big-beaked Champion Smeller of Australia on the
pedestal now occupied by the herring-gutted,
hump-backed, no-account cycling crank ?
Wild, wet Dunedin (M.L.) is likely to
become still more wet, as the Pacific shows a
strong inclination to roll right across Musselburgh
and St. Kilda into Otago harbor. For years the
authorities have squabbled over dour Atheism and
true-blue Presbyterianism while builders and
Chinese contractors carted the sand away from
Ocean Beach. Now the sand-hills have gone, and
when gales pile the waves on Ocean Beach the
flood-waters swamp the houses on the Flat and
flow off into the harbor. Unless something is
done quickly, the damp Edina of the South will
wake up some rainy morning and find two of its
suburbs gone and a whale preaching in First
Church pulpit.
Verb sap. for Melbourne University Coun-
oil:
When we are permitted to speak about the origin of
virtue and religion without employing whispetable and
circuitous language, and with the same freedom as we
now speak about our mental or motive temperaments,
the population problem will be virtually solved. The
wildest of all abstractions is the idea that our bodies are
dotted with shame-spots, and that we must act as if we
had no natural instincts. This abstraction has driven us
to the most shameful extremes of immorality.—W. A.
MacDonald in “ Humanitism.”
A Westralian lady aged 21 is charged with
being the champion blackmsHfer of her province,
According to the story told in court she obtained
credit from a Hindoo draper, and when Ram
Chunder wanted his money she threatened to
“ make it hot ” for him. Three days later a letter,
purporting to be signed by an infuriated brother,
struck the Hindoo like an avalanche. It stated
that the lady had just died as the result of an
illegal operation performed on her by the Hindoo,
who was a perfectly peaceful and respectable
draper and probably hadn’t the faintest idea how
to do such a thing; that she had perished after great
expense confessing the horror in her last moments ;
that it had cost £lO to square the doctor; and that
nothing but the immediate despatch of £55 to
a certain P.O. address could hush the matter
up. Then, apparently, Ram Chunder or his solicitor
went round to look at the building where the £55
was to be sent, and found the corpse there waiting
for her money. This was the tale told in the
police court, with the addition that the lady had
no enraged brother, and had been in perfect health
all the time. The Hindoo’s name wasn’t Ram
Chunder, but so many Asiatics are named Ram
something or other that that is as good as any
other.

with the result that planters along said route were
circularised in a confidential manner, to the effect
that, since H.E. was a staunch total abstainer, nis
entertainers would banish pro tern, from their
tables the accursed thing in every form ; but that
a supply of the milk of green cocoanuts would be
very acceptable to H.E. _ Dutifully, there-ore,
each planter produced his ayer klaper and
heroically the big man swallowed it every time,
till at length he struck Stephens on the confines or
Malacca and the Negri Simbilan. The hospitality
of Stephens was proverbial, and though it was
capable of sacrifice to the extent of giving cocoanut
milk to a guest, he would scarce go the length or
sharing in the consumption thereof, and a nip of
whisky m a glass of milk was invisible. A glass
so furnished fell by accident to H.E. ; and, as he
set it down, empty, his eyes glistened, and he
thanked heaven to find m that benighted End one
man who didn’t despise the noblest gift of God.
“For two weeks,” he said, plaintively, I have
had nothing to drink but that thnce-accursed
cocoanut milk.” A fortnight later a host of
planters were seeking for the hoaxer’s blood, with
every assistance from an infuriated Excellency-
wko could have known all about it by applying to
the present scribe.

POLITICAL
POINTS
British New Guinea is larger by 3000 sq.
miles than the province of Victoria.
“ A number of insolvent scoundrels are
already preparing themselves for the coming elec-
tion.”—Queensland paper.
Alleged on unreliable authority that one
Australian leader of a Parliamentary Opposition
hasn’t been sober even by accident for five months.
Yic. Attorney-General Isaacs on the
Argus : “ I desire no support from that quarter.
It is generally the kind of support that the hang-
man’s rope gives to its victim.”
Westralian Govt., forced to economy, has
cut off all subsidy from some way-back goldfields
hospitals—Bui ong among them ! And so, of
course, has made more friends.
The Q. Mines Bill, which has many good
Democratic points, is damned by its sweeping con-
cessions to foreign investors. Boodle clauses will
be resolutely stormed by the Opposition ; Demo-
cratic clauses by Ministerialists. Between them
the Bill will probably be wrecked, and the pro-
vince thrown into the throes of a general election
—now bound to come soon, anyhow.
A Kempsey (N.S.W.) correspon-
dent writes to say that, while the late
election was on, the Kempsey Agricul-
tural Society received an offer from
the Government of the loan of an im-
ported red bull on moderate terms.
(Minister-f or-VegetablesSmith officially
imported these bulls, and as Smith was
standing for Macleay, this sudden forc-
ing of Smith’s bull on the notice of the
electors was an earnest appeal to the
agricultural soul.) But Smith wasn’t
elected, and promptly came another
notice that the red bull wasn’t avail-
able. The notice was dated two days
before the election, but the postmark
showed that it wasn’t posted until after
Smith’s rejection. The Kempsey corre-
spondent’s red-bull story ends just here.
_ Apropos N.S. W. Medical Practi-
tioners Bill. The profession would, of
course, like to impose legal penalties on
any undiplomaed person practising. It
would be good to enact such a law. But
as.N.S.W. public opinion is averse to
this, the reasonable and imperative com-
promise is to enact that no person
shall be allowed to pose as possessing
a qualification to which he is not
entitled. The public, when they
consult an amateur doctor, have
a right to know that he is not
technically “ qualified.” When
the law has provided for this, it
can let John Smith take care of
his own carcase. If Smith likes to
believe that he can be cured of organic
heart disease, or cancer, or phthisis, by
bread-pills or horehound-tea_ —well, that ia
John Smith’s own funeral.
The vehicles used in the procession of
welcome to Barton at Sydney the other night
were supplied by a firm of undertakers. Ye gods !
what an omen ! Has humor utterly departed from
the world !
Brisbane Telegraph, commenting on the
iniquity of the working classes, says that in six
years Queensland has lost £150,000 through
strikes. Just think of that! And the Q. N.
Bank alone lost more than that by one Fat Man’s
overdraft.
Sydney D.T. remarked superciliously that
the news of Barton’s return for the Hastings and
Macleay was received with no enthusiasm in the
city. Why should it have been ? The result was
never doubtful. Nobody grows enthusiastic over
dead certainties.
One Protectionist ex-M.L.A. considered
himself so safe at the recent N.S.W. general elec-
tion that he resolved not to spend a cent, and
actually painted his own calico signs. This in-
censed the “ working man,” and the cocksure one
found himself “ left ” when the numbers went up.
How Barton must curse the “eloquent”
blatherer who labelled him “ Australia’s noblest
son.” He may be Australia’s noblest son, but
how absurd to say such a thing—except beside a
grave! And as to the people who talk about God
taking this side, or that side, on any given politi-
cal question, they should be smothered.
The now famous “ Johnny” Wallace lifted
his big, shaven, stubborn chin, the other night, in
Victoria’s House of 48 Rich Landlords to demand
that legacies to charitable institutions should be
exempted from probate-duty, though a trifling
sum left to the widow should pay duty as usual.
Considering the horrors of most charitable institu-
tions, it is pleasing to note that the idea fell
through.
Be the defeat of the motion by Griffith,
M.L.A., in N.S.W. Assembly, for a plebiscite on
the question of partial opening of hotels on Sun-
days. The cold-tea brigade clamors for Local
Option, the same thing as the plebiscite, only on a
smaller scale, and say it is “ trusting the people.”
But when the same means are proposed to relieve
publicans the people, it seems, are not fit to be
trusted.
Premier Reid in his bad temper constantly
accuses N.S.W. Opposition of being “ after the
beans.” But the most ardent.Oppositionist will
have some difficulty in catching up to Yes-no’s
record of 13 furious motions of censure in two
years, presumably in search of the same vegetable
product.
Premier Reid was wont to boast of how
his “jolly and fat” disposition had helped him
along, but the gradual souring of his temper has
been very noticeable since the rise of Barton, and
the trials of the last few weeks have made No-Yes
so irritable that everyone is aware how sadly he
fears political extinction.
One N.S.W. politician makes great way in
his electorate by kissing all the small children.
He has probably kissed every infant in his elector-
ate at least twice over. It is a grimy district, and
several cases of leprosy have arisen there. Yet
that member has been put on the Refreshment
Committee of the House.
Now that the limelight is thrown strongly
°n Minister Yfcrang, he stands exposed in
all his remarkable political nakedness, and makes
an outsider inquire how ever such an ineffably
nervous, commonplace, and uneloquent man suc-
ceeds to positions like that of Speaker of the
Assembly or Minister of the Crown ?
Westralia charges a duty of 20s. per ton
on potatoes, but Premier John Forrest admitted
the other day in Parliament that at Fremantle
(the port of Perth) only 18s. is charged, the other
2s. being illegally knocked off as discount by Big
John’s Government. The Forrest party’s great
desire is to bring trade to Perth, where it has
much property, and it isn’t at all particular how
the thing is done.
Of the 134,000 S.A. Assembly electors
only 45,000 have a vote for the Council, so that
there is far to go ere the democratic idea is
reached. If Kingston’s Household Suffrage
Bill is passed
another 65,000
men and women
will get the fran-
chise for the
Upper House.
HIGHLY SATISFACTORY.
FRIEND OF THE FAMILY : “ Well, Margery, how
are you getting on with your music? ”
MARGREY : "Oh, splendid! I'm my teacher's
favorite pupil. ”
FRIEND : “ Ah! and how many pupils has he? ”
MARGERY : “ One.”
Cook, N.S.W. Minister for Fuel, at
Wallsend : “ The Govt, are not afraid of Mr.
Barton being in the House ; in fact, they thought
he would have more Federal influence if he stayed
outside.” They are afraid of Federal influence,
then. Most people thought so, but hardly ex-
pected one of the party of protestations to blurt
it out. Probably Jocook didn’t know it was
loaded, and Reid will now provide him with a
muzzle.
Maloney and Trenwith vainly tried, when
the Pilot estimates were before Vic. Assembly,
to get a condition inserted against Chinese cooks
being employed on pilot-vessels. The pilots say
that they can’t get a white man to do the work.
As it is only a matter of wages, surely the Turner
Govt, could have followed Premier Kingston’s
splendid initiative in the matter of coolie-manned
coaster colliers, and insisted on the employment of
white labor.
When the charges against Minister Young
were being discussed in N.S.W. Assembly, Mr.
O’Conor said : “ Why does not the Minister bring
an action for libel?” To which Premier Reid :
“ That was a petty junior barrister—the man who
hoped to get a brief on a trial some day.” That
very day the minute had been submitted to the
Governor conferring the title Q.C. on Reid, and
thus lifting him out of the contemned junior bar-
rister sphere.
The Age is trying to stir up Melbourne to
amalgamate its 20 corporations and make a res-
pectable, solvent municipality out of the lot. Its
case is hardly so awful as that of Sydney, with a
rather smaller population and 41 municipal
governments ; but it is bad enough. The pocket-
handkerchief-municipality disease is so bad in both
cities that an agile thief can bolt through two or
three cities and a few boroughs before even a
swift policeman runs him down.
Statement made by a Sydney daily that
members of N.S.W. Labor-party begin to see
hitherto-unsuspected merits in the Barton brand
of Federation puckers The Bulletin’s face with
a sardonic smile. The real fact is that the labor-
ists are coming to the opinion that the Reid Govt.,
even with the Labor support, is not a stable con-
cern, while, on the other hand, Barton, with their
help, could guarantee them security in their par-
liamentary positions for three years.
FURTHER INFORMATION.
FRIEND OF THE FAMILY (who has just told a spicy yarn) :
“ Do you think your wife can have heard me in the
next room? ”
HOST : “ I'll tell you to-morrow, old man.”
Queensland tobacco-manufacturers are
trying to bite the State’s ear for £15,000 a year to
be spent in bonuses ; £2O a ton on all weed ex-
ported. The manufacturer—not the farmer—
does the exporting, so he would get the bonus.
And it is sheer foolishness to pay to promote an
export trade when Queensland hasn’t yet got
within many miles of supplying local require-
ments. Also, it is more foolishness to take money
from the tobacco-people with one hand as excise,
and then give it back with the other as bonus.
The short way is to spend the £15,000 in abolish-
ing the excise.
Crick’s activity in the present N.S.
Wales Assembly is in marked contrast to his
apathy in the last. In his electorate (West Mac-
quarie) he is invariably beaten by a handsome
majority in the large centres, the returns from
which come in first; but this result is always re-
versed by figures from the outskirts. Thus, for
the last three or four elections, first returns showed
him to be hopelessly beaten. While his opponents
are throwing up their hats with joy, a very well-
known Sydney sportsman goes round and takes
long odds that Crick’s not beaten.
Storey, M.L.A., in N.S.W. Assembly,
after protesting his Federal enthusiasm :
So far as the Federal Capital is concerned it should be
in N.S.W., and personally, I think in Sydney. If we made
Wagga or Albury the capital, when the border duties are
removed the trade will go to Victoria.
Yet if anyone else asks that the capital shall be at
Adelaide or Hobart or Melbourne, otherwise the
trade will go to N.S.W., Storey recognises at
once that their shameless greed renders Federa-
tion impossible. “I am perfectly fair and un-
biassed. All I want is the whole earth, and I
must have it.”
Dan’l Cooper seems to have become a sort
of official lone fisherman. Saul Samuel got 12
months’ leave of absence on full-pay, and Premier
Reid, bent on keeping down expenses, kicked at
paying another A.-G. salary during that period.
Which simply meant that the office should stand
vacant or be filled by someone willing to work for
nothing—a rather contemptible situation for a
high office of State. But now the year is up, and
Saul’s screw has stopped, and still no A.-G. is ap-
pointed. Reid says Dan’l is ready to go on for
another couple of months—but for what? The
reason for not paying him hitherto was that Saul
was drawing full salary ; now that he is drawing
nothing, surely Dan’l should come under pay.
N.S.W. doesn’t want Baronet Dan’l’s services
for nothing.
SHOW SKETCH.—FORBIDDEN FRUIT.
If Barton beats Reid by a narrow majority
and takes office, the N.S.W. Labor-party will
have an enormous power and a corresponding
responsibility. They can maintain Barton and
the House without dissolving, or they can throw
him out and precipitate a dissolution ; or they can
split. They will be in a somewhat analogous posi-
tion to the pioneer N.S.W. Labor-party, which had
a big responsibility and no experience. The Liquid-
arities have experience, and ought to be able to
stand the strain better. Still, Barton would
hardly feel comfortable in holding power by the
good-will of a “political dog that bites ’’—having
bitten its last owner—unless he can muzzle the
dog !
“Spud” Young’s peculiar tac-
tics at the recent bye-election will
tend to force on what all „T HE Bulle-
tin sermons for years have failed to
accomplish the bringing about of
N.S.W. local government. Here is a
ludicrous instance of the “ our ener-
getic local member ” farce. The 2d.
section of Balmain tram ended at
Forest Lodge. By energetic deputa-
tions and other means, M.L.A.
Mahony got it extended to Johnston-
st., Annandale. Now Govt, whip
Hawthorne wants it stretched a quar-
ter of a mile on to Abattoirs-road.
When this succeeds —and “Johnny”
never fails to benefit his own crowd
at the general tax-payer’s expense—
M.L.A. Law and his deputations will
clamor to have the 2d. journey go
as far as Roselle. Then Bill Wilks
will be expected to drag it down a
few hundred yards into the other
Balmain division. When that is
accomplished, Clark, North Shore
M.L.A., will probably want to know
about the fares in his electorate.
The 737 persons on the eight
village settlements on the Murray in
S.A. have received during the last
four years more than £70,000 in
State aid ! At the end of Aug.
they had 6585 acres under crop, of
which 6019 was in cereals, and at last
it looks as though they would be
able to support themselves. When
the Tories attack the settlements,
the Govt, reminds Downer and co. of
the vast sums spent in the past on
relief works which have now no value
at all.
Jawbone Neild, M.P., transparently and
obviously drew a political red-herring across the
trail of retributive Justice on Roads-and-Bridges
Minister Young, and has probably made his peace
with the Reid- Telegraph combination for his
absence from the censure division. Neild is a man
who never gets into Ministries. Young is a man
who always gets into them. Why should the one
be taken when the other is left? Why are not both
left? Parkes, in his last Ministry, and Reid in
his two, left J. C. Neild out, contrary to a general
understanding that office would be offered a,t the
first opportunity.
The recently-deceased N.S.W. branch of
the National Ass was doomed from its very in-
ception. Its first reach-out, the ridiculous attempt
to give an extra vote to Patman under the eupho-
nious title of the “ thrift-vote,” was received with
derision; but the summit of its asininity was
reached when it took upon itself the “duty” of
purging the rolls— i.e., examining into the fran-
chise rights of those not likely to be of correct
political color. This brought on its devoted ears
universal execration, and parliamentary candi-
dates secretly favorable to its views regarded its
open support as inimical to their chances of elec-
tion. That the capitalists of N.S.W. continued
to waste money for seven years on a manifestly
useless institution is indeed a satire on money-bag
intelligence.
The present session should be long remem-
bered in Maoriland. It has been one bitter
stonewall with day-and-night sittings and streaks
of lurid language, and the work done has been
practically nil. The Tory Opposition has sought
to demonstrate, among other things, that Premier
Seddon was partner with a Chinaman in a mining
spec. 20 years ago, and that the Chow called him
Bun Tuck ; also, that a long-dead relative of Sed-
don’s was once a corporation employe, and got
more wages than he should have done through
some accounts being cooked. Probably, this un-
clean dragging-up of dead relatives will do a little
to bring the Elective Executive idea nearer. The
object of it is to get the Ministry out of office, and
if each Minister is elected by the House for a fixed
term, and at the end of it his portfolio becomes
vacant and there is a fresh election, then there
will be no need to disinter anybody’s late brother
so as to bring about a chance of a change of Minis-
try. The chance will come round automatically.
T. Smith, M.L A., somewhat startled Vic.
Assembly t’other night by quoting the case of a
vigneron who onetime planted grape vines “ for
the sake of the Govt, bonus,” and then, having
“ pocketed the money,” proceeded to uproot them
all and plant wheat, or something, in their stead.
Smith, a Nonconformist hatter by persuasion,
told the tale as a warning against the folly of
bonuses, especially bonuses for grape-growing.
The snuffling Smith made so great a mouthful of
the alleged vineyard atrocity that the Viticultural
Board called for explanations. Then it appeared
that the “ bonus-grabber ” had planted eight acres
with raisin-grape vines, which proved to be of the
wrong kind, and, after experimenting with them
for three long years, he pulled them up as a bad
job. Meanwhile he had spent much more than
the ,£24 bonus in the purchase of a raisin-drying
apparatus, not to mention the waste of land and
labor. Smith’s revelation was typical of temper-
ance attempts to discredit everything and every-
body concerned in the accursed Drink industry.
The Kingston Government gained a great
triumph in carrying the second reading of its
Household Suffrage Bill by 32 to 17 in S.A.
Assembly. The measure extends the Leg.
Council franchise to between 60,000 and 70,000
citizens, but its defeat is certain in the Coun-
cil, which now contains a solid Tory party
with a small majority. As a compromise, the
Govt, offers, if opponents consent to vote for
the Bill, to make its enactment contingent on a
successful referendum.
Premier Kingston is introducing in the
Leg. Council all the W.C.T.U. legislation to
which his Government is committed. And, so far,
every ‘ ‘ moral ” measure submitted has got through
without much trouble. The only one of the Bills
which has been talked about in the Assembly, so
far, is that entitling a woman to recover £2O
towards the expenses of her confinement either
before or after the illegitimate baby is born.

The Delicate Relations of Husband
and Wife.
King O’Malley’s Bill providing that when the
propertied old man dies his widow and children
shall have'a legal claim (no matter what direc-
tions are contained in his will) on enough of
the pile to keep them in comfort, was jumped
on both by the S.A. Premier and the leader of
the Opposition :
Mr. Kingston opposed the motion, as the tendency was
now to give the fullest power to a testator, and because
no specific cases of injustice had been mentioned. He
agreed that a maintenance-order obtained during the life
of a testator should continue in force after death, as
against the estate.
Mr. John Downer urged that the children of testator,
whether legitimate or illegitimate, should have the same
claim on the estate after death as in life, but declined to
interfere with the delicate relations of husband and wife.
Which is just as strictly logical as a Chinese
opera. If the old woman has quarrelled with
the old man, and separated from him, and got
a maintenance-order, and he dies during
the currency thereof, Kingston reckons that
she should have a claim on the estate for the
income specified in the order. But if she
wasn’t cantankerous enough for that, then she
can be left without a penny while the whole
pile is bequeathed to the church. Therefore it
is her plain duty to aggravate the old man
into blackening her eye, so that she can get a
maintenance-order. And Downer, who is
just as illogical in this matter as Kingston,
reckons that the old lady shopld be liable to be
left homeless and penniless in her old age lest
the “delicate relations” of husband and wife
should be interfered with and shaken up.
Yet the O’Malley scheme would only interfere
after the old man’s death, and what relations
there could be between the widow and the
remains (unless the lady was a vampire) is a
question which Downer couldn’t answer if he
lived to be as old and stupid as Methusaleh.
Moreover, if the relations were so strained that
the old man deliberately fixed things that the
old woman was to starve after his death, then
they couldn’t be made much worse if they were
attacked with dynamite. Supposing Downer
found the live old man, instead of the dead one,
turning his wife out to starve, would he decline
to interfere with the delicate relations of hus-
band and wife then ? And, if he wouldn’t, why
wouldn’t he ?
Cheap Prohate Law.
“ Demos,” to The Bulletin :
That interesting little Vic. experiment in the direction
of free law, the Intestate Widows Relief Act 1889, to
which reference has been made in your columns, was
originally suggested by two English Acts of 1873 and 1875.
But whereas the English Acts apply to the widows and
children of intestates and the children of their widows
only in regard to estates under £lOO, the Vic. Act applies
both to those who make a will and those who don’t, and
gives any person entitled to apply for either probate or
letters of administration the benefit of the Act. It is,
therefore, a much more important experiment ; and
it is gratifying to note that its provisions are being
eagerly availed of throughout the province. In
Melbourne the applications number hundreds, while
about 100 applications a year are put through from each
of the larger centres, such as Bendigo, Ballarat, &c.
Though many cases are still dealt with by solicitors, to
the credit of'many of them it should be said that they
refer estates within the limit of the Act to the local
County Court. There the forms are filled up and sent to
Melbourne, where the application is considered by the
Registrar of Probates. The total cost for a simple pro-
bate of £5OO is 355., but so many wills are drawn in-
formally at the last moment that extra affidavits are
often required, particularly when the local parson or
school-teacher has taken a hand in the matter. The
defects of the Act are that it is not retrospective, and
that only the gross estate is considered. An estate of
£6OO, with £2OO debts, is outside the Act, though the net
amount is £lOO below the maximum. Something
should be done to remedy such anomalies and further
simplify procedure. Registration should be sufficient to
enable a widow or next of kin to obtain administration
without having to find sureties, and so on. Certainly, an
amendment of the Administration Act enables the Regis-
trar to dispense with sureties in the case of small
estates, but, as in most other discretionary powers in
the hands of court officials, the provision is not as flexible
as it looks.
Another late Vic. Act, from an English source, allows
a childless intestate’s widow the net estate up to £lOOO,
or a charge of £lOOO where net value exceeds that sum.
It lacks, however, a provision enabling the widow to get
administration cheaply or to dispense with a bond. She
must go to a lawyer, who naturally makes the larger
estates pay for the smaller ones he loses under the other
Act.
The difficulties in this respect, and the affidavit-
troubles alluded to above, give point to the contention
that a public officer should be appointed to prepare wills
in a ship-shape form, and advise officially, and without
ruinous cost, about all the details of probate and adminis-
tration.

Prof. Walters (U.S.) furnishes N.Y. Sun
with a paper on an unprecedented mass of skele-
tons, just turned up in excavating for an extension
of Kansas City Railroad. He says that about
75,000 men were buried there, at one time, and the
dints on skulls show it was after a battle. The
period would not be less than 20,000 years ago.
J. L. Jacobsen has maintained his Aus-
tralian chess supremacy by winning the cham-
pionship contest from W. H. Jonas —scoring seven
wins to Jonas’s one, with five drawn. Jacobsen
and Wallace, the former champion, are next ex-
pected to try conclusions, after a reasonable inter-
val.
Westralia is about to start a Zoo at Perth,
and a son of Mr. Le Soeuf, of Melb. Zoo, has been
appointed director. To do justice to the fauna of
the country, Perth Zoo’s wild-cat department will
have to be very large.
The entry in the journal of Capt. Matthew
Flinders (Feb. 7, 1802), re the naming of Denial
Bay on the west coast of S.A. (near the isles of
St. Peter on Nuyt’s chart), states that the title
was given “as well in allusion to St. Peter as to
the deceptive hope we had formed of penetrating
by it some distance into the interior country.”
In the course of a few days The Bulletin
will publish Will Ogilvie’s book of verses, “Fair
Girls and Gray Horses,” for which a warm wel-
come is already assured. There is no man on the
lengthening list of Australian bush bards who has
a tenderer touch or a truer heart-beat. The price
of the book, handsomely bound in cloth, will be
4s. 6d., post free, and orders sent in at once will
be attended to immediately on publication.
The kink in Q. politics gets an additional
twist through Premier Byrnes’ death. Neither
“ Oily ” Dickson nor renegade Philp can command
a sufficiently servile following. The only apparent
loophole is that, avoiding other contentious mat-
ters, the House will pass the Estimates (already
under way) and then let the present Parliament die
a natural death. The most virile and unscrupulous
men—the MTlwraiths, Griffiths, Nelsons, Tozers
—have feathered their nests and retired, and the
Remnant is the quintessence of ineptitude.
James Dickson (“ Oily Jim ”), the new Q.
Premier, is a man with political brains and no
political character. He will be 66 years of age in
November and he has shed so much hair
already that by the time he is 76 an egg will be a
cross-bred fleece compared with his cranium. He
was Minister of Works in ’76, Col. Treasurer
under Griffith in ’B3, and acting-Premier while
Griffith was being nobbled at the Imperial Con-
ference of ’B7. Nobody likes him ; nobody politi-
cally trusts him ; but Q. Parliament is so short of
brains that Dickson has come in handy time and
again. And he can talk ; Philp can’t. A Premier
must be ready to attack, debate, reply ; and Philp
needs three days’ preparation for a speech. Hence,
probably, his rejection.
Victor Daley writes : I have been glancing
at an advance copy of Ogilvie’s volume, “Fair
Girls and Gray Horses.” In my opinion it will
be the most charming book of Australian verse
that has yet appeared. For Ogilvie can not only
write ringing rhymes that make the blood spin
through the veins ; he can also sing dainty and
elegant songs, which remind me at times of the
cavalier songs written between a battle and a
bouquet. There is much of the cavalier, and, as
becomes his name, not a little of the moss-trooper
in Ogilvie. He writes with a galloping heart.
But, above all, there is the passionate bloom of
youth in his verses. He looks out upon the world
around him and finds it fair and young, and
makes it so for his readers by the magic of his
rhyme. Sadness is not his metier. If I were
asked to name the source of his literary inspira-
tion I would say— the old Scottish ballads. He is
essentially lyrical in his manner. His songs need
no musical setting. They provide their own
music.
The last single action fought by a British
warship was in 1878, between H.M. composite
cruiser Shah and the insurgent Peruvian ironclad
Huascar, which had been seized during one of the
periodical local revolutions. The Huascar had
been stopping British vessels and relieving them
of the trouble of carrying coals without the trifling
formality of payment. So the Shah, Admiral
de Horsey, arranged to present the bill. On
paper, the Huascar ought to have sunk the Shah
in one round, for she could manoeuvre more
quickly, and was more heavily armored and armed.
Moreover, one of the Shah’s two best guns went
wrong at the start, forcing the vessel to keep her
antagonist always on the same side. Several
times the Huascar tried to ram, but was beaten
off by the gatling and other fire. Eventually she
made off, sound in hull and engines, but badly
battered otherwise, and took refuge in Iquique,
where the Shah made two abortive attempts to
torpedo her. A funny incident of this fight was,
that, while the fiery little Admiral was lecturing
a young marine for ducking at a shot, another
shot whizzed so close to the great man himself
that he also made his bow —and shut up.
Brandt Bros., of Sydney and Melbourne,
are about to patent and place on the market a
contrivance for making ice. It is about the size
of an ordinary bucket. Water is poured into the
centre cylinder around which is an acid, and by
turning the handle for 10 minutes a block of ice
is produced. This may be repeated several times
before the power of the acid is exhausted. It is
claimed that a child can manage the machine.

INCLUSION OF BENEFIT AND ASSUR-
ANCE FUNDS.
Committee of management propose
that the following funds be Incorpor-
ated and established in connection with the
Institute :
(1) A Mortuary Benefit Fund. —To
assure a sum payable at a member’s death to
whomsoever he may appoint as his beneficiary.
(2) A Widows’ and Orphans’ Fund.—
For the assistance of the necessitous widows and
orphans of members.
(3) A Loss of Employment Indemnity
Fund. —To make certain fixed weekly allowances
of money to support members out of employment.
(4) A Permanent Helief Fund, styled the
Comrades’ Help Fund, for the relief chiefly by
loan of necessitous banking men who are aged, or
incapacitated from earning a subsistence, or
unable to find employment.
(5) A Fund for Scholarships to be com-
peted for by children of members.

My dear Mr. Maly on, —
I have used your ointment Ligniolliene with distinct
success in my own ease, but with greater as an emollient
unguent for the face of a young lady suffering from
“ Acne,” very common during puberty, and you should
push it in that direction. Kindly send me another box
of it.—Yours truly, GEO. D. WOOLEY, M.D.
WHAT THE DOCTORS SAY:—
Doveton-street, Ballarat,
Mv dear Mr. Malyon, — August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record pro-
gress with regard to ray old enemy “ Eczema Capitas,”
from which I suffered, notwithstanding constant treat-
ment for some years under the prescriptions of Pro-
fessor M‘Call Anderson and other eminent derma-
tologists, with only partial and temporary relief.
When I met you I commenced somewhat sceptically
your ointment Ligniolliene, and after about two months’
treatment I may honestly sa.y my enemy is defeated,
and lam perfectly well. I will only add that since I
commenced the treatment of myself I have prescribed it
to others, and am still doing so with brilliant results.
Please accept my hearty thanks, and remain yours sin-
cerely, THOS. LE GAY HOLTHOUSE, M.RiC.S., L.

Fred. Shaw, the Sydney midget cyclist, has
struck a winning gait in Queensland against the northern
cracks. At Maryborough he made the Queenslanders
look like a bowl of bread and milk struck with a club.
The judge saw that “crimson streak” several times
during the day as Shaw, on his Red Bird “ Special,” won
the important events.*
* * * *
The following is a new clause on the Melbourne
Bicycle Club’s Austral entry-form “ I declare that the
machine I intend to ride possesses no mechanical advan-
tage to accelerate speed beyond what is commonly known
and used.” Is the Melbourne Club frightened that
some embryo Edison will slip in and win the Austral
Wheel Race with some patent gear? How would this
clause affect a rider who rode with a pair of the Dunlop
Co.’s new '99 tyre ?
* * * *
Ben Goodson, the Queensland amateur cham-
pion, established a record last Saturday at the Sydney
Amateur Race Meeting, when, starting (on Dunlops) from
scratch six times, he succeeded in winning every race.
This is the best afternoon’s work yet accomplished by
an Australian cyclist, although W. Martin ran this per-
formance very close on the same track in Sydney two
years back.*
* • * *
The English one-hour track record has been
raised by A. A. Chase, who, paced by the crack Dunlop
pacing teams, succeeded in riding 33 miles 712 yards in
60 minutes on the Crystal Palace track, London.*
* * * *
By winning the 50 miles Vic. amateur road
championship last Saturday on Dunlop tyres W. E.
Shrimpton has now secured the dual honor of having won
the two Vic. amateur championships for 98-9.*
* * * *

SPORTING NOTIONS,
As showing what sort of business the Adelaide
books did in the old days, a correspondent sends us Bob
Sutton’s list for the A.R.C. Park meeting of June 1, 1882.
The list invited punters to back their fancies before the
day on terms that read like a joke. The Midwinter Han-
dicap, for instance, had 18 entries, and Sutton’s prices
were 2 to 1 against the favorite, 4 to 1 against four
others, 6,7, and 8 to 1 against three more of them, and
10 to 1 the “ outsiders.” They had the tote in S.A. at
that time, yet there must have been some fools who
rushed in to take these early odds, or Sutton wouldn’t
have issued his circular. Since then the Adelaide public
has learned the lesson of the tote, and the early bird
gets no more worms.
* * • *
“M. ” writes from' Adelaide “ Your pars, on
the tote question afford me a deal of interest. I have
been mixed up in racing in Australia for close on five-and-
twenty years, and have been in the thick of it in Victoria
and N.S.W., as w 7 ell as here, so know a little about the
strength of the game. When racing reached its low ebb
here, during the 80’s, the tote was urged as the reason,
no credit for the downfall being given to the proper and
real cause, reckless land speculation. Experience teaches
me that tbe tote is the only fair means (to the backer) of
speculation on racing, for, in the case of the favorite
getting home, the machine invariably pays a better price
than that on offer by Ikey Mo, and, when an outsider gets
home, why the tommy-rooks are distanced. lam not able
to supply .you with the bookmakers’ quotations on races
held in tbisprovince for straight-out betting on the course,
because the law prohibits such, but you will find enclosed
a statement giving the tote prices against every horse
that started at the Port races on Saturday, Sept. 17 —of
use to you when making further comparisons between the
tote and the book.” Tiie Bulletin will quote only two
cases from the list. In another case, the winner started
as sixth favorite (20 runners) at 15J to 1, the odds against
tl of the runners ranging from 25 to 450 to I. Tote odds :
Trial Stakes (18 starters)—Winner (4th favorite), 7 j to 1;
Ist favorite, sto 1; 2nd do., 6i- to 1; 3rd do., 6J to 1;
tw»o others would pay over 9£ to” 1; one other, 12 to 1 ;
another, 14 to 1; the remaining 10 would pay from 25 to
240 to 1. Steeplechase (eight starters) —Winner (4th
favorite), 6 to 1; Ist favorite, 7to 4 ; 2nd do., 4J to 1;
3rd do., 6to 1; one other, 6to 1; the three outsiders
would pay from 15 to 55 to 1.
* * * »
“ W.N.” : From personal knowledge and a con-
siderable amount of experience as secretary of a racing
club in South Australia, permit me to congratulate The
Bulletin on its advocacy of the totalisator. People will
gamble—and why shouldn’t they ?—and there is no fairer
way than that of the tote. The 20-mile radius, however,
in force here makes the thing a perfect farce. An
instance or two will show you why. Port Augusta, by'
paying a guinea for a license and £1 for police super-
vision, is allowed the privilege of using the tote, while
Quorn—a much more populous centre, and the other side
of Flinders Range -is refused a license because it doesn’t
happen to be 20 miles away' as the crow flies. Still worse.
An old-established club at Port Pirie is refused a license
because Laura—l9(4 miles from Pirie, and on the opposite
side of Flinders Range—got one first. Port Pirie has a
population of as many thousands as Laura has hun-
dreds. Same thing applies in many other places, but a
special Act has been passed so that the clubs in and
around Adelaide may not suffer by the stupid radius
enactment.
• * * •
It isn’t out of friendship for the champagne-
swilling birds of prey that Labor-member Hancock labors
to defeat the Tote Bill in the Vic. Assembly'. Perish the
thought! Hancock opposes the tote in the interests of
the young. He believes that it would encourage betting,
and although he himself started to bet at an early age,
and hopes to continue the pernicious practice until his
dying day, or thereabouts, his sense of duty to his
adopted country impels him to fight like a demon for
Ikey Mo’s monopoly. Hancock in this matter is a pure
patriot, and deserves to he recognised as such. Ikey Mo
recognised him as such by inviting him to the Victorian
Club’s smoke-night festival last week, and Hancock
turned up, with a halo round his head—by way of offering
further protest against the accursed machine, no doubt.
* » * *
Hancock’s brother legislators at that smoke-
night were Zox, Theodore Fink, and Nat Levi (of the
Council). Frank Madden was not present in the flesh,
but a front seat was reserved for his soul.
* * * *
“ Timi ”: The backer of the racehorse D. O’D.
when it paid the sensational £BOO tote div. at Adelaide in
’BO, was a complete stranger to the city and backed
D. O’D. for no stronger reason than that the rider’s colors
were green. Another big div. was paid on Auckland
course, when the rank outsider Kalo flashed first past the
post in a field of six or seven and paid its three backers
£256 each. These two cases are splendid arguments in
favor of tote as against Ikey Mo. D. O’D.’s backer, had
he risked his pound with a bookie, might have—and the
presumption necessitates a stretch of the imagination-
got 50 to I ; the other £750 would have been “ bunce ” to
Mo. In Kalo’s case the books would have had about £75
to pay out, and over £750 to do it with.

* * • •
When he wishes to bolster up an argument, edi-
tor Fitchett, Wesleyan parson though he be, is now and
then riot too beastly particular on the subject of accuracy.
Being desirous of damning the totalisator, he points to the
fact that Premier Seddon is moving for: its abolition, and
adds: “The case must be strong which induces an Aus-
tralian Premier to surrender £13,000 per annum, and
Maoriland, it must be remembered, is the one province
which has given the totalisator a fair and full trial.” Even
Mr. Fitchett must have known that the argument of
the friends of the machine in Victoria is based on the
experience of South Australia, where the strident voice
of the bookmaker is now stilled and the totalisator for
years has held continuous and quiet sway.
* * * »
“What are you laying about this race?” in-
quired a punter of a gem-studded layer at a recent M.L.
meeting. “ Three to one, bar four,” was the reply. And
there were only six competitors.
* « * *
Wellington (M.L.) Racing Club has raised a
howl by setting apart an enclosure for books to lay
“ straight-out ” odds at meetings. Hitherto the club has
waged war against the fraternity. As the Legislature has
been conserving the interests of the tote, from which it
derives revenue, it is quite on the cards that next season
permits will not be issued to clubs who license pencillers.
» * * *
Odds on tap at leading Sydney “ book ’’-shops
last Saturday were delightfully satisfying. One race—l 7
presumed starters; longest price, 10 to 1. No scratch-
ings posted, and the town punter not only got daylight-
robbed on the odds, but had his choice of a fine crop of
“ stumers ”as well—“ all in.” Time the tote came, any-
way, wherefore this paper is pleased to hear that N.S. W.
Parliamentary Committee re the totalisator means busi-
ness, and that Mr. A. O. Whittington, secretary of the
S.A. J.C., is in Sydney to give evidence of the “George
Washington’s” working in the Holy City. Significant,
too, in another way, that neither of the Sydney morning
organs has yet given its views re the tote. Both waiting
for the cat to jump?
• * * *
Moorfield (Syd.) had record attendances last
Saturday and Monday, the feature of the meeting being
the forward running of the “pony” division. On the
first day three little ’uns, Churohlight, Federation, and
Loch Katrine, filled the places in Kogarah Stakes ; and
Howlong - , Arden, and Loch Katrine finished ahead in the
Stewards’ Mile. On Monday pony Federation ran second
to Satanelle in the Trade Stakes, and Crimson and Arden
ran first and second in the Workman’s Handicap. Fitz-
roy, carrying second top weight, appeared to have the
Demonstration Handicap won, but M'Carthy, on Feu-de-
Joie came with a rush, caught Fitzroy napping, and won
on the post. W. Kelso’s Lauri, by Goldsbrough from
Equation, won the first two-year-old race of the" season.
The meeting bore out the aphorism that a good little ’un
is better than a bad big one.
• '* ♦ *
At Moorefield (Sydney) “ horse ’’-races on
Saturday all the placed horses"in two of the events were
galloways, driven into the “legitimate” ranks by the
A. J.C.’s recent ukase. Tub Bulletin’s old-time prophecy
that a pony will win a Melb. Cup is not at all improbable.
* * * *
Prior to winning the two steeplechases at the
late Randwick meeting, Levanter, during morning work,
got stuck on a jump through getting one of his legs
through a panel, and had to be helped off by about a
dozen bystanders. While the operation of lifting him off
the fence was going on the horse was as docile as a kitten,
and seemed rather glad of the enforced idleness on
top of the logs in preference to continuing his three-mile
journey.
» • * *
A correspondent refers to the distressful rolling
of Amberite and Merloolas at the end of the Randwick
Plate, 3 miles, as evidence they are “ stones below ” the
form of Abercorn and Trident. But, then, Abercorn and
Trident were not asked to run 3-mile races in an unfit
state.
* * * *
Next Saturday (Bth) the Caulfield Cup meeting,
which is to be a three-days’ affair this year, will open the
Southern horse festival in the good old style. Guinea-day
at Caulfield is famous, like the average day at other
courses, for cronk racing. With the Sydney division, in
particular, it has frequently been an occasion for sow'ing
the seeds of “ surprises” to come, and the surprises have
generally burst forth a week later. The ’orse that runs
nowhere in Caulfield Stakes or Toorak Handicap is quite
likely to be a prominent performer in the Cup race. If
the writer were one of the V.A.T.C. stewards, he would
probably suspect something of a “ pretty good ” character
for Toorak Handicap, but, even in that case, he probably
wouldn’t suspect it at time of writing. And he isn’t a
steward, anyhow. He’s a sick passenger, as a rule.
The comparative strength of N.S.W. and Vic.
horse “form” for the coming carnival can only be
guessed at. The writer guesses that the visiting quad-
rupeds will take more than their share of prize-money,
as they did last year. Amberite is likely to win either
of the Cups—the Caulfield for choice. ’ The Victorian
“cracks”—or apologies for cracks—appear to be of the
one-down-t’other-come-up order, which means that none
of them have any brilliancy. Massinissa may win Toorak
Handicap, but the writer would sooner back one of the
foreigners. Why shouldn’t Staffa, from S.A., win it?
* • * *
After Bobadil’s ridiculously easy wins at Flem-
ington these last two Saturdays, the Bill of Portland colt
looks the deadest of birds for V.R.C. Derby—“ on paper.”
He gets away from such as Cocos and Lee Metford with-
out apparent effort, and his hind-legs simply laugh at
George Frederick, who has laboriously clattered into
second place on each occasion. Bobadil is a trifle smaller
than the ideal Derby winner—about the same height and
length as Aurum, and not so nuggetty—but his action is
perfect, and he appears to have forgotten his early ten-
dency to “ cut it.” There will be lots of clever people
tipping, this, that, or the other 3-year-old to beat Bobadil
—“any fool can pick the favorite.” The Bulletin can
see no wisdom, though, in picking anything else at
present.
* * * *
Wait-a-Bifc and Majestic are good-looking horses
that have often deceived their backers, and will carry a
lot more money this season. Of the two The Bulletin
prefers Majestic’s chance in Caulfield Cup.
* * * *
At Brisbane, the Childe Harold stallion Harold
tried to beat the Australian record (Fritz’s 2min. 14see.),
but could not do better than 2.22 4-sth, Harold had no
show of beating Fritz’s time, and his owner made a mis-
take in not announcing that his trial was to beat the
Australian stallion mile record, which he succeeded in
doing. The Vic. mares, Calista and Mystery, trotted the
best of three one-mile heats last week, when Calista won
straight out in 2.38% and 2.29%. M.L. is out early with
some big trotting-programmes, good enough to "tempt
Mr. Wonbobbie Buckland to take a ruri-over with another
team, including champion Fritz.
» * * *
Though there was much dispute as to the
superiority of Carbine over Abercorn on the turf, nearly
all agreed that Chester’s handsome son must prove the
more successful at the stud. Yet here, too, old Jack is
easily ahead, even before he got his English connection,
and Abercorn, it must be remembered, had a whole year’s
start or more.
* • * *
The late Major Walmesley, for many years
manager of the Maoriland Stud co. and the most success-
ful breeder of horses in Australasia, used to say that the
best mare got by Musket was Mitrailleuse, which old Aus-
tralian sports will remember as a favorite for Lochiel’s
Newmarket. Walmesley pointed her out to the writer
when she was saddling up for her first race. “See that
filly. She’ll be a clinker. Backed her dam, Erycina, for
the Oaks—and that’s What brought me out to the
colonies.” .
* * * *
. Nowadays it is the rule, rather than the excep-
tion, for Australian horses to run unkindly, and common-
sense cries aloud for an improved system of educating the
little animals who bestride the ’osses’ backs. Good riders
make game thoroughbreds. In England, where horse-
racing is less hippodromey than the whip-spur-and-jostle
business which piasses for “ sport ” at Flemington and
Randwiek, there is a constant demand for higher culchaw
in the saddle. Said London Referee lately : “ The
shortest way to improve our thoroughbreds is to find good
jockeys for them, so as to give all a chance of doing them-
selves justice. A means of doing this always presents it-
self, viz., to raise the standard of weight so that the
apprentice will not have to look forward to being too
heavy to follow' his profession by the time he has
learned it. Let us have men instead of little nip-
pers.” The lowest handicap weight in Australia is a
stone heavier than the Hinglish minimum, and very
few adult jockeys grow too fat for their work if they
“ keep off the drink ” ; and yet horse-racing is more and
more of a boys’ game every year. The men who have
learnt something of their profession seem to retire early,
if they live to retire of their own accord. Not unfre-
quently they get killed in a scrimmage coming round one
of the turns. Brainless nippers are mounted on heavy
saddles to ride at Bst. or more, and even when the owner
gets a good go, as it is called, for his money, he doesn’t
know whether to blame the horse or the jockev for a
defeat.
Again, when the boy has obviously ridden a
foolish race there may be a fair excuse for him. Some-
times he is not so much an incapable jockey as a dutiful
ass, afraid to act up to his own knowledge in an emer-
gency. An Australasian writer remarks of such cases :
“ The fault lies with those who load them up with all
sorts of absurd instructions. We remember a jockey of
experience and reputation who had just run nearly last
on a good horse in a weight-for-age race at Randwiek
saying afterwards that he might have won if he had not
been told to do all sorts of impossible (as the race
was run) things in the race. Hales was merely
told how' he was to ride. He was left to use his own
judgment, and his phenomenal success was no doubt
due in a great measure to his being left to think out his
own plan of battle.” Talking of Hales reminds Tiie Bul-
letin that he had the making of the late James White’s
horses, and he taught them to run gamely when they
were two-year-olds. He went out with instructions to
win, and if Tommy 11. didn’t win the fault was in the
horse’s legs, not in its temper. Nowadays we find
“ crack” horses running roguishly. They haven’t been
handled by a Hales, and they haven’t always been asked
to run straight. The j'oung horse that is “pulled” will
be apt to turn rogue on its own account.
* * » *
Lawyer Hutchison’s Restraint of Wagering
Bill has been shelved in M.L. Assembly, but Govt, has
introduced a more drastic measure in the Leg. Council.
It is so arbitrary, in fact, that there is a suspicion that
Govt, does not want the measure to pass, and that it is
merely bidding for popularity with the spotless saints
who never do anything wrong. One provision :If any
person loiters on a roadway, or two or more assemble for
the purpose of betting, in view of a constable, the latter
may arrest them without a warrant and detain them
until they can be brought before the court. No bail is pro-
vided for. In proceeding against owners of gaming houses,
if they knowingly and wilfully keep such places open,
such knowledge and wilful intent shall be deemed to be
proved if it is established to the satisfaction of the court
that prior to the date of the alleged offence the defend-
ants had been warned by the police that such place was
suspected to be a common gaming house.
* * * *
Anomaly of the gambling law : A Wanganui
(M.L.) Maori bought a totalisator ticket and subsquently
sold a share to a brother Day and Martin. Horse won,
paid a big div., but the ticket-holder refused to “part”
to his co-shareholder. Result —row. Defence—denial of
sale, and reliance on the Gaming Act which says a magis-
trate cannot adjudicate on a gambling transaction. Plain-
tiff was non-suited, the beak remarking that in such
matters a man had to trust to another’s honor for pay-
ment. This decision practically means that where four
bankerers after wealth make up a £1 ticket the gent who
buys the pasteboard is top dog because he can stick to the
div.
* * * *
A clumsy trotting s.vindle was lately perpe-
trated at Christchurch (M.L.) A fair goer was nominated
for an event, but his clever party sent out an old trap-
horse under his name and supported the ultimate winner
with a bookie both straight out and in a winning double.
The fish smelt so strong, however, that enquiry resulted
in disqualification for life. Bookie vamoosed and the
party didn’t get a tin-tack out of the whole transaction.

Hunk of turf condemnation from Beetham, sti-
pendiary beak at Christchurch (M.L.) : “Horseracing
was a most damnable business. It meant ruin—morally,
intellectually, and physically—to those who followed it
up, and wherever it was tliere were lies. How people
could bring themselves to make a living at it he could
not imagine, and, for his part, he would take for choice a
job as night-contractor.” Then he fined a motley crowd
for “ trespassing ” on a racecourse. The assumption of
the role of night-merchant by Mr. Beetham w ould, on the
de qmtibus principle, possibly not be incongruous. His
unwarranted reference to the lying proclivities of racing-
men, however, comes ill from a gentleman who indulges
inordinately in angling.

Westralian society was recently much puzzled
as to what could be the “ urgent private business” which
called home the much-gilded Oapt Haig, A.D.C., within
a few weeks of his arrival in the province. The facts
transpired lately, when files Of English papers arrived
containing an account of the final thatch in the inter-regi-
mental polo tournament at Hurlingham, to play in which
the gallant capt. had “ come all the way front the
Antipodes.” He made the only goal scored in the
match, which won it for his regiment; the Inniskilling
Dragoons.
• • • •
Veteran huntsman Basil Grey, who met his
death in the saddle a few weeks back, had, during his
long hunting career, broken pretty nearly every bone irt
his body. About four years ago, lie got his elbow badly
fractured, but, within a week, was again after the hounds;
with his arm in a sling. The veteran safely negotiated et
couple of stiff fences, but old George Watson pulled hint
up, swearing that he could not allow a friend to so openly
suicide. Grey yielded to his old chum’s entreaties, and
went no further, but, all the same, was not very thankful
for the interference.
* * « •
“ Blister ” : Australian love for the horse. The
other day there was a gee-gee to be landed from the
Casino at Apollo Bay, where, as there’s no wharf, the
intelligent natives came in a boat with a rope 60ft. long
to take delivery. A slip-knor. was run over the prad’s
head, and he was blindfolded, and hoisted in slings
about 10ft. above the rail. “ Let go ! ” yelled the boss,
and “ let go” it was. The boatmen, 30ft. away, tightened
the rope, and rowed for their lives ; the tackle
dropped, and the neddv went down head-first (blindfold)
20ft. into the sea. Presently he came up, and after a
struggle, got the sight of one eye, and would have swum
ashore all right, if left alone. But those in the boat
pulled away about three times faster than the poor brute
could swim ; and, by thus dragging his head under water
succeeded in drowning the miserable animal. Perhaps,
after all, though, the prad was better dead than in the
hands of people who had no more sense than to try and
carry him about by the neck

Sydney senior cricket started on Monday last
with a match Paddington and South Sydney. Paddington
scored but 89, M’Kenzie topping with 27. For South Syd-
ney, Walters and Pite both made centuries and retired,
and at time the score stood 294 for four wickets.
* * » *
Melb. Cricket Club has already had agreements
signed by prominent Australian cricketers engaging to go
to England under its auspices if selected. There has been
much private comment in Adelaide over the fact, now
first published, that Giffen was not invited to sign any
agreement, although Lyons was, and did so. But what
has the Cricket Council got to say about this agreement
business —managed so early and so quietly ?
» * * *
Professional football in England. Aston Villa
last year received £11,077, all but £735 of which was ex-
pended. Everton Club received £10,601, over half of
which went to players. Players are bought and ex-
changed in the same way as ball players in America. And
Australian football would perhaps be cleaner were profes-
sionalism openly allowed instead of winked at as it is now,
