The N.S.W. amateur athletic championship
games, on Sydney Cricket Ground last Saturday, pro-
duced some fine performances. Amateur Stan. Rowley
tied “ Billy” Macpherson’s record, lOsec. in the 100yds. ;
covered 220yds. in 22 3-ssec. ; and ran 120yds. in‘even
time. The 1-mile run was a great go between S. W. York
and Englishman Moates, the pair running a dead heat in
4min. 45sec. Spectators badly wanted to see a “go”
between Rowley, the amateur champion, and Rogers, the
prof, ditto. Each won his race easily in lOsec. “dead,”
and each was willing to run off, but the authorities de-
cided that a lily-white “ amatuah ” couldn’t be allowed
to race with a pro.
A MELB. PUNTING SONG.
Now, let us have the tote-maohine for sick are we of Ikey
Mo,
Of Ikey Mo, the parasite, despised of the gods,
Who maketh turfy sport unclean, arid stiffens e’en the
bikey go;
We want a fairer, squarer fight, arid better bloomin'
odds.
Chorus :
So agitate and raise a din, my punterkith and punterkin,
Take action in November for to bring about the tote—
The tote that parsons deem a sin
Because the lambs, whom bookies skin,
Can thereby get a chance to win
O, button-hole your meriiber and instruct him how to vote.
Instead of totes-upon-the-sly (as cronk as “coppers”
foxin’ ’em)
We want the open-air machine which Adelaide has got;
And if it cooks the smaller fry who suck the brains of
Oxenham—
Well, let ’em go to work a bit, we’ve worked for them
a lot.
(Chorus)— So agitate, &c.
You like the books! What flummery! And you a
gamblin’ martyr, too!
The most of them are vulgar brutes, a-waxing fat as
kings.
The tote don’t guzzle Pommery; it doesn’t keep a
“ bart ” or two,
Nor spend our stuff in gaudy suits and cluster diamond
rings.
(Chorus)— So agitate, &c.
It lives on small percentages (O think of the disparity
Between the prices punters get from Ikey and the
tote),
And, least of its advantages, it works in aid of charity ;
No filthy language—don’t forget—can issue from its
throat.
(Chorus)— So agitate, &c.
Cry out on Franky Madden, boys; he burkes the Bill
in Parliament ;
And simply wink your other eye at Hancock’s little
gags;
For Ikey Mo’s a bad ’un, boys, and healthy public senti-
ment
Is dead against protection for the boodlers with the
bags.

J. A. Philp to Thbi Bulletin :
Von Kotze’s :mti-Chinese logic is “chop” logic. He
says that the relationship between Chinese exclusion and
the increased price of vegetables is a capital argument
for his contention—that the exclusion of the Chinese
means higher wages. Admitted so far, but can’t he see
that my contention is “No Chinese, no vegetables at
all”? — i.e., that under the arid conditions of interior
Australia no white man can grow vegetables to commer-
cial advantage. Would European-grown cabbages at
half-a-erown a piece suit working men at Wileannia,
Blaekall, or Milparinka? I can fancy Von Kotze’s feel-
ings as he “ scoffed ” up the whole of the proceeds of a
twenty-line Bulletin par. in a heliogabalic pot of greens,
or “ did in ” the price of four stanzas in one tiny salad !
As to the goldfields, we have but Von K.’s bare assertion
that “ the Palmer would be carrying a white population
to-day .. . but for these slant-eyed gleaners.” N.S.
Wales experience is that the fields tackled by Chinese
fossiekers are too poor to attract white miners, and Pre-
mier Seddon, of Maoriland —once a working miner himself
and anti-Chinese to the finger-tips—publicly stated the
other day that many of the towns on the West Coast of
M.L. would be deserted but for the gold fossicked out of
old workings by Chinese. He certainly did not regard
the Chinkie as a “ curse ” in this capacity. Von K.’s
opinion that I have not improved the case for the China-
man carries small weight against the many letters I
have received from all the Australias admitting that I
have thrown a great deal of new light on a little-under-
stood subject.
“ C.T.” writes :
Please let me have this smack at the dog-on-top,
despite the fact that there’s precious little credit in lick-
ing an obsolete enemy, rendered doubly weak by
treachery in high places. The Yank is just as conceited
as if he had won a desperate uphill fight, and innumer-
able ink-slingers will follow a recent scribe’s attempt to
deprecate the personnel of the British army. Material
victories are sometimes moral defeats. Had Britain fol-
lowed the Yank course of sending every available man to
the front, there would to-day be no U.S., nor would there
have been an Isandula or Majuba to sneer at. Further,
had the Yanks started “ on their own,” as they intended,
they would have made a holy show of themselves over the
present affair. No amount of mouthing can get over the
fact that England always fights short-handed and mostly
wins, barring the two accidents above mentioned.
Also, the Yank-reports of their own losses, like those of
the Boers, don’t somehow fit in with the amount of glory
claimed. Announcement of a “ warmly contested fight ”
is not corroborated by a loss of two or three on the
attacking side. It is the old mistake of protesting too
much—since one or the other report must lie. Also, re-
garding the alleged inferiority of the present Tommy
Atkins, founded on the erroneous assumption that he is
largely “gutter-bred,” the disparagement would more
fitly apply to the army of a country which is the refuse
heap of Europe. A long period of peace had produced
the impression that the U.S. land-forces were merelv a
national formality, and as there was therefore no moral
inducement to enlist, the Yankee private had come to be
locally regarded as merely a sort of superior Weary
Willie. Now, bad parents can’t throw good stock ; and,
speaking generally, first-class men don’t emigrate. There
is too much demand for them at home.
[There often is too little demand, under an effete
civilisation, for the man so essentially first-class
that he is best fitted for the founding of a new
one.—Ed. B.]
“Horsehair”: It is not true that Vic.
Attorney-General Isaacs and Justice Hodges
earned their livelihood in other directions while
studying for the bar.” While reading for the bar,
Isaacs was having a good, practical education in
law as clerk in the Vic. Prothonotary’s office
under the late J. A. Porter, a Sydney “boy.”
Hodges, too, was studying and teaching all the
tune he was preparing for the bar. He was tutor
in Ohief-Justice Stawell’s family, and his readings
in law were practically under the direction of that
bilious man and cut© lawyer.
“ A.J.8.” to The Bulletin from Wes-
tralia :
You Easterners amuse us out West with your fuss
about your meat-supply. Recently heard a local
slaughterman explaining the mutilation of a side of beef.
‘‘ You see, I had to cut a lump off that part because he
had a big cancer there.” You have at anyrate a pretence
of meat-inspection ; vve haven’t even that.
Helen Hart, disgusted with the lack of
recognition of her efforts to obtain womanhood-
suffrage, has made up her mind to shortly quit
Australia for England. Miss Helen lately, when
the plate went round one evening after a sermon
with which she disagreed, put in a couple of
tickets for an approaching lecture —in the course
of which, no doubt, she put the rev. brother right.
The poor mother thought it was measles,
so administered marigold tea and whisky ; and
when the child’s neck swelled she rubbed it with
herbs and poulticed it. Then it had convulsions,
got a hot mustard bath and died. When the
doctor arrived he diagnosed scarlet fever. —Facts
elicited at a Vic. inquest.

“ Combo Re eel-catching. Menangle
(N.S. W.) Pond, near the station, went nearly dry,
and one Sunday was exploited by. a crowd of eel-
spearers. Some eels caught weighed 50 lbs. and
measured 8 feet. Too rank for eating, they were
left to rot.
A prize temperance yarn. At Mt. Mac-
donald, one night, a digger was drinking at the
pub, when someone told him that a bush-fire was
burning near his camp. Making the best of his
way home he fell into an old hole, and being too
drunk to get out, camped there. In the morning,
the first thing he saw was a 6oz. slug sticking in
the side of the hole within reach of his hand.
The Murray and Darling river-boat, Tyro,
whose boiler blew up a few months back, is in
service again, captained by the officer who was
sent skywards at the time and was regarded as
practically done for. But, contrariwise, a Syrian,
who walked off unhurt, has since died, apparently
of shock.
Yic. Judge Hamilton doesn’t, like his
learned brothers, affect a lamb-like innocence of
the wicked world. In the recent Olive case, where
the wife summoned hubby for detaining certain
property of hers, counsel referred to certain pre-
nuptial relations between the parties, whereupon
Hamilton : “ The Court is not so horribly stupid
that it needs to have these things explained to it.
One would think I was a boy fresh from school,
who had never seen a girl in his life before. This
kind of thing won’t enable me to determine which
of the two parties owns a piano, though it might
possibly enable me to decide who owns a child.”
In the days when W.A. mines were still
saleable and experts listened to reason, a certain
expert was sent out to report on a claim under
offer. He proved to be not at all the man the
sellers bad expected ; at the very first hint at a
small commission he boiled over in a manner that
was simply disgraceful. He insisted on going
down, broke off the quartz himself, put it in the
sack himself, held the sack firmly in his hands till
the tent was reached, and, finally, sat down and
watched the stuff dollied much as a cat watches a
mouse-hole. Bill had the “salt” in his shirt-
bosoin ready to be slipped in at the first chance.
He had told his mates to keep out of the way
that he might not be hampered by inexperienced
aid. Now, he cursed them fiercely in silent
rhythmic accompaniment to the dolly, and the
sweat of agony came to his brow whenever he
looked up to find two eyes carefully regarding his
every motion. Then inspiration came. Suddenly
dropping the pestle he jumped for the tent opening
and gazed expectantly down the track. The expert
looked surprised, but said nothing till the per-
formance was repeated for the third time. Then he
got answer that Bill was expecting per bicycle-
messenger a, letter from “ the missus,” with whom
an interesting event was slightly overdue. The
missus was in bad health, Bill explained, and grave
fears were entertained. The tent being off the
track, too, Bill was mortally afraid the cyclist
would miss it unless hailed, and anxiety was writ
plain on his features. Being himself a family
man the expert was sympathetic and offered to
keep a look-out while Bill finished the dollying.
The letter did not arrive, but both Bill and the
expert were pleased with the “ panning-off ”
results. The report was favorable and the claim
was bought. Truth compels the postscript that
Bill’s share of the spoil was mostly appropriated
by a painted charmer who had previously enjoyed
much alcohol in his company, and that Bill himself
was unmarried except in the casual way of the
West as it was in those days.
“H.”: On the Red Bluff (St. Kilda,
Melb.) were buried three pioneers, in 1840.
The consuming lips of the ocean are crumbling
off the Bluff, so that soon the three skele-
tons would be washed away. So it was
decided to exhume and re-bury them. A goodly
assemblage witnessed this job. Men dug down,
and found that the coffins, of red gum, had gone,
only the skeletons being left. Had the wood been
charred, as with our fence-posts, it might bave
remained sound. Other pioneers are buried on
the Flagstaff Hill, West Melb.
“ Melb. Dropped into the Town Hall,
t’other night, while, the ever-masterful Jew held
’em in his oratorio clutch. Pastor Abrahams, at
a Wesleyan mission meeting ! He quoted Dis-
raeli’s saying : “ All sensible men are of the same
religion,” and the plaudits were roof-shaking, but
the cunning Rabbi neglected to add the other
clause—“ What is it? They never tell.” The
Rabbi is quite a reminder of Gainbetta, and
though he only dealt in magnificent generalities,
he knocked all the orthodox specialists out of
time.

Charming Grace Noble, perhaps the nicest girl the
Brough and Boucieault co. ever imported, would like to
come out again. She has suggested as much to Robert
Brough, but, according to late letters, he had by that
time completed his arrangements.
“ Old Jack ” : Fact that I’ve just had a dish of peas acci-
dentally dressed with sugar reminds me of a story of the
Bianchis. One day, when crossing from Hobart to Mel-
bourne, Biauchi to his sposa : “ Angel of mine, I have
heard from the cook there are peas—i piselli —eh 1 but,
alas, only one dish ! What shall I do?” “Heart of my
heart,” whispered the lady, “dress it with oil. These
savages detest it.” So, when the table was laid, and
before the dinner-bell was rung, the fat little tenor and
his enormous wife drenched the peas with best Lucca.
The skipper, beholding, whispered something to the
steward, who, forthwith coming to Bianchi’s shoulder,
said, “Sauce, sir? Yessir,” and poured a good gill of
anchovy sauce over the pea 3. A grin went round. But
Bianchi was all smiles. “ Aha ! t’ank you. Ze anehova,
angel of mine. You lika? Si? Anybody else lika ze
anehova wiz i piselli —eh ? No ! Vat peety ! ” So the
pair gobbled up the peas between them. “ But, my
Gawd I ” said providore Mason, who told me the yarn,
“ just wasn’t they sick, right up to the wharf. Had to
be carried ashore.”
That gorgeous farce, the Melb. Shakespeare Ball, only
panned out a paltry £29 for the theatrical charities—after
all the advertising and the distribution of, probably, over
£lOOO among the Melb. drapers for the costumes. The
most striking figure of the pageant was a Melb. art-
student who walked all the way from the Princess Theatre
attired in brass helmet, sleeveless cotton blouse, old
sword with belt, and ancient tweed pants.
Some years ago, at Sydney Opera House, was staged a
hair-raising drama by a local author, in which the late
Frank Oates was enacting the usual heroine-hunting
villain. In a scene in which he was supposed to be dig-
ging down to the gas-main in a cellar, preparatory to
blowing everybody to atoms, he was “ discovered ” by a
well-known actor who was playing a Jew part and had to
ask “ Vat are you doing there, mein friend?” To which
Cates, in stentorian tones, “ Digging a grave for the
author of this d d rotten piece !” And the house with
boisterous applause signified its agreement with the
estimate.
During the bankruptcy exam, of a well-known actor
some time ago, creditor’s solicitor asked, with reference to
an item £BO for carriages, if bankrupt didn’t think that
very extravagant for a man in his position. “ No, sir,”
replied the Thespian, “ the extravagance is not in the
carriages, but in the owner’s charges.”
The stage fall looks quite simple, but it isn’t. At one
Sydney theatre the other night, either the lady was inex-
perienced and fell badly, or the base deceiver cast her off
clumsily ; anyhow, when her shoulders struck the floor
and her boots and stockings flew eighteen inches in the
air the audience gave up taking that heroine seriously.
It had discovered that she wore one black sock and a
brown one.
An Australian actor-manager—though few would sus-
pect it—wears a wig; or, rather, several wigs. Peruke No. 1
is rather long, ditto No. last rather short. He alternates
them quarterly. “ Well, well,” he will remark with an
amusing effort at casualness, “ my hair is getting posi-
tively disgraceful ” ; and next day his appearance in the
shortest wig will convey to the uninitiated the idea that
he has just been to the barber’s. But the most cunning
little wig extant is worn by another theatrical manager,
now much over seven. You know how a tight hat makes
a deep crease in the hair behind. Well, he always wears
a tight hat, and then the crease caused thereby is the
spot where the wig joins his natural hair—what'there is
of it. There ain’t much.
“ P.A.”: I remember Hazon conducting the overture to
“William Tell.” He was specially great in the part
descriptive of a storm in the mountains where the tenor
drums, after a hair-raising crescendo, culminate in a
thunderbolt from the bass drum which used to make
brave men yearn to crawl under a haystack out of the
wet. _ Hazon worked up these thunderbolts with most
thrilling effect. He began by beating time with dainty
movements of his baton, delicate little bird-like twitters,
recalling the most charming girl you ever saw tripping
with up-raised skirts over a muddy crossing ; then, as
the throbbing of the drums increased, his arms would
make wider sweeps and he was a cuirassier mowing down
the foe. Anon he would become a Titan juggling with
mountains, and, finally, when the nervous tension had
continued long enough to make people want to rise up
and shriek in order to let off the accumulated emotion,
he would launch both arms towards the lair of the bass
drum, and a crash like a falling world would answer
the frenzied gesture. People forgot that it was
only catgut, horsehair, sheepskin and whistles kicking
up a row. To them, Roberto was embodied Jove hurling
veritable thunderbolts. He was the most sympathetic
and magnetic conductor I ever saw. His magic baton
interpreted every shade and tint of emotion that music
is capable of expressing, from the airy grace of Boc-
cherini’s minuet to the bombardment of Manila. Even a
stone-deaf man could tell what sort of music was being
played by watching the beats. I knew a man who used
to cause great laughter by an imitation of the “Tell”
overture. He simply ran his fingers through his hair to
make it stand like a chimney-sweep’s besom, and did the
roll of the drums with his tongue. The gestures, and his
auditors’ recollection of the unique original, did the rest.
As you say, Biondi could make his biggest hit as Hazon.
Some years ago, an English opera co. playing at
Adelaide Royal engaged Susini from Melb. to play Meph.
in Faust. S. was supposed to come up through a trap-
door, and on the first night a head appeared for a second
or two; then head and shoulders came into view and
made rather a longer stay, the big man looking as
pleasant as was natural in the circumstances. The
second time the full h.p. of the stage hands must have
been suddenly applied, for up came Meph. like a Jack-in-
the-box. Next night he came from behind a tree.
When Creswick was playing Virginius in Melbourne
some years ago one of the supers., just before the curtain
rose, was noticed to have his Roman toga slung over his
shoulders like a sundowner’s blanket, and on being asked
by the irate supermaster “ What the he was sup-
posed to represent?” replied, “ Please, sir, I am a ‘Decem-
ber ’” (decemvir). “I don’t care a d- if you were a
blanky January,” was the answer; “ you don’t go on like
that! ”
“ I had come into New Orleans,” says mesmerist Ken-
nedy, “ and agreed with the church eiders there to give
a mesmeric entertainment for $3OO, for the benefit of "the
church. Waal, the hall was packed—big church, it was—
with niggers, and I had about 30 nigs on the platform,
and it was a warm night, and niggers waal, niggers
aren’t nice company on a warm night. Anyhow, I got
through my contract, and tuned those niggers up to such
a height that half the audience got mesmerised too. As
a finaie, I showed them Heaven, with the angels in all
their glory, and the transports of the subjects were some-
thing tre-mendous. Next day, the church elders came to
me and said, ‘ Mr. Kennedy, we think it would do our
people a mighty power of good if you showed’em hell.’
I agreed, and accordingly, that night, brought in hell as
a finale. I said to them, ‘ You see where we are all
sitting here in this church. The windows are there, and
there, and there, as you see, and the walls are all
around; but the roof is off. You can see the sky.’
‘Oh, yaas,’ they came in, ‘see the sky!’ One fel-
low says, ‘ See stars ! ’ ’Nother says, ‘ Moon shinin’.’
‘ Yaas,’ I said, ‘ you all see the stars and the
moon shinin’ in the sky. But over there you
see a great black cloud ; it is getting bigger ’n’ bigger,
blacker and blacker ; it is coming towards us—see 1 coming
on, coming on. Very soon it will burst, and you will see
Satan and all his imps. Watch it, now watch it !
THERE ! it has BURST ! ’ Whew ! it did burst. I was
knocked over in the rush, and, before I could snap my
fingers, every one of those nigs, was gettin’ through the
winders and the whole audience was stampeding through
the doors ! I spent all that night chasin’ niggers round
New Orleans. You can imagine me in a cab galloping
down New Orleans and shouting out, ‘There ain’t no
hell at all! ’ and snapping my fingers on every nigger I
lassooed and shouting, ‘You’re alright, sir! Alright,
sir ! ain’t no hell at all, sir ! ’ ”
The Vic. Operatic Society promises some capital voices
for the forthcoming “ Bohemian Girl ” show at Melb.
Bijou. Miss Millie Tree Chapman, Kitty Grindlay and
Cesca Yollugi, A. W. Pett, William Neilson, Horace
Stevens, and J. Goudy can sing well, and two or three of
them act fairly. The V.O.S. will give a fetching musical
entertainment, anyway, for the amateur chorus is to go
SO strong, and a professional orchestra of 20 has been en-
gaged.

“ Melb.”Met this morning a well-known septua-
genarian harlequin crossing Carlton Gardens. I know
two sexagenarian clowns in this city. All which does not
alter the.question as to where the ballerina, who is more
than seven, six and aged, as they say on the turf, finds
her earthly paradise. The Scotch schoolboy asked
“ Whaur do the figures go when they a,re rubbit oot?”
Stay, a lady neighbor of mine was in the ballet 40 years
ago, and is married to a Chinese gentleman.
“ Melb.” : A Bourke-street bookseller has in his window
a venerable photo, of the Salle de Valentino, pulled down
30 years ago. It was a circular wooden building on the
site of Noble’s canvas circus, the first in Melb., at the
north-east corner of Bourke and Spring streets, opposite
where Parliament House is. The Valentino is depicted
in picturesque dilapidation, with interesting reminis-
cences of the playbills, auction bills, and what not, stuck
about it. ■'Rowe’s;Circus knocked out Noble’s, and was,
in turn, overthrown by the troupe fetched out by G. B.
W. Lewis, from Astley's, London, on the Princess Theatre
site.
“luna” : In Queen-st. still stands Melbourne’s first
theatre, the Queen’s. It is now occupied by a billiard-
table-maker. The building is substantial enough, and is
surmounted by a crude representation of the lion-and-
unicorn allegory. A friend tells me he saw comedian
Charley Walsh playing there when it was the pleasant
habit of the miners to attend with ladies whose charac-
ters could not be called doubtful, and to throw nuggets
on the stage for the favorite actor.
Emma Rogers, eldest daughter of the old-time come-
dian, G. H. Rogers, is taking to the stage again, chiefly
with a view to “bringing out” a very clever little
daughter of her own. Many Melb. playgoers will remem-
ber Emma Rogers as a good all-round actress, and a par-
ticularly effective Queen, in “Hamlet.”
The Argus last week deeply regretted to mention that
Mrs. Palmer, Australia’s longtime champion warbler of
“ Home, Sweet Home” —had been “ attacked by a throat
trouble which renders it impossible for her to continue
either her public appearances or the work of tuition.” In
the same issue of that valuable organ appeared a notice
of the disabled lady’s singing, overnight, at a Liedertafel
concert in aid of Julius Herz’s widow, so a word of expla-
nation was necessary. Therefore, the Argus next day
said a “ misconception ” might possibly arise from the
paragraph stating that Mrs. Palmer’s throat was affected,
inasmuch as it wasn’t affected, and wouldn’t prevent her
singing. In fact, said the Argus, she has recovered
from her illness, which had no connection with her voice,
and is now going stronger than ever. A newspaper “ mis-
conception ” is a wide term, seemingly.
Three English concert co’s., in addition to tenor-mono-
loguist Lely, sought Australian shekels during the past
winter. Results : Lemmone dropped some hundreds
over Alva and Verne ; Amy Sherwin gave shilling pops,
in Sydney ; Lely was by no means Smythe’s most
profitable investment ; and only Albani made anything
like a scoop. Her trills had lost their sweetness ; you
could hear the clavicular bellows blow ; she sang with the
aid of various vocal tricks ; but she was Albani ; and she
was on singing terms with Queen Victoria.
John J. Burke, comedian, lately in Australia, and since
killed in U.S., was a most tremendous stutterer—though,
curiously, not on the “ boards.” Arguing with a fellow-
actor once, Burke took so much time spluttering over a
word that the other held out his hat, saying, “ Here you
are, John —spit it into this ” !

Richmond, near Melbourne.” Chance missed to have im-
proved upon the “ancestral mansion” gag, with “our
house-boat on the Yarra.”

“8.E.”: “5.5.,” who, to judge by his tilts at every-
thing and everybody, evidently thinks no small pumpkins
of himself as a theatrical critic, is sometimes ludicrously
inaccurate when driven to a last desperate expedient in
order to pick flaws. Occasionally, as in the Coleman-
Ashcroft case, he gets punctured. (“ John’s” Scarecrow
Dance is undoubtedly the most humorous and the
cleverest bit of eccentric song-acting seen here since,
years ago, Keeno, I think, did “The Automaton,”
and it isn’t even a plagiarism on that.) Now
(B. 24/9/’9B) “ S.S.” oracularly sums up Peggy Pryde
as having “more confidence than ability,” and as
being consumed by a desire to look “ nice.” Peggy, of
all people, trying to look nice ! Peggy, as the almost
pathetic but awfully smudged and raggedy lodging-house
slavey ; Peggy, as that appalling guy, the gamp-carrying
woman’s rights apostle; Peggy as a Coster-’Ria ; or
Peggy, once more, as the black (and blue) eyed but defiant
wife of Bill, who has pawned everything but her tattered
shawl! Why, lam almost tempted to say that, after
Leamar and other birds whose plumage was their chief
recommendation, Peggy Pryde is the only visiting variety
star who, so far, has not sacrificed art to “ nicelooking-
ness.” “5.5.,” who, no doubt, has never been outside
Australia, probably does not know that Peggy is, as her
mother was, always a type in whatever she does. It
might instruct “ S.S.” if he were to see the prototypes—
he’ll find them in the Little Bourke-streets of London. As
to ability. Well, Peggy Pryde is the most intensely vital
artist, bar none, who has appeared on Australian stages.
She is feminine quicksilver, and that is the quicksilver.
Her dash and ilan are far superior to Leamar’s, and her
vulgarity is far more good-humored and less offensive.
And the tones she gets out of that hard voice of hers
prove the artist, for she makes you believe that she can
singl

“ H.” : Mrs. Lynn-Linton said that both Dickens and
Thackeray were consumed by secret passions. In the
case of Dickens, the object was Miss Ternan, with whom
he appeared in theatricals. This caused the rupture with
Mrs. Dickens.

ables at Grace’s jubilee cricket-match, clad in light-blue
silk, white lace fichu, &c., &c. The purfesh always spoke
of Marion Hood as a “ marvel.” Nobody could say how
old she was.

covered on W. coast Tas. by Mr. Moore, F.G.S.,
and named the Urquhart,‘is now found to be a
salt-water inlet 200 yards long.

[Directions lately given to a member of N.S.W.
Assembly.]
During the recent N.S.W. general election
a strong point was made at Ashfield against B. R.
Wise that he could not possibly attend to local
wants, or that he would not, which was the same
thing. The Reidite candidate, who had no occu-
pation except being a M.P., made a strong argu-
ment of that fact, and said he “ had no clients to
attend to except his constituents ” ; also, that his
valuable services were at the disposal of electors
“all day long.” Yet, the first week of Wise’s
membership, he seemed to obtain from a hostile
Govt, more than his opponent had got for ages—
as witness the =£looo for the Cottage Hospital, at
Croydon. A strong opponent generally gets more
from the Govt, of N.S.W. than a faithful, depend-
able, thick-and,-thin supporter. The man who
gets most of all 'is he who sits on a rail when close
divisions are about, and—makes terms.
The latest report states that Rougemont
ia Tozer in disguise.
John Downer, the be-knighted nominal
leader of S.A. Opposition, grows more and more
insufferably tedious. This session, on Hourigan’s
annual motion in favor of unimproved land values
taxation in municipalities, Downer, instead of
making a new speech, got hold of an old Hansard
and read most of his last year’s speech over again
in a gloomsome monotone that nearly drove the
House frantic. John has represented Ba.rossa for
20 years, and has run two short-lived governments
to the great monotony of everybody, but the
affable old man’s political career is apparently
about over.
The venerable Mrs. Badgery, mother of
the well-known Mr. H. S. Badgery, of Pitt, Son
and Badgery, and of the N.S.W. pastoralists of
that name, who died at Bathurst last week, car-
ried her recollections of N.S.W. back to the early
thirties and was rich in anecdote and personal
experience. She ambitioned verse-making and
painting. Her husband, who predeceased her,
was the first white man to enter the valley of the
Araluen, and was an explorer in certain useful
aspects of the word.
Labor-member Watson offered a sensible
suggestion on the first night of the Federal debate
in N.S.W. Parliament, but was enthusiastically
sat on by both parties. He proposed that, when
the two Federal Houses disagree about a measure
they should straightway have a joint sitting and
let the majority decide. This instead of the cum-
bersome idea that there must be two disagree-
ments (which means two sessions of Parliament),
and then a dissolution and a new election and
another disagreement before the joint sitting can
be arrived at. If a remedy for deadlocks is to be
effective it should be capable of application 20
times, if necessary, over one measure; not be a
slow, elephantine process which takes years
to get into motion. And, seeing that both
Houses will be elected on the widest and most
Democratic suffrage, if the people can’t trust the
majority they can’t trust anything.
Sydney Telegraph, in its leading article
of 27th Sept., declared that “if by such a wanton
attack as they are threatened with the Reid Govt,
were displaced, Mr. Barton’s new team would find
itself confronted with a powerful and justly ex-
asperated Opposition, which would render legisla-
tion practically impossible.” What a beautiful
admission ! If the Reid Govt, is displaced it can
only be because it is in a minority. If it is in a
minority, then it certainly ought to be displaced.
And, if it is justly displaced for being in a minority,
then it will be so mad at losing its sordid billet
that it will block Federation and all other business
out of revenge.

Dear B.—The s.s. Bourgogne business
suggests that one reason why men of Latin race
come so ill out of such emergencies is that, in any
dangerous or exciting situation, your Frenchman.
Italian, or Spaniard falls to talking. But
with British races, and Germans, and Scandina-
vians, the reverse is observable. The writer was
nearly cast away on two occasions ; once in a
Scotch, and another time in a Trench ship. When
the former ran ashore (in the Caroline group), no
voice was heard, save those of the officers giving
orders. Everybody—Scotch, English, Germans
and Swedes—listened and obeyed. And the ship
was saved. In the other case, when the Erench
barque took a_ reef in Torres Straits, there arose
such a jabbering of all hands as made discipline
impossible for some time. Each man yelled out
his idea of what should be done, and, as hardly
any two notions agreed, the cackle very nearly
cost us our destruction.—G.B.
Until a few months ago any Chinaman in
Victoria was permitted to go home and return
again to the province provided he produced a
naturalisation-paper and two photos, of himself.
The paper would be endorsed with the name of
the vessel by which he was going and the date,
one of the photos, would be attached to the paper,
the other retained at the customs for identification
on his return. The customs officials never dropped
to the idea that many papers were not genuine, until
one day a well-known Chow storekeeper brought
a big Celestial about 6ft. high to have his paper
endorsed. The clerk asked how old he was.
“Twenty-four,” replied the Chinaman. The
clerk glanced at the naturalisation-paper and saw
that it was issued in 1884. “ What were you
doing when this paper was issued ? ” “ Hawkem
tea.” “You were hawking tea when you were
ten years old ? ” asked the clerk. Just here some-
thing seemed to dawn on both Chinamen, and
they spurted through the door and never re-
turned. On further examination, it proved that
the paper was a hired one. Since then a rigid
examination has been made of all papers pre-
sented, and, during the last six months, not half a
dozen Chinamen have been able to crowd through
the needle’s eye of the law, where they used to
get through by scores.

The Miseries of
DYSPEPSIA
EFFECTUALLY ERADICATED
The Case of
Mrs. CLARKE,
(From the "Nepean Times,” N.S.W.)
(by our special reporter.)
Directions were given to our reporter to
look up Mrs. Clarke, of Woodroffe Street,
Penrith. On calling the pressman received
a cordial reception, and, on inquiring about
her illness, Mrs. Clarke said :
“I first guessed there was something the
matter win n I found that I couldn’t do my
ordinary work.
I felt Restless
and relaxed, and if I wanted to go out any-
where I felt so tired by the time that I had
finished dressing that I often changed my
mind and took my things off again. Then
I began to notice a twitching, pricking pain
between the shoulders, which got worse and
Wui'se. At night it gave me the most
distress, then the anguish of it was so great
that night after night I was
Not able to Lie Down,
but sat up in bed, as it was easier to bear
the pain in that position ; and, as for sleep,
why, I hardly ever got an hour’s rest from
evening to morning.”
“ r-’o bad as that ? ”
“ Yes, and all this time I had a heavy
sick headache that never ceased, and my
food disagreed with me every time I took
it. After eating I felt great oppression on
my chest, and i would have given the world
to have thrown it up again. But there it
stuck, cold and undigested, till it turned
sour and brought on windy spasms. There
was always a nauseous taste in my
mouth which nothing could remove. It is
no wonder that I was
So Thin and Weak,
and of a pallid, unwholesome colour.
People used to say that I looked
more like a walking ghost than a
human being, but I looked no worse
than I felt. I had to give up trying to
work; it was no use, I couldn’t do it.
Tortured with unceasing headaches, and
harassed by
The Never-ending Pains
in my back, life seemed not worth the
living, and everything was a misery. Meal
times, which, to those who are in health, is
a time of cheerful gathering, only wearied
me, for I had no appetite, and only ate when
I was actually obliged to.”
“Things couldn’t last long like that,”
Said our reporter.
“ You’re mistaken; a woman takes a lot
of killing, for, on and off, I was
ill for Three Years,
and always getting worse.”
“Surely the doctors could relieve you?”
“ Nothing of the sort; the doctors could
do nothing for me. Their physic quite
f ailed in its object. I can’t stop to tell you now
how many different pills and other things
I took trying to get relief. If they were all
put together I believe the bottles of
medicine and boxes of pills I took would
have filled buckets. Every penny of the
money I paid for these things
Was Utterly Wasted,
and I can hardly understand how my poor
body stood it all. It was quite plain that
none of these remedies bit off my disease,
so I ceased taking medicine altogether; but
I grew no better. A sluggish torpor lay
heavily on me all day, my eyes were di
and swollen, and yet I was not able m
sleep, and at night I never could find to
easy position in which I could get a fevi
hours’ rest, which I needed so badly, so
exceedingly acute were the
Tortures which Affected Me.
I grew very dejected. A great weight
of despair hung over me like a cloak. The
conversation of my husband and children
was not able to arouse or interest me.
Lower and lower I sank, and got weaker
and weaker, till very often I thought that
my spatk of life was going out, and that I
was about to die. Medicine had seemed
useless, and I could not see a gleam of hope
anywhere, and yet, at last, I found a remedy
That Defeated the Disea®'-
which had hold of me.”
“Yes, what?”
“ Clements Tonic. I was repeatedly
advised to take it, but I put it down as
being about as much use as the other things
which I had swallowed. But I was quite
out of my reckoning, for, when I did give
in and commenced to take it,
An Immense Difference
was easily seen in my general health after
only one large bottle. The dull appearance
about the eyes gave place to a brighter and
healthier look. I soon took a pleasure in
reading and the company of friends, who
complimented me upon
My Marked Improvement.
I obtained four more bottles of Clements
Tonic. It brought my strength back
splendidly, and my headache and the pains
in my back gave me less trouble and pain
after every dose. I felt quite sprightly and
lively in the daytime, instead of heavy and
stupid as I used to do, and at night, not
feeling any pain to speak of, long sleep
began to be a regular thing. No day passed
but what I was a step further towards my
usual vigorous health. Once I sent my
food away, but now I called for more, and
it did me good, digested easily, and I began
to fill out all over. I avoided any chance
of a relapse by sticking to Clements Tonic
even after I thought I was convalescent.
All traces of
Pain and Disease were
Dissipated,
and I was robust and vigorous again, thanks
to that fine remedy, Clements Tonic, and I
need hardly tell you I’m never now without
a bottle in the house. It cured me. You see
I am in perfect health now; I hope I ever
shall be. No one knows
The Misery and Despair
of one in ill health except those who suffer
it. lam glad to give the credit to Clements
Tonic. It did worlds for me, and I don’t care
who knows it, for you or Clements Tonic
people can publish what I’ve said in any
way you like.
STATUTORY DECLARATION.

On the Track.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They met on the Darling. One was a raw new-
chuiri, smoking a silver-mounted pipe. The
steamer “ Rob Roy ” had landed him at Wilcahina
the day before. He was going west. The other
was an old stager, who had spent his five-and-
thirty years in the backblocks. His back was
turned to the western heat, the western dust-
storms, and the western flies. Old and grizzly,
leg-weary, careworn and gray, he was “ going
in.’’
The new-ehum put down his swag very care-
fully. He was fond of it, as they all are in the
honeymoon stage of “ the wallaby.” Recognising
a brother in misfortune, he held out his hand
with effusive greetings. The old stager said
nothing, just stared. It was a treat to see him
stare. Down went his swag like a ton of bricks,
and down he flopped on top of it. Damn me,
he said, “ but you’re the greatest chump I’ve run
agin since old Brickfielder died. How long ’ave
yer been at this, in the name o God ?
“ Only to day.”
“ To-day? Phugh ! I sed that meself 35 year
ago. That’s a tidy while afore you left the dock,
yard. An’ this is the game that’ll make yer curse
the day yer ever did leave it. An’ fore you go
any further, lemme give yer some advice. Yer
not above talcin’ advice ? ”
“By no means. I’d be obliged, sir.”
“ gj r ! I’m old Bill Tarkalson. I
aint one o’ them coves as hankers after ‘ sirs ’ an’
‘ beg yer pardons,’ an’ that sort o’ tommyrot.
Now, listen to me. In the first place, never ’old
out yer ’and to a man without a bit o’ baccy or a
bob in it. Where’s the good o’ offerin’ him a
empty ’and ? Many a cove would ’a’ knocked yer
sprawlin’ for that. It’s like rubbin’ it in when he’s
down. Don’t make that mistake ag’in. By-an’-
by you’ll want some baccy. Now, the golden rule
on the track’s this : Never cadge off a footman,
or the one-horse bloke. The cove wot has two
nags is good game. So’s drovers an’ carriers, an’
anyone that’s in a billet. Go for them—all of ’em,
an’ partic’ly the cove at the store. Nail him
Don’t wait till yer want a smoke. Cadge every
chance yer see till yer get a supply in. Gorra
match on yer? When yer can’t carry no more
baccy, cadge matches. Gorstruth, safeties !
“In a day or two yer’ll want rations. Do a
sneak, see, and land yerself in the kitchen. Soon’s
the cook twigs yer, if it’s a shemale, stop short an’
gasp. Always put the gasp in. Then start givin’
*er taffy as if yer couldn’t help it, an’ with all the
big vvords ye can manage. She’ll want to know
then what you want. Ask her for anything in the
tucker line—you’ll get it! Then yer want to go
to the boss with yer bags—big ’uns. No use
thinkin’ yer’ll get ’em filled if they’re small ones.
Yer won’t. He’s nearly sure to be into his last
’alf bag o’flour. Never mind that. ’Alf bag’s
more’n you want. Never mind if he scratches his
’ead and performs. You just hold fast, an’ he’ll
shell out.
“ Have to change the style o’ yer clo’es though.
Gorany with patches on ? Then keep an eye
about yer and cop the first bit o’ stuff yer see an’
patch yer oldest pair o’ pants with it. No holes
in’em? What’s that matter? The patches will
cover the places where the holes ought to be.
Them’ll be yer ration-pants. You only want two
more pair—track pants and Sunday pants. Sun-
day’s the day yer in a town. Doesn’t signify
what the day really is. If it’s not Sunday, it’s
Sunday-pants day anyway.
“Lemme see, now. You’ve got baccy and
matches and tucker and rations. Well, now,
young fellow, you want a job. Reach us that
fire-stick, will yer ? Yer a d poor hand at
makin’ a fire, I can tell yer that much. Shove
that billy in a bit. Some ask for ‘ yacker,’ some’s
lookin’ for ‘graft,’ and some’s ‘after a job.’ One
cove wants something to do ; another cove wants
to know if anything is doin’. The men that asks
for ‘ employment ’is laughed at. He should say,
‘ Ken yer give us a job, sir ? ’ or ‘ Eny chance of a
billet, sir?’ Don’t forget the sir, whatever you
do. Be a little awed in the boss’s presence. That
flatters his vanity. If yer can’t feel awed, look it.
By’n’by you’ll feel it, in spite of yerself, and sink
to the level of the rank-and-file. They all do.
Yer want to cringe and crawl, beg and
smooge, else yer’ll get no job. Study slang
for all you’re worth, an’ be careful not to
use genteel talk. Don’t betray yer edgecation—
if yer gorany. Bosses don’t like edgecated
servants. Inconvenient to ’ave a man about them
who knows more’n they do. Jerk us off a hunk
o’ that there brownie o’ your’n. Looks better’n
mine. Woman-made ? Ah, thought so. Lor’
bless her doughy fivers !
“ Sometimes yer’ll meet a gentleman. Yer’ll
know him the moment he speaks. Gen’ally the
other doesn’t speak. He merely looks important
at yer, and waits for you to speak. Then he
grunts. Don’t cringe to a gentleman or your
cake’ll be dough. Be ’onest an’ ’upright, be polite
but independent like, forget yer’ve rubbed
shoulders with the toe-rags. Damn you, be a
man 1 ”
EDWARD S. SORENSON.
The Tohunga and the Taniwha.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hone Tui was small, wizened, and hunchbacked,
but, for all that, a tohunga of the first magnitude.
Like all tohunga, white or black, red or brown,
Hone lived by his people’s dread of the super-
natural, and his biggest stock-in-trade was the
local taniwha.
Miromiro was, of all Maori maids, the sweetest
and most beautiful. She was the pride of her
blood-thirsty father, the chief of Onatea, and the
despair of all men for miles around.
Harrington represented the commercial enter-
prise of that age—a typical trader, who sailed a
small schooner chartered in Sydney.
The taniwha was a terrible creature that
haunted the estuary of Onatea. He was the recog-
nised local demon, and possessed a vested interest
in the place, over which he had received sovereign
rights from the powers of darkness. His mana
extended along the coast and a great way inland.
Within the memory of living man, he had
accounted for the deaths of at least six persons,
and was, consequently, reputed to delight in
slaughter. His usual haunt was the river’s mouth,
where he swam and sported ill the form of a fish-
like monster, or, with his head just above the
water, he would lie on a sandbank, waiting to
catch any canoe that might pass over the shallows.
Also, he had the power of changing his shape, and,
in the form of a huge pig or of a gigantic dog,
would seize his victims on shore. Altogether he
was a devil of a beast —or a beast of a devil!
But all the diabolism in Maoriland couldn’t rob
Onatea of its romance, dr abolish its feasts and
powhiri, its courtships and marriages. In spite of
the taniwha, Hone Tui contrived to fall in love
with Miromiro, and, though the taniwha, out in
the tideway, might roar all night, Miromiro, with
her luxuriant hair and reddest of lips, remained
the most luscious of maidens.
Hone Tui had demanded her in return for
occult services rendered, and her father decided
that she should marry the hunchback tohunga,
from whom he had derived the greatest good for-
tune —Hone’s spells were of wonderful efficacy,
and his incantations were as far-famed as the
taniwha.
* * • *
In the tideway, a stone’s-throw from the beach,
upon which were drawn the canoes of Onatea,
Harrington’s schooner Jay at anchor. Her skipper
paced the soft sand with Miromiro, half-a-mile
from the kainga. He held her hand,.and Miro-
miro was crying—she had just told him of her
impending fate. The feast was ready ; the , n . ew
house was built for her ; that night she would be-
come the wife of the tohunga, and ever afterwards
cook his kumera. .
Harrington was exasperated. He had tried to
get the girl himself, and, to that end, made large
presents to the chief —a tupara (double-barrelled
gun) for the great man himself, a dozen muskets
for his men, and ammunition in abundance and
he had been supplanted by a miserably deformed
tohunga. But that is the way with parsons all
the world over—they get the pick of the women.
And Miromiro, besides being fond of the pakelia,
heartily loathed the tohunga. Marrying Hone
Tui was next thing to marrying the taniwha him-
self. , , .. ...
They sat down on the sand for a time, and dis-
consolately watched the incoming tide. J-hmi
Harrington got up. “ Come, Miro,. he said,
“ let’s walk on.” The girl rose submissively; put
her hand in his again, and walked along by his
side ; but they had not gone far when, suddenly,
Miromiro screamed, and stood still. ‘ Stop, she
cried, “ don’t go any further ! ”
“ What’s the matter, child ? ” said Harrington,
firmly holding the girl, as she struggled frantically
to run away. “ Frightened I shall hurt you, and
spoil the tohunga’s wedding ? ” .
“Look! Look!!” she cried, pointing far
ahead, where something lay like a log, half in the
water, half on land. “The taniwha!!! Come
back, Haritone.”
“ Why, Miro, that’s only a log washed up by
the tide.” , , .
“ No, no, it’s the taniwha! We must go back,
or we’ll be eaten alive.”
“What rot!” said Harrington. But he went
back all the same. And, as they returned, he
questioned Miromiro about this wonderful tani-
wha, and she told him everything. ,
“ I see,” said Harrington, when the girl had
finished. “Comes ashore sometimes, does he?
What if he came to-night, Miro, and seized you
in vour hut ? That would be an eye-opener for
the tohunga.” And he laughed.
He had spoken in English, and Miromiro mar-
velled at his merriment. Harrington next asked
for all the details of the coming wedding, and the
girl told him all that would occur in the kainga
till the time that Hone Tui should enter her hut.
They were now opposite the schooner, lhe
time had come for them to part. “ Well, good-
•bye, Miromiro,” said the pakeha. ‘ I shall be
sailing by the morning tide, and may not see you
°Harrington kissed the girl, and with an effort
she tore herself from him, and went weeping
towards the village. Harrington whistled for a
boat from the schooner.
The night was moonless but starlit. The kainga
Was hushed and still. Nothing could be heard
but the voice of Horle Tui, as he stood in the
middle of the village repeating incantations-all
the kain'ja knew he was working up the strongest
spells agfiinst all -imaginable' sorts' of taipo
that might assail his married life ; mis-
chievous taipo , that might undermine his hap-,
piness and filch away his mana. At length the
chief, who stood by the tohunga’s side, became
impatient, and said, “ Make a finish to these
karakai, Hone. The girl is expecting you. Do
you not see she has kept a light burning for you
all this while? You should not keep her waiting
so king.” „
“ Only two things now remain for me to do,
answered Hone. “ hirst, X must lay the taipo of
Te Kanawa ...”
“ Ay,” said the chief, “ Kanawa is very jealous
of your mana as a tohunga, Say the karakai to
defeat Kanawa.”
“ And then I must break the power of the
taniwha said Hone. “To do that, I must walk
three times round the kainga, repeating the special
karakai I have made for the occasion. Then I
shall go to my wife.” .
For a few minutes he stood, repeating the in-
cantations that were to confound his rival in
sorcery, and then he set off to charm the village
against the power of the taniwha.
The chief stood where he was. He would wait
till the rites were finished and he had seen Hone
Tui enter Miromiro’s hut —he could then sleep
contented. He heard the tohunga’s voice rising
and falling, as the decrepit old sorcerer paced
around the village. Hone had completed the cir-
cuit twice, when suddenly his incantation ceased.
A monstrous black form was moving towards the
kainga. The chief saw it. The tohunga saw it,
and had stopped his karakai in horror and amaze-
ment. He jran into the village, and screened him-
self behind the chief. .
“It is the taniwha!l” he cried. ‘ He has
heard my karakai and has come to stop me before
they are finished. Urrrrgh!” _
“Your karakai are strong,” said the chief.
“ Say the proper one to stop him coming into the
kainga His faith in the tohunga’s magic was
firm.
Hone could not own that he was unequal to the
occasion. He prayed vociferously and with all
his might. But the taniwha advanced, unimpeded.
The two men hid themselves under the lee of a
hut, and Hone Tui fired off another incantation.
But still the taniwha approached—a monstrous
beast, with an enormous head and many legs, which
moved slowly but surely onwards.
Hone Tui fell flat on his face and grovelled
abjectly in the dirt, and the chief was paralysed
with fear. But the taniwha took no notice of
them. It paused a moment, then cumbersomely
turned itself round, and made for the hut where
the light was burning—Miromiro’s hut—burst in
the door and thrust half its body inside. Miro-
miro screamed franctically, and the. light went
out. Then the taniwha emerged with the poor
girl between its jaws, and lumbered awkwardly
down to the beach.
The tohunga’s wedding was “ off.”
• « • •
Next day on board Harrington’s schooner, as
she bowled along before a stiff breeze, Miromiro
sat with her back against the bulwarks basking in
the sun, whilst Harrington stood at the helm and
laughed with her. The vast tarpaulin hide of the
taniwha lay blistering on the deck. Miromiro
kicked it with her bare foot, and said, “Ha!
good old taniwha ; he saved me from thetohunga!”
And the taniwha’s “legs” stood forrard and
grinned broad nautical grins.
M.L.
ALFRED A. GRACE.


Millions Use Bile Beans
“I Suffered for Many Years.”
DYSPEPSIA AND CONSTIPATION CURED.
An interesting story is told by Mr. William Attwood, of
Rose-street, Glebe, Newcastle, who recounts his experience
as follows :—“ I suffered for many years from dyspepsia and
constipation of the severest description, and I could get no
relief until I chanced to come across Bile Beans for Bilious-
ness. I was troubled greatly with a feeling of fullness after
eating, no matter how little I might eat. Then between my
shoulders I had a terrific pain. I had an almost continual
pain across the bowels; so bad at times was this
that I could not bend to pull on my boots. Want
of breath was a serious trouble at all times. Some
people thought I had asthma. Then I was a martyr
to constipation. At work or off work I always had the
same languid feeling, with the pains either in my back or
across the bottom of my stomach. I saw several doctors ;
one said I had ‘ water-brush,’ and others put it all
down to dyspepsia and indigestion, and spoke about
asthma. They gave me plenty of medicine, but I never felt
any real practical benefit from anything till I tried the Bile
Beans. I read of them in the paper; and a neighbor gave
me two of the Beans to try. I felt some improvement from
these, and then I put myself under a course. I used them
for a number of weeks, and now I feel as lively as ever I
did, and can get about my work with comfort. The pains
and aches I never feel now, and my brain is clear and my
eyesight practically as good as ever it was. No tongue can
tell,” went on Mr. Atwood, “how grateful I feel to the
makers of these Beans.”
COSTIVENESS.
The most effectual way of overcoming a costive habit is
to take at bed-time half to three Bile Beans fob Bilious-
ness, or enough to operate once, and not more than twice,
the next morning. Pursue this course for eight or ten days,
and then omit taking the Beans for a single night, but,
nevertheless, the next morning make an effort to be regular.
If successful, so again the next morning, and ever after
regularly at the same time of day. It must not be put off
for a single moment beyond the usual time, as the old habit
will return again. If no satisfactory results follow, neither
the first or second morning after omitting the Beans, then
return to the use of them again for two or three days, and
then omit again as before, until regular daily habits
are established.

organs. It is accompanied by nausea, from which it derives
its name, and is generally more violent in the front part of
the head. The appetite is impaired, and there is a bad
taste in the mouth. There is a general want of energy, and
the sufferer is irritable and restless. These headaches are
caused by gases arising from the digestive organs to the
head, crowding the nerves of the brain, and causing at
times sharp pains behind the eyeballs. Bile Beans fok
Biliousness will be found an excellent remedy, as they put

to carry off the waste matter of the body.

The Man of Waukaroo.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Well, we’ll put the blessed peg in, and we'll start
out on the road,
For we’ve torn our two-years’ cheques up, and
we’ve got a liver’s load,
An’ we wakened up this mornin’ with a big an’
achin’ ’ead,
For the whisky won the wrastle, an’ we wish that
we was dead.
But we 11 never whip the cat—it was worth the lot
that night
When Sands of Blazin’ Glory fought the man from
Sandy Blight.
We’ll never whip the cat, old mate—we’re one,
are me an’ you—
And we’ll dare the flies and pad it on the road to
Waukaroo.
Out to good old Waukaroo,
Where we’ll find a mate that’s true.
It ain’t a silver city, it’s a busted tinfield town,
But he lifts the man that’s down
In that busted, blackjack town—
There’s a weloome at the end of all the roads to
Waukaroo.
An’ you go up to him shy-like, for. you owe three
quid for grub,
An’ you’ve strapped the blessed township from
the bakehouse to the pub.,
An’ damn the word he says when you come
crawlin’ back to him ;
Oli, he’s got a baby’s bowels, though his eyes are
old and dim.
An’ he don’t kick up a shindy when you elsewhere
blewed your stuff—
Though he keeps a blessed shanty, an’ ’e’s with a
crowd that’s rough—
But he lets yer start to owe ’im when ’e sees yer
want a few,
Though yer did yer greed in otherwise than out at
Waukaroo.
An’ it ain’t because that ’e’s the man to stand a
boozer’s bounce
That he gives us rum an’ grub on strap, an’ whis-
key by the ounce;
No, ’e loves us, that is why he stalls the jim-jams
from us two
By a tot av rum in season from the man of Wau-
karoo.
Why, Big Tassy held the shanty with a neddy
and a knife,
An’, when Matey got at ’im, Big Tassy pleaded
for his life !
Which, uv course, ’e got—but damaged from a
beauty point of view—
For ’is ugly jaw was broken by the Man of
Waukaroo !
For here or there it’s few
That can hit like Waukaroo—
Oh ! ’is arm’s a blessed bunch of beaks—’is fist’s a
steel-bound law !
An* there ain’t a fault or flaw
In a judgment on the jaw
That’s delivered at the end of all the roads to
Waukaroo !
We ain’t much chance ter serve him—we will
never own a pub.
Where we’ll fill him now and ever with our
whisky and our grub;
We mine the ore, and coin it, and the bits go in a
spree,
And we’ll never keep a thickun till our blessed
souls are free.
But when within the Final Stope we’re breaking
diorite,
With the Man of Blazing Glory an’ the Man of
Sandy Blight,
An’ a cockeyed-devil shift-boss says, “My thirsty
mates, a brew ? ”
We’ll remark together, “ Give it to the Man of
Waukaroo !
For on earth or here there’s few
Mr. Nick, like Waukaroo;
He’s saved our lives a many a time—he’s a mate
that’s staunch and true ;
He’s too good for me an’ you—
And the proper thing to do,
Mr. Nick, ’s to send him Heavenwards—he had
hell at Waukaroo.”
Q.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
Tranfc Chambers mentions, in his Wes-
tralian “ Land of Promise,” that the biggest price
known to have been paid for a pearl from the nor’-
west fisheries is .£l5OO. But this, according to
the best accounts, was not the price Streeter first
sold the gem for. It was originally disposed of
for .£9OO (£2O a grain), but was afterwards bought
back by Streeter himself, who wanted it for a
special purpose, for the figure Chambers mentions.
The ’Murkan who now owns it no doubt paid still
more.
The cabled statement that Louis de Rouge-
mont—whoever he may really be—has furnished
particulars of an alleged meeting with Alfred
Gibson, Giles’ fellow-traveller in the Australian
desert, should increase the feeling of doubt as to
Rougemont’s bona-fides. Giles’ expedition con-
sisted of W. H. Tietkins, Alfred Gibson, James
Andrews and Giles himself. They left the tele-
graph line in Aug. 73, and six months later, when
Giles and Gibson were 90 miles from water and in
one of the arid wastes of Australia, Gibson’s horse
gave out. Giles remained while Gibson pushed on
to the main camp on the other horse for water r
presently Giles followed and, after a terrible
journey, managed to reach camp at Port McKellar
only to find that Gibson had not arrived. All
attempts to discover any trace of Gibson were
useless, and his death remains a mystery. Rouge-
mont now announces, however, that Gibson died
amongst a tribe of North Australian blacks, but
how he managed to cross the impassable desert
intervening will take a lot of explanation. Giles,
in his journal, surmised that Gibson had mistaken
certain dry ridges for the Rawlinson Range, which
he should have made for. The possibility of
Gibson’s survival never entered Giles’ mind, con-
sidering that the mare he rode was nearly dead-
beat before he started.

is to man the most interesting fact of existence.”
Smollett: “ Facts are stubborn things.”
The surgical treatment proposed by Bailes,
member for Bendigo (Vic.), for certain sexual
offences, is commonly thought to have been an
Oriental punishment only. But a search through
history shows that it was quite in vogue in England
during the good old times. For instance, it is found
in the laws of King Alfred, and in the alleged
“ laws of William the Conqueror ” allusion is
made to it in the last section.

“Aggie.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Aggie came to us vice her eldest sister, “ Emmer ”
Rose, invalided. There are altogether eleven
Rosebuds, and, with the exception of “me
brother,” who lives in Sydney and owns a racing
pony, the whole family slam our back-gate and
tramp down the right-of-way in continual and
profitable visits to the kitchen, bewildering me by
exhibiting themselves as caricatures of all my
juvenile relatives, whose hand-me-downs clothe
the children. The Rosebuds are sturdy and
healthy, with ever-clamorous appetites. Some-
times I think that Mrs. Rose has done her duty
nobly by her adopted country, and sometimes I—
don’t.
Aggie is of nondescript age, “standing with
reluctant feet where the brook and river meet.”
Her feet are literally reluctant; she has an honest
and quite frankly expressed contempt for life.
She is so very tired that existence is too much of
a problem for her, and she would like to give it up.
“Do you think life’s worth living?” Aggie
asked abruptly, one day, when she was watching
our cook. In arguing, she employs the Socratic
method, and fires off her questions point-blank.
“Don’t you '/” I returned, fencing the question.
‘‘No. What’s the good of it ? Too much graft
for me. I’d as soon die as live, any day.”
“How was Emma last night?” (I am con-
scious of being weakly evasive.)
“ Better. Doc. says it’s eneemia. Father says
it’s graft that’s made her bad; but it’s not : it’s
dancing.. She goes to a black-dress-and-flower
party twice a week. I wish Em. was always ill
when father’s out of a job.”
“Why?”
“ ’Cos father’s always cross when he’s at home ;
but he’s all polish when Em.’s there. He thinks
the world of Em. Don’t you think Em’s awfully
stylish ? ” J
“Father” is an “art-decorator,” and perhaps
has an eye for the ornamental, though Emmer, in
a black flop hat with pink calico roses, sixpenny
diamond side-combs in her touzled locks, and dyed
rabbit edging her cape, might seem a trifle gaudy
even in a fond father’s eye. Aggie is a humble
understudy of her sister.
Mrs. James, green-groceress in a small way in
Main-street, is Aggie’s particular friend. After a
visit to Mrs. James one night, she knocked for
admission at the locked door at 11.30 p.m.
“ I’ve been with Mrs. James buying bananas
from the Chows in Little Bourke-street,” she ex-
plained. Aggie’s frank truthfulness would be a
luminously beautiful trait if it weren’t quite so
cynical. “ I said I’d ‘ rnally ’ the Chow if he took
tuppence a dozen off, and he did,” with a giggle
of reminiscence. (Mental note by me :to see
Mrs. Rose about putting a stop to the James
friendship).
Next day Aggie stopped me in the passage to
ask : “ Would you marry a Chow ? ”
“ Marry a Choivl Certainly not. No decent
girl,” &c., &c., &c.
“ Well, 1 would. Chows make jolly good hus-
bands. I’d as soon marry a Chow as a white man.
They keep yer in luxury and yer don’t have to
graft.”
The penalty of Adam weighs too heavily on
Aggie and hundreds like her. That is how the
Chinaman scores.
CLEO.
“Pick’s Ole Man.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dick explained his filial impiety to me as follows ,:
“Yersee, me an’ me brother Tom ’ad a con-
track ter cart the Queen Cumpney’s quartz to the
batt ry an’ empty the san’-pits. We wuz maltin’
good money, an’ we ’ad two orlrite teams. One
night we lost the run uv four uv the best ’orses we
ad obbles, bells, an’ all. My oath, they wuz
’orses—cost us over fifty quid in the yard ; an’ we
never seen them again frum that day to this,
altho’ we offered ten notes reward for ’em !
“Well, me an’ Tom wuz livin’ with the ole man
at this time, an’ uv course we tells ’im erbaht the
’orses bein’ shook. ’E reckoned it sarved us dam
well right fer not ’avin’ no proper brand uv our
own. Sez ’e : ‘ Yer fools, don’t ye see we otter ’av
our own brand, an’ then the ’orses wud be darnd-
easy tracked ef they wuz shook.’ So the ole man
selecks a brand, an’ regestiz it in ’is own name—
me an’ Tom not bein’ fly fur ’im, an’ not suspectin’
nothin’ cronk. Soon as ’e’d got the brand ’e gets
us ter sign a lawyer’s paper, givin’ ’im permission
ter brand our ’orses with it—becoz ’e reckoned
it wud be expensive ter get seprit brans.
’E dries our names, an’ puts the paper
away in ’is pocket. Then we goes out
an’ brans all our ’orses—saddle-’orses an’ all. Soon
as we’d finished that, the ole man sez. ‘Now,
boys, yer can just go and dance jigs to a inilestun’’
—them ’orses is mine now, yer can ’ave the use uv
’em ef yer tucker me an’ gimme a quid a week,
but ef I ketch yer tryin’ ter sell one uv ’em I’ll
gaol yer fur ’orsestealin’, shoorazelh’ Well, we
calls ’im all the names we cud think uv, but ’is
trubbles—’e only larfed. ’E went to Coolgardie
larst summer, an’ afore ’e went ’e offered ter sell
us our ’orses back fur ninety-five quid—damcheep
’e reckoned they wuz. We thought ’e wuz only
bluffin’ us, an’ told’im ter go ter ’ell. That narked
’im, an’ ’e puts the lot up fur auction an’ clears
out with all the stuff. ’E wrote to us the other
day arslcin’ us ter back ’im into a claim—reckoned
’e wuz goin’ter make our fortune—but we hain’t
takin’ no fortunes just now.”
K. C. WYE.

FANCY
often plays such pranks with some of us. There are excellent
people who fancy the earth is flat, and who decline to accept
evidence to the contrary. Others fancy they can run this
journal better than any able editor of ancient or modern times.
Some folks fancy they are so strong they can never fall ill.
Others fancy they are so ill they can never get well. The
latter class is, in large part, composed of sufferers from pro-
longed nervous dyspepsia, with all its insidious, ever-increasing
troubles. Such people are weighed down by a sense of hope-
lessness. They are the victims of lassitude and headache—
aye, and of heartache, too, for they can enjoy little, and envy
the buoyancy of their more robust fellows. In short, the v
endure a life of misery,
